Affirmative vs. Negative: Which Existences Are Required
The passage explains the different existential requirements for affirmative and negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an affirmative or negative proposition requires the second mental existence and explain why.

Core Idea
Affirmatives need both mental existences; negatives only need the first.

Building on the distinction between the two mental existences, this lesson applies it to the two types of categorical propositions: affirmative and negative. The passage explicitly states that the negative proposition shares with the affirmative in requiring the first existence but not the second. We will explore the logical reason behind this asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two existences from the previous lesson
The first is required for judgment, the second for predicate attribution.
2
Contrast the requirements of affirmative vs. negative
Read the text: 'The negative shares with the affirmative in requiring the first existence but not the second.' Why? Because negation does not posit the predicate as actually belonging to the subject, so the subject need not exist for the predicate to be denied of it.
3
Test with an Islamic example: 'Every sin is forgivable' vs. 'No sin is unforgivable'
The affirmative requires both: the concept of sin at judgment and the actual possibility of forgiveness (external existence of sins). The negative only requires the concept of sin.
4
Note the exception for mental propositions
The text says that when propositions are considered mentally (dhihniyya), even affirmatives only require the first existence, because the subject has only mental existence.
This exception is further discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك الوجودُ الذهني الذي يقتضيه الحكم مغاير للوجودِ الذهني الذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فإنَّ الوجودَ الثَّانِيَ إِنَّما يُعتبرُ بحسَبِ ثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع، إن دائماً فدائماً، وإن خارجاً فخارجاً، وإن ذهناً فذهناً، وإن لحظةً فَلحظة، والسَّالبة تشارك الموجبة في اقتضاء الوجودِ الأَوَّلِ دونَ الثَّاني، وكذا الحال في الفرق بين الموجبة والسالبة إذا أخذَتْ ذهنيَّة .ا.هـ. وقيَّد المصنِّفُ في شرح الرّسالة اقتضاء الموجبة وجود الموضوع بما إذا كانَتْ خارجيَّةً أو حقيقيَّةً، وأمَّا الذّهنيّةُ، فَلَا تقتضي إِلَّا تصور الموضوع حال الحكم كَمَا في السَّوالِبِ مِن غير فرق، ولا تفتقر إلى وجودِ الموضوع حال ثبوتِ الحكم، بل لا يصح وجوده في تلك الحالة، والقولُ بأنَّها سوالب في المعنى ممنوع؛ إذ الحكمُ إِنَّما هو بوقوع النّسبة. ا.هـ. أي: والإرجاع إلى السَّلبِ تَعشُفٌ، وردَّهُ عبد الحكيم بأنَّه يهدمُ المقدِّمة البديهيَّةَ الَّتي يُبتنى عليها كثيرٌ مِنَ المسائلِ مِن أنَّ ثبوت شيءٍ لشيء؛ فرغ ثبوتِ المثبتِ له؛ إذ التخصيص لا يجري في القواعد العقليَّةِ. (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ دَائِمَاً) أي: إنْ كانَ التَّبوتُ المقتضي دائماً؛ فالوجود المتقضي يكون دائماً، وهكذا . وإيضاح الفرق بين الوجودَيْن يظهرُ فيما إذا قُلْنَا : الله تعالى موجودٌ أزلاً وأبداً، موجوده في الذهن لأجلِ الحُكْمِ؛ إنَّما هو حال الإيقاع، وَوُجودُهُ لأجل ثبوتِ المحمول لهُ؛ أزليّ أبديّ. وإذا قيلَ : البرقُ لامع ؛ فَوجود البرقِ في الذهن لأجل الحكم؛ إنَّما حال الحُكْمِ، ووجوده في الخارج لأجل ثبوتِ اللّمعانِ لَهُ فِي لَحظة؛ لَأَنَّ اللَّمَعَانَ إِنَّمَا يثبت للبرق لحظةً. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنْ ذِهْنَاً؛ فَذِهْنَاً أي : كَمَا في قولِكَ : شريك الباري معدوم، فَثبوتُ العدم لِلشَّريكِ ذهني، كما أنَّ وجوده ذهني. (قَوْلُهُ : فِى اقْتِضَائِهِ) أي : في اقتضاء كل منهما إيَّاه. (قَوْلُهُ: يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الْوُجُوْدِ الثَّانِي) أي: وهو الوجود الذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فَلَا تصدقُ الموجبة إلا إذا كان موضوعُهَا مَوجوداً؛ لأنَّ ثبوت شَيءٍ لِشَيءٍ؛ يقتضي ثبوت الشَّيء المثبت له. (قَوْلُهُ : بِخِلَافِ السَّالِبَةِ) أي : فإِنَّهُ لا يتوقَّفُ صدقها على الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ سَلْبِ المحمول؛ لأنَّ سَلْبَ المحمول عَنِ الموضوع لا يقتضي وجوده؛ بخلاف ثبوتِه له . ومِنْ هذا قِيلَ : إِنَّ السَّالبة تصدقُ مع نفي الموضوع، والموجبة لا تصدقُ إلا مَعَ وجودِ الموضوع. (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ) أي : في هذا المقامِ لِدِقتِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ) أي : حتَّى يظهر لك الفرقُ بين الموجبة والسالبة عند من يرى اطّراد الحكم في سائر الموجبات؛ كَمَا هو المنقولُ عَنِ السَّيِّدِ وغيرِهِ، أَوْ أَنَّ هذا الحكم الواقع
That mental existence which the judgment requires is different from the mental existence which the predicate's attribution to the subject requires. For the second existence is only considered in accordance with the predicate's attribution to the subject: if permanent then permanent, if external then external, if mental then mental, if momentary then momentary. The negative shares with the affirmative in requiring the first existence but not the second. The same is the case in the difference between the affirmative and the negative when considered mentally. End quote. The author in the commentary on the Treatise restricted the affirmative's requirement of the subject's existence to when it is external or real; as for the mental, it requires only the conception of the subject at the time of judgment as in negatives without difference, and it does not need the existence of the subject at the time of the attribute's affirmation; rather its existence in that state is not correct, and the statement that they are negatives in meaning is prohibited because the judgment is only about the occurrence of the relation. End quote. That is: referring it to negation is arbitrary. 'Abd al-Hakim refuted it by saying that it demolishes the self-evident premise on which many issues are built, that the affirmation of something to something requires the affirmation of that to which it is affirmed, for specification does not apply in rational principles. (His saying: 'If permanent') i.e., if the required attribution is permanent, then the required existence is permanent, and so on. And the clarification of the difference between the two existences appears when we say: God is existent eternally and perpetually; His existence in the mind for the sake of judgment is only at the moment of assertion, and His existence for the sake of the attribute's affirmation to Him is eternal and perpetual. And when it is said: lightning flashes; the existence of lightning in the mind for the sake of judgment is only at the moment of judgment, and its existence externally for the sake of the flashing affirmation to it is at a moment, because the flashing is only affirmed to lightning at a moment. (His saying: 'And if mentally, then mentally') i.e., as in your statement: The partner of God is non-existent; so the affirmation of non-existence to the partner is mental, just as its existence is mental. (His saying: 'In its requirement') i.e., in each of them requiring it. (His saying: 'Is dependent on the second existence') i.e., it is the existence that the predicate's attribution to the subject requires. Thus the affirmative is not true unless its subject exists, because the affirmation of something to something requires the existence of that to which it is affirmed. (His saying: 'Unlike the negative') i.e., its truth does not depend on the existence that the denial of the predicate requires, because denying the predicate from the subject does not require its existence, unlike affirming it. Hence it is said: The negative is true with the subject's non-existence, while the affirmative is true only with the subject's existence. (His saying: 'Reflect') i.e., on this matter due to its subtlety. (His saying: 'Reflect') i.e., so that the difference between the affirmative and negative becomes clear to you, according to one who considers the rule to apply to all affirmatives, as is transmitted from al-Sayyid and others, or that this actual ruling...
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a prior matn statement about the distinction between two types of mental existence required in propositions. The commentators (al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, with a mention of 'Abd al-Hakim) explain that a judgment (hukm) requires a mental existence of the subject at the moment of judgment (al-awwal), while the predicate's attribution to the subject requires a separate mental existence (al-thani) that corresponds to the temporal or modal status of the attribution. The affirmative proposition requires both; the negative only requires the first. The author then discusses an exception for mental propositions, where the affirmative also only requires conception, not actual existence of the subject at the time of the attributive relation. He criticizes those who would reduce such affirmatives to negatives, calling it arbitrary (ta'assuf). 'Abd al-Hakim defends the standard view by invoking the self-evident principle that 'affirmation of something to something requires the existence of that to which it is affirmed.' The passage then provides examples: God exists eternally (affirmative with eternal attribute) and lightning flashes (affirmative with momentary attribute). The second existence for God is eternal; for lightning it is momentary. The third example, 'The partner of God is non-existent,' illustrates a mental affirmative (a shirk denial) where both existence and attribution are mental. The commentary emphasizes that the negative proposition does not require the subject's external existence, unlike the affirmative. The final instruction 'Reflect' (ta'ammal) warns the reader of the subtlety. This analysis uses kalam examples (God's existence, refutation of shirk) and a natural phenomenon (lightning) to ground abstract logical distinctions.

Key Terms
الموجبة
السالبة
الوجود الأول
الوجود الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the negative proposition not require the second mental existence?
A) Because negation does not imply the subject's existence
B) Because negatives are always false
C) Because the second existence is only for external propositions
D) Because negatives only require mental existence

Key Takeaway
Negation frees you from having to verify the subject's existence; affirmation binds you to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Given the proposition 'All prophets are infallible' (affirmative), explain which mental existences are required and why.
?
2. Given the proposition 'No prophet is a deity' (negative), explain which mental existences are required and why.

Propositions
advanced
8 min
The Controversy over Mental Propositions: Are They Negatives?
The passage refutes the reduction of mental affirmatives to negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument against reducing mental affirmatives to negatives and evaluate 'Abd al-Hakim's refutation.

Core Idea
Mental affirmatives are not disguised negatives; the self-evident principle prevents this reduction.

Some later logicians argued that mental propositions (where the subject exists only mentally) are effectively negatives because the subject lacks external existence. This lesson examines the rebuttal by 'Abd al-Hakim, who invokes a foundational principle: affirming something to something requires that to which it is affirmed to exist in some way. Reducing such affirmatives to negatives would undermine countless proofs built on this principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductionist claim
Some claim that mental affirmatives are negatives in meaning (sawalib fi al-ma'na) because the subject does not exist externally.
2
Read the text's rejection
The passage says 'The statement that they are negatives in meaning is prohibited (mamnu') because the judgment is only about the occurrence of the relation.'
The judgment in a mental affirmative is about the relation, not about external existence.
3
Examine 'Abd al-Hakim's refutation
He argues that this reduction destroys the self-evident premise 'thubut shay' li shay' yafridu thubut al-muthbat lahu' (the affirmation of something to something requires the existence of that to which it is affirmed). This premise is used in many proofs.
If the reduction were accepted, this principle would be violated, wrecking many theological and logical arguments.
4
Evaluate the strength of the refutation
The reductionist would have to deny this self-evident premise, which is unacceptable. Hence, the reduction is invalid.
The refutation shows that the reduction leads to an absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a prior matn statement about the distinction between two types of mental existence required in propositions. The commentators (al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, with a mention of 'Abd al-Hakim) explain that a judgment (hukm) requires a mental existence of the subject at the moment of judgment (al-awwal), while the predicate's attribution to the subject requires a separate mental existence (al-thani) that corresponds to the temporal or modal status of the attribution. The affirmative proposition requires both; the negative only requires the first. The author then discusses an exception for mental propositions, where the affirmative also only requires conception, not actual existence of the subject at the time of the attributive relation. He criticizes those who would reduce such affirmatives to negatives, calling it arbitrary (ta'assuf). 'Abd al-Hakim defends the standard view by invoking the self-evident principle that 'affirmation of something to something requires the existence of that to which it is affirmed.' The passage then provides examples: God exists eternally (affirmative with eternal attribute) and lightning flashes (affirmative with momentary attribute). The second existence for God is eternal; for lightning it is momentary. The third example, 'The partner of God is non-existent,' illustrates a mental affirmative (a shirk denial) where both existence and attribution are mental. The commentary emphasizes that the negative proposition does not require the subject's external existence, unlike the affirmative. The final instruction 'Reflect' (ta'ammal) warns the reader of the subtlety. This analysis uses kalam examples (God's existence, refutation of shirk) and a natural phenomenon (lightning) to ground abstract logical distinctions.

Checkpoint
What is the self-evident principle that 'Abd al-Hakim invokes to refute the reduction?
A) A thing cannot be both affirmed and denied
B) Thubut shay' li shay' yafridu thubut al-muthbat lahu
C) The negative is true even with no subject

Key Takeaway

The Modified Proposition (al-Macedula): Definition and Key Distinctions
Author defines the modified proposition and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a modified proposition and distinguish it from a simple negative proposition.

Core Idea
A modified proposition integrates the negation into the term, not the judgment.

In this passage, the author explains one of the divisions of categorical propositions: the modified proposition (al-macedula). Unlike a simple negative proposition which negates the predicate, the modified proposition embeds the negation particle into the subject or predicate itself, altering the term's meaning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate logical analysis, especially when evaluating definitions and arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle
Look for particles such as 'la', 'ghayr', or 'laysa' that are part of the subject or predicate term, not separate.
The passage lists these as examples.
2
Locate where the particle is placed
The passage says the particle is made 'a part of a part' (juz' min juz'), i.e., part of the subject or predicate.
Check if the particle is integrated into the term, not standing alone.
3
Classify the proposition as modified
If the negation is internal to the term (e.g., 'ghayr katib' as predicate), it is modified. If the negation is external (e.g., 'Zayd laysa bi katib'), it is simple negative.
The commentary discusses that modification can occur in both categorical and conditional propositions.
4
Determine the positivity or negativity
Even if the term is negative, the whole proposition can be positive (affirming a negative term) or negative (denying a negative term). The passage states: 'modified, positive or negative'.
This is a key subtlety: the formal quality (positive/negative) is about the copula, not the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الحملية إلى معدولة ومحصلة وبسيطة ( وَقَدْ يُجْعَلُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ) كلفظةِ : «لا»، و«غير»، و«ليس» (جُزْءاً مِنْ جُزْءٍ)؛ أي: من جزء القضيَّةِ كالموضوع والمحمول؛ (فَيْسَمَّى) جزء القضيَّةِ الَّذي جُعِلَ حرفُ السَّلبِ جزءاً منه (مَعْدُولاً)، والقضيَّةُ معدولةٌ موجبة، أو سالبة. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: مِنْ جُزْأَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ هَكَذا في بعض النسخ بالتثنية، وهي ظاهرة، وفي بعضها : (أي : مِنْ جُزْءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ) بالإفرادِ، وعليها ؛ فَجزء مفردٌ مُضافٌ يعم الجزأين . وبعبارة قوله : (من جزء (القضيَّةِ ؛ أي من جزء من جزأي القضيَّة، وقولُه : (كالموضوع ... إلخ) ربَّما أوهم هذا أنَّ العدول خاص بالحمليَّاتِ، معَ أَنَّهُ يكونُ في الشَّرطيَّاتِ، فَكانَ عليهِ أنْ يزيد المقدَّمَ والتّالي؛ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ: الكافُ للتَّمثيل، فيدخل ذلك لا أنَّها استقصائية. (قَوْلُهُ : مُوْجِبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ) أي : وهي موجبة أو سالبةٌ . العطار فقالَ : إِنَّ زيداً أعمى مُحصَّلةٌ، وفي شرح المطالعِ أَنَّها مَعدولةٌ، وأنَّ مدار العدولِ على اعتبار العدم في المفهوم، وأورد العصام اللاجمادَ إِذا سُمِّيَ بِهِ شخص حيواني، وقُلْنَا : اللاجمادُ حيوان وزيدٌ أعمى ؛ فَإِنَّ الأُوْلَى : مُحصَّلةٌ مع دخولها في التّعريفِ، والثَّانية : معدولةٌ معَ خروجِهَا .. هـ . والجواب : أنَّ القضيَّةَ الأَوْلَى معدولةٌ ا.هـ مِنْ حيثُ اللَّفْظُ ؛ مُحصَّلةٌ مِن حيث المعنى، والثَّانيةُ : بالعكس؛ بناءً على أنَّهُ لا بُدَّ في العدولِ مِنَ التَّصريح بحرفِ السَّلْبِ. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعْدُولَاً) لأَنَّهُ عدلَ بِهِ عَن موضوعِهِ الأصليّ، وهو سلب الحكم، فتوصفُ القضيَّةُ بالمعدولةِ وصفاً للشَّيء بحال جزئهِ وهو حرفُ السَّلْبِ، وفيه إشارةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ أَصلَ
Division of the categorical into modified, simple, and simple. (And the negation particle,) such as 'la', 'ghayr', and 'laysa', may be made (a part of a part); that is, of a part of the proposition like the subject and the predicate; then the part of the proposition in which the negation particle is made a part is called (modified), and the proposition is modified, positive or negative. al-Dasuqi: (His saying: that is, from the two parts of the proposition) thus in some copies with the dual, and it is apparent, and in some: (that is, from a part of the proposition) with the singular, and according to it, 'a part' is a singular noun that generalizes over the two parts. In other words, his saying: (from a part of the proposition), i.e., from one of the two parts of the proposition, and his saying: (like the subject, etc.) might give the impression that modification is specific to categoricals, whereas it occurs in conditionals, so he should have added the antecedent and consequent; unless it is said that the 'kaaf' is for exemplification, so that is included, not that it is exhaustive. (His saying: and the predicate) i.e., or the predicate. (His saying: then the part of the proposition is called, etc.) In reality, the modified one is the negation particle, because it is that which is diverted from its position, which is cutting the relation; but when it is diverted in that part by the negation particle from its position, the part is called modified; so it is naming the place after the state in it. Our saying: it was diverted in that part by the negation particle from its position means that the intention with it is the negation of the judgment about the subject. (His saying: positive or negative) i.e., and it is positive or negative. al-Attar said: 'Verily Zayd is blind' is simple, and in Sharh al-Mutali' it is modified, and the criterion for modification is the consideration of non-existence in the concept. And al-Isam objected to 'la-jamad' when a living person is named by it, and we said: 'Al-la-jamad is an animal' and 'Zayd is blind'; the first is simple while entering into the definition, and the second is modified while exiting from it. The answer: the first proposition is modified in terms of wording, simple in terms of meaning, and the second is the opposite, based on the fact that in modification there must be explicit mention of the negation particle. (His saying: modified) because it is diverted from its original position, which is the negation of the judgment; so the proposition is described as modified, describing the thing by the state of its part which is the negation particle, and it contains an indication that the origin is...
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage defines the modified proposition (al-macedula), which is one of the divisions of categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that a negation particle such as 'la', 'ghayr', or 'laysa' may be made a part of another part (i.e., the subject or predicate). This transforms the proposition into a modified one. The modified proposition can be either positive or negative in form. The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies textual variants and notes that modification applies not only to categoricals but also to conditionals, and the example 'ka' is for illustration. Al-Attar then discusses a case: 'Zayd is blind' is considered simple by some but modified by others, depending on whether the negation is explicit in wording. The key point is that the negation particle, when made part of the subject or predicate, diverts from its original role of negating the judgment, and the proposition is named after this condition. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about the origin.

Scope of 'Adl: Does Modification Apply to Conditional Propositions?
Author examines whether the concepts of 'adl and tahsil apply to conditional propositions, resolving the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the concept of modification (al-'adl) applies to a given type of proposition, based on 'Abd al-Hakim's verification.

Core Idea
Modification applies to categoricals, not conditionals, because negation in conditionals affects components, not the whole.

In earlier lessons, you learned about modified (ma'dula) and unmodified (muhassala) propositions in categorical logic. Now you will encounter a debate: do these concepts also apply to conditional propositions? The great scholar 'Abd al-Hakim settled this question definitively. Understanding his reasoning will sharpen your ability to classify propositions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the propositions in the debate.
Examine the examples given: 'Zayd is blind' (categorical) and 'al-la-jamad is an animal' (also categorical). Note that some scholars consider 'Zayd is blind' unmodified, others consider it modified based on whether non-existence is considered in the concept.
The debate centers on the role of the negation particle in the proposition.
2
Understand the criterion for modification.
Modification requires an explicit negation particle (harf salb). The resolution offered is that the first proposition is modified in wording but unmodified in meaning, and the second is the opposite. This shows that the term 'modified' can be ambiguous.
The passage stresses that modification is about deviation from original positive expression.
3
Analyze the extension to conditionals.
Some commentators argued that modification applies to conditionals as well. Note the commentator's restriction to categoricals and the gloss that extends it to conditionals. This sets up 'Abd al-Hakim's verification.
The disagreement shows the need for a precise logical definition.
4
Apply 'Abd al-Hakim's verification.
He argues that if the negation particle is part of the antecedent or consequent, it modifies only those components potentially, not the conditional judgment itself. Therefore, the concept of modification does not apply to conditionals as such.
This is a critical refinement: the scope of 'adl is restricted to categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار فقالَ: إِنَّ زيداً أعمى مُحصَّلةٌ، وفي شرح المطالعِ أَنَّها مَعدولةٌ، وأنَّ مدار العدولِ على اعتبار العدم في المفهوم، وأورد العصام اللاجمادَ إِذا سُمِّيَ بِهِ شخص حيواني، وقُلْنَا : اللاجمادُ حيوان وزيدٌ أعمى ; فَإِنَّ الأُوْلَى : مُحصَّلةٌ مع دخولها في التّعريفِ، والثَّانية : معدولةٌ معَ خروجِهَا .. هـ . والجواب : أنَّ القضيَّةَ الأَوْلَى معدولةٌ ا.هـ مِنْ حيثُ اللَّفْظُ ; مُحصَّلةٌ مِن حيث المعنى، والثَّانيةُ : بالعكس؛ بناءً على أنَّهُ لا بُدَّ في العدولِ مِنَ التَّصريح بحرفِ السَّلْبِ. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعْدُولَاً) لأَنَّهُ عدلَ بِهِ عَن موضوعِهِ الأصليّ، وهو سلب الحكم، فتوصفُ القضيَّةُ بالمعدولةِ وصفاً للشَّيء بحال جزئهِ وهو حرفُ السَّلْبِ، وفيه إشارةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ أَصلَ المعدولةِ ; المعدولةُ بها بناءً على الحذفِ والإيصال والاستتارِ كَمَا في لفظ مُشترك، أو لأنَّ الأصل في التَّعبيرِ عَن الأطرافِ هو الأمورُ الثَّبوتيَّةُ ; لأنَّ الوجود هو السَّابِقُ، والسَّالبُ مُضافٌ إليهِ ، فَفي التَّعبيرِ عَن طَرفَي القضيَّةِ بالسَّلْبِ: عدولٌ عَن الأصل . (قَوْلُهُ : كَقولِنَا : اللَّأحَيُّ جَمَادُ ترك مثالي مَعدولتهما ومحصلتهما ; لظهورهما مِمَّا ذكره من الأمثلة، ثمَّ إِنَّ قضيَّةَ كلامِ الشَّارحِ تَخصيص العدول بالحملية، ويؤيده أنَّ القومَ إِنَّما أوردُوا مباحث العدولِ والتحصيل في الحمليَّاتِ، وفي الحاشيةِ : أَنَّهُ يجري في الشَّرطيَّاتِ ، والذي حقَّقَهُ الفاضلُ عَبد الحكيم : أَنَّهُ لا يجري العدولُ والتحصيلُ في الشَّرطيَّاتِ ; لأنَّ حرفَ السَّلْبِ إِذا كانَ جُزءاً مِنَ المقدَّم أو التَّالي; كانَ العدول في أطرافها باعتبارِ الحُكْم الَّذي فيها بالقوَّةِ لا في الشَّرطيَّةِ ; لأنَّ الحُكْمَ ...
Al-'Attar: He said: Indeed, 'Zayd is blind' is an unmodified (muhassala) proposition, and in Sharh al-Matali' it is said that it is modified, and that the criterion for modification is consideration of non-existence in the concept. And al-'Isam objected: al-jamad (the inanimate) if a person is named by it, and we said: 'al-la-jamad is an animal' and 'Zayd is blind' — the first is unmodified while it enters the definition, and the second is modified while it exits... The answer: The first proposition is modified in terms of wording, unmodified in terms of meaning, and the second is the opposite, based on the necessity of explicit mention of the negation particle for modification. His saying: 'modified' because it was turned away from its original position, which is the negation of the judgment, so the proposition is described as modified as a description of a thing by the state of its part, which is the negation particle. And in it is an indication that the origin of the modified proposition is the modified one based on deletion and connection and concealment, like a shared term, or because the original in expressing the terms is positive matters, because existence is prior, and the negative is appended to it. So in expressing the two terms of the proposition with negation, it is a deviation from the original. His saying: 'like our saying: the non-living is inanimate' — he left out examples of both modified and both unmodified because they are obvious from the examples he mentioned. Then indeed the purport of the commentator's words is the specification of modification to categorical propositions, and it is supported by the fact that the scholars only introduced the topics of modification and unmodification in categoricals. And in the gloss: it applies to conditionals. And what al-Fadil 'Abd al-Hakim verified: that modification and unmodification do not apply to conditionals, because if the negation particle is a part of the antecedent or consequent, then modification is in its components by virtue of the judgment in it potentially, not in the conditional itself, because the judgment...
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage engages in a critical discussion about whether the concepts of 'adl (modification) and tahsil (unmodification) apply to conditional propositions (shartiyyat). Al-'Attar begins with an example: 'Zayd is blind' — some consider it unmodified, others modified based on whether non-existence is considered in the concept. Al-'Isam raises a nuance about naming. The resolution is that the first proposition is modified in wording but unmodified in meaning, and vice versa, requiring explicit negation particle for modification. Then the text notes that the commentator (al-Dasuqi) seems to restrict modification to categoricals, supported by the majority's treatment. However, some glosses extend it to conditionals. Finally, 'Abd al-Hakim's verification is presented: modification does not apply to conditionals because if the negation particle appears in the antecedent or consequent, it modifies only the component judgment potentially, not the conditional judgment itself. The passage thus refines the scope of 'adl, concluding that it is not properly applied to conditional propositions. This is a typical critical refinement: the super-commentator corrects a potential over-extension by earlier commentators.

Key Terms
مُحصَّلة
شَرْطيَّة
معدولة

D) It applies only to positive conditionals

Key Takeaway
Modification (al-'adl) is a property of categorical propositions only; it does not apply to conditionals because negation in conditionals modifies only the parts, not the whole judgment.

Identifying Modified Propositions: Quality and Modification Type
Author enumerates examples of modified propositions with modified subject or predicate in both positive and negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any modified proposition as positive or negative and identify whether the modification is on the subject, predicate, or both.

Core Idea
The negation particle inside a term changes the term's reference, not the proposition's quality.

Modified propositions are those where the subject or predicate term contains a negation particle (like 'non-'). Al-Dasuqi provides concrete examples to show how the same logical form can be positive or negative independent of term modification. This lesson guides you through his examples so you can recognize and classify them in any Islamic logical text.

Guided Path
1
Determine the quality (positive/negative) of the proposition
Look at the copula: if it affirms, the proposition is positive; if it negates, it is negative. The presence of a negation particle inside a term does not change the quality.
For example, 'The non-living is inanimate' is positive (is), while 'No non-living is knowing' is negative.
2
Examine the subject term for a negation particle
Check if the subject term contains a negation prefix like 'غير' or 'لا' (non-, not-). If yes, the subject is modified.
In 'The non-living is inanimate', the subject is 'non-living' – modified subject.
3
Examine the predicate term for a negation particle
Check if the predicate term contains a negation particle. If yes, the predicate is modified.
In 'The inanimate is not knowing', the predicate is 'not knowing' – modified predicate.
4
Combine quality and modification type to classify
Now you have four possible classifications: positive with modified subject, positive with modified predicate, negative with modified subject, negative with modified predicate. Al-Dasuqi gives an example of each.
The passage also provides examples where both terms are modified, for both positive and negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَقَوْلِنَا: «اللَّاحَي جمادٌ»، و«الجمادُ لا عالم»، و«لا شيء من اللَّاحَيّ بعالم»، أو: من العالم بلا حي». الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : اللَّأحَيُّ جَمَادٌ) أي: أنَّ ما يصدقُ عليه أنَّه غيرُ حي؛ يصدقُ عليه أنَّه جمادٌ، وهذا مثال للموجبة معدولة الموضوع. وقوله : (الجمادُ لَا عالم مثال للموجبة معدولة المحمول، وقوله : (لا شيء مِنَ اللَّاحِيّ بعالم؛ مثالٌ لِلسَّالبة معدولة الموضوع. وقولُهُ: (أو مِنَ العالِم بلا حيّ ; مثالٌ لِلسَّالبة معدولة المحمول، وترك مثال معدولتهما في الموجبة والسالبة، ومثال معدولتِهما في الموجبة : كلُّ لَا حيوان هو لا إنسان)؛ أي: إِنَّ كلَّ ما صدق عليهِ أنَّه غيرُ حيوانٍ ; صدق عليهِ أَنَّهُ غيرُ إنسان. ومثالُ مَعدولتِهما في السَّالبة: (ليس غيرُ الحيوانِ بغير جماد)، فقد حكم بسلْبِ حدم الجماديَّةِ عَن غيرِ الحيوان، وإذا سُلِبَ عدم الجماديَّة عن غير الحيوان؛ كان جماداً .
For example, our saying: 'The non-living is inanimate' and 'The inanimate is not knowing' and 'No non-living is knowing' or 'from the world without life'. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'The non-living is inanimate' means that whatever is truly described as non-living is truly described as inanimate, and this is an example of a positive with modified subject. And his saying: 'The inanimate is not knowing' is an example of a positive with modified predicate. And his saying: 'No non-living is knowing' is an example of a negative with modified subject. And his saying: 'or from the world without life' is an example of a negative with modified predicate. And he left out the example of both (subject and predicate) being modified in the positive and negative. An example of both being modified in the positive: 'Every non-animal is non-human' meaning that whatever is truly described as non-animal is truly described as non-human. And an example of both being modified in the negative: 'Not-non-animal is not-non-inanimate' for he has judged the negation of the inanimateness from non-animal, and when the non-inanimateness is negated from non-animal, it is inanimate.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage provides and classifies examples of modified propositions based on which part (subject or predicate) contains the negation particle and whether the proposition is positive or negative. Al-Dasuqi explains each example: 'The non-living is inanimate' is positive with modified subject. 'The inanimate is not knowing' is positive with modified predicate. 'No non-living is knowing' is negative with modified subject. 'From the world without life' is negative with modified predicate. He then supplies the missing examples where both subject and predicate are modified: for positive, 'Every non-animal is non-human' (both modified); for negative, 'Not-non-animal is not-non-inanimate' (both modified). This classification is crucial for analyzing propositional form in logic, as the presence of negation within terms affects the logical relationships and conversion rules. The examples demonstrate the range of modified propositions, showing that modification can occur on one or both sides, and the quality (affirmative or negative) is independent of the modification.

Key Terms
مَعدولة الموضوع
مَعدولة المحمول
مَعدولة الطرفين

Key Takeaway
Modified propositions are classified by their quality (positive/negative) independently of whether the subject or predicate term contains a negation particle.

The Rule of 'Qad' with Negative Verbs in Conditional Propositions
Al-Dasuki objects to using 'qad' with a negative verb; al-Attar clarifies it is acceptable when negation is not part of predicate or subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the particle 'qad' may precede a negative verb in conditional propositions based on whether the negation is part of the predicate or subject.

Core Idea
'Qad' can precede a negative verb only if the negation is not part of the verb's meaning.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement in the use of the particle 'qad' in conditional propositions. Al-Dasuki objects to the commentator's use of 'qad' with a negative verb, arguing that 'qad' is reserved for affirmative verbs. Al-Attar responds by clarifying that 'qad' is acceptable when the negation is not an integral part of the verb's meaning, as in the phrase 'wa qad la yakun'. This distinction is important for precise logical and grammatical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of a conditional proposition
Recall that a conditional proposition (shartiyya) consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali). The examples show negated antecedents, consequents, or both. Identify the negation particle (harf al-salab) in each case.
2
Understand the objection
Al-Dasuki objects that 'qad' is specific to affirmative verbs and cannot precede a negative verb. The commentator used 'qad' in 'wa qad la yakun', which appears to violate this rule.
This objection assumes that the verb after 'qad' is genuinely negative.
3
Examine al-Attar's response
Al-Attar explains that the phrase is true either because there is no negation particle at all (the verb is actually affirmative) or because the negation particle is not part of the predicate or subject. In either case, the negation is not integrated into the verb, so 'qad' is permissible.
This shows that the prohibition of 'qad' with negation applies only when the negation is a component of the verb's meaning.
4
Apply the rule to the example
In 'wa qad la yakun harf al-salab juz'an', the negation particle 'la' is separate and not part of the predicate or subject. Therefore, 'qad' is allowed. Practice identifying similar cases in conditional propositions.
The key is to determine whether the negation is integral to the verb phrase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثالُ الشَّرطيَّةِ المعدولة المقدَّم : (إنْ لم تكن الشَّمس طالعة؛ كانَ اللَّيلُ موجوداً) . ومثالها معدولةُ التَّالي : (إنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ؛ لم يكن اللَّيلُ موجوداً ) . ومثالها معدولةُ المقدَّمِ والتَّالي: (إنْ لم تكنِ الشَّمس طالعة؛ لم يكنِ النَّهارُ موجوداً).
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ لَا يَكُوْنُ) اعترضَ : بأنَّ «قد» خاصَّةٌ بالفعل المثبتِ، فَلا تدخل على المنفي، والشارح قد أدخلها عليه . وقوله : (وقد لا يكون حرفُ السَّلْبِ جزءاً ... إلخ)؛ صادق بأن لا يكون فيها حرفُ سَلْب أصلاً، أو فيها؛ إلا أنَّه ليسَ جُزءاً من واحدٍ منهما .
And an example of the conditional proposition with negated antecedent: 'If the sun is not rising, then night exists.' And an example of it with negated consequent: 'If the sun is rising, then night does not exist.' And an example of it with negated antecedent and consequent: 'If the sun is not rising, then daytime does not exist.' (His statement: 'And it may not be') There was an objection: that 'qad' is specific to the affirmative verb, so it does not enter upon the negative, and the commentator has entered it upon the negative. And his statement: 'And it may not be that the negation particle is a part... etc.' is true in that there is no negation particle at all, or there is, but it is not a part of either of them.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement. Al-Dasuki objects to the commentator's use of 'قد' (qad) with a negative verb in the phrase 'وقد لا يكون حرف السلب جزءاً' ('and it may not be that the negation particle is a part...'). The objection is that 'قد' is reserved for affirmative verbs and should not precede a negative. Al-Attar responds that the phrase is accurate: either there is no negation particle at all (in which case the verb is not actually negative) or if there is a negation particle, it is not a component of the predicate or subject, thus the negation is not integrated into the verb phrase. This shows that the prohibition of 'قد' with negation applies only when the negation is part of the verb's meaning, not when it is separate. The examples illustrate conditional propositions with negated components.

Key Terms
قد
حرف السلب
معدولة
شرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, under what condition can the particle 'qad' appear with a negative verb?
A) When the verb is in the past tense
B) When the negation particle is not a part of the predicate or subject
C) When the conditional particle is 'in'
D) When the proposition is affirmative

Key Takeaway
The particle 'qad' may precede a negative verb in conditional propositions as long as the negation is not an integral part of the predicate or subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a conditional proposition from a fiqh text (e.g., 'in lam yakun al-‘aqdu ṣaḥīḥan, fa al-ḥukmu al-buṭlān') and determine whether 'qad' could be added to the negated antecedent according to the rule discussed.
?
Construct a conditional proposition with a negated consequent and explain whether 'qad' can precede the negated verb based on al-Attar's clarification.

Simple Negative vs. Affirmative with Negated Predicate
Commentator distinguishes simple negative from affirmative with negated predicate, then classifies them as muhassala and basita.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a simple negative (saliba basita) from an affirmative with negated predicate (mujaba ma'dula al-mahmul) in terms of meaning and wording, and classify propositions as muhassala or basita.

Core Idea
The simple negative is more general because it does not require the subject to exist.

This lesson clarifies a crucial logical distinction: the simple negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing') versus the affirmative with a negated predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is non-standing'). Though both involve negation, their truth conditions differ. The commentator (al-Dasuqi) explains this distinction in meaning and wording, and then shows how these propositions are classified as muhassala (affirmative) and basita (simple negative). Understanding this difference is essential for accurate logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the meaning distinction
The simple negative removes the predicate from the subject; the affirmative with negated predicate affirms the absence of the predicate. Note the difference in the judgment.
Example: 'Zayd is not standing' vs. 'Zayd is non-standing'.
2
Note the truth-condition difference
The simple negative does not require the subject to exist for its truth, while the affirmative with negated predicate does require the subject to exist.
For instance, 'The present king of France is not bald' is true if there is no king, but 'The present king of France is non-bald' would be false because there is no subject.
3
Recognize the wording distinction in Arabic
In Arabic, the simple negative uses 'laysa' (e.g., laysa Zaydun bi-qa'in), while the negated predicate uses 'la' or 'ghayr' (e.g., la katib or ghayr katib).
This helps identify the type from the wording.
4
Apply the classification
After distinguishing, classify the proposition: muhassala if it is affirmative (even with a negated predicate), and basita if it is a simple negative.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' is muhassala; 'Zayd is not standing' is basita.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْفَرْقَ بَيْنَ السَّالِبَةِ الْبَسِيطَةِ وَالْمُوجَبَةِ الْمَعْدُولَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ؛ أَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْمَعْنَى فَهُوَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْأُولَى بِانْتِزَاعِ الْمَحْمُولِ عَنِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَفِي الثَّانِيَةِ: الْحُكْمُ بِثُبُوتِ عَدَمِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ، فَالسَّالِبَةُ أَعَمُّ بِحَسَبِ الْمَادَّةِ، فَإِنَّ صِدْقَهَا لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى وُجُودِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، بِخِلَافِ الْمُوجَبَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْدُولَةً؛ فَإِنَّ الشَّيْءَ مَا لَمْ يَثْبُتْ؛ لَا يَثْبُتُ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ؛ فَإِنْ كَانَتِ الْعِبَارَةُ فَارِسِيَّةً؛ فَالْأَمْرُ ظَاهِرٌ؛ لِأَنَّ لُغَةَ الْفُرْسِ أَمْرٌ، وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ اللَّفْظِ فَفِي الْعَرَبِيَّةِ يُفَرَّقُ بَيْنَهُمَا بِتَخْصِيصِ لَا كَاتِبٍ أَوْ غَيْرِ كَاتِبٍ بِالْعَدْوَلِ، وَتَخْصِيصِ لَيْسَ كَاتِبًا بِالسَّلْبِ، وَعَلَى تَقْدِيرِ جَعْلِ حَرَكَةِ الْإِعْرَابِ رَابِطَةً، فَالْفَرْقُ بِتَخْصِيصِ الْأَلْفَاظِ لِتَخْصِيصِ لَا كَاتِبٍ أَوْ غَيْرِ كَاتِبٍ بِالْعَدْوَلِ، وَتَخْصِيصِ لَيْسَ كَاتِبًا بِالسَّلْبِ. وَفِي الْفَارِسِيَّةِ الْأَمْرُ ظَاهِرٌ.
فَالْقَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ تُسَمَّى: مُحَصَّلَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجِبَةً. وَبَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَالْقَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ تُسَمَّى: مُحَصَّلَةً) أَيْ: لِتَحْصِيلِهَا لِلْحُكْمِ وَاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَيْهِ، وَقَدْ تُطْلَقُ الْمُحَصَّلَةُ عَلَى مَا لَيْسَتْ مَعْدُولَةً مُوجِبَةً أَوْ سَالِبَةً؛ لِتَحْصِيلِ طَرَفَيْهَا؛ أَيْ: وُجُودِهِمَا. (قَوْلُهُ: إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجِبَةً) نَحْوُ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَبَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً) كَقَوْلِكَ: لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِقَائِمٍ، وَسُمِّيَتْ بَسِيطَةً؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ هُنَاكَ إِلَّا سَلْبٌ وَاحِدٌ، وَلَوْ قُلْتَ: لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ لَا نَاطِقًا؛ لَمْ تَكُنْ بَسِيطَةً؛ لِتَكَرُّرِ السَّلْبِ. وَالْمَعْنَى: أَنَّ عَدَمَ النُّطْقِ مَسْلُوبٌ عَنْ زَيْدٍ أَوْ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّ أَجْزَاءَهَا لَيْسَتْ مُرَكَّبَةً؛ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا جَعَلْتَهَا مَعْدُولَةً؛ فَإِنَّ أَجْزَاءَهَا مُرَكَّبَةٌ.
And know that the difference between the simple negative and the affirmative with negated predicate: as to meaning, it is that the judgment in the first is the removal of the predicate from the subject, and in the second is the judgment of the establishment of the absence of the predicate for the subject. The negative is more general in terms of matter, for its truth does not depend on the existence of the subject, unlike the affirmative even if it is negated; for a thing, as long as it is not established, the word is not established for it. If the expression is Persian, the matter is obvious, because the Persian language is... As to wording, in Arabic they are distinguished by specifying 'la katib' or 'ghayr katib' for the negated, and specifying 'laysa katiban' for the negative. On the assumption of making the inflectional vowel the copula, the distinction is by specifying the words to specify 'la katib' or 'ghayr katib' for the negated, and specifying 'laysa katiban' for the negative. In Persian the matter is obvious. So the proposition is then called: muhassala if it is affirmative, and basita if it is negative. (His statement: 'So the proposition is then called: muhassala') i.e., because it contains the judgment and includes it. It may also be applied to what is not negated, whether affirmative or negative, because its two extremes are realized, i.e., their existence. (His statement: 'if it is affirmative') like 'Every human is an animal.' (His statement: 'and basita if it is negative') like your saying: 'Zayd is not standing,' and it is called basita because there is only one negation, but if you say: 'Zayd is not non-speaking,' it would not be basita due to the repetition of negation. The meaning is that the absence of speech is negated from Zayd, or because its parts are not composite, unlike when you make it negated, for its parts are composite.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion by the commentator (al-Dasuki) clarifying the distinction between two types of propositions: the simple negative (saliba basita) and the affirmative with negated predicate (mujaba ma'dula al-mahmul). The distinction is made in terms of meaning (ma'na) and wording (lafz). In meaning, the simple negative judges that the predicate is removed from the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing'), whereas the affirmative with negated predicate judges that the absence of the predicate is affirmed for the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is non-standing'). The simple negative is more general because its truth does not require the existence of the subject (it is true even if the subject does not exist, as in 'The present king of France is not bald'), while the affirmative with negated predicate does require the existence of the subject (something must exist to have a property absent from it). In wording, in Arabic, the distinction is made by using 'laysa' for the simple negative and 'la' or 'ghayr' for the negated predicate. These are then categorized into muhassala (affirmative, simple or with negation) and basita (simple negative). Muhassala is called such because it contains the judgment (hukm) or because its extremes are realized (exist). Basita is so named because it involves only one negation, not double negation like 'Zayd is not non-speaking'.

Key Terms
سَالِبَة بَسِيطَة
مُوجَبَة مَعْدُولَة الْمَحْمُول
مُحَصَّلَة
بَسِيطَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the key difference in truth conditions between a simple negative and an affirmative with negated predicate?
A) The simple negative is always false when the subject does not exist
B) The affirmative with negated predicate is true even if the subject does not exist
C) The simple negative does not require the subject to exist for its truth
D) Both require the subject to exist for truth

Key Takeaway
The simple negative (basita) and affirmative with negated predicate differ in truth conditions regarding subject existence, affecting logical analysis and classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a simple negative or affirmative with negated predicate: 'Every intoxicant is not permissible' vs. 'Every intoxicant is non-permissible'. Explain the truth condition difference.
?

Distinguishing Muḥaṣṣala and Basīṭa Propositions
Author defines muḥaṣṣala and basīṭa propositions with commentary on their structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is muḥaṣṣala, basīṭa, or ma'dūla based on its structure and negation pattern.

Core Idea
Muḥaṣṣala obtains the judgment; basīṭa has one negation; ma'dūla has a modified predicate.

In classical Islamic logic, categorical propositions are classified not only by quantity and quality, but also by their internal structure. The author of the Tadhheeb distinguishes between muḥaṣṣala (affirmative), basīṭa (simple negative), and ma'dūla (modified). This lesson unpacks that distinction with commentary from al-Dasūqī and al-‘Aṭṭār, showing how a single negation differs from a modified term.

Guided Path
1
Identify affirmative vs negative
Determine if the proposition is affirmative (positive copula) or negative (contains a negation particle like 'laysa' or 'mā').
2
Count the negations
If negative, count the negation words. If exactly one negation, it is basīṭa. If the predicate itself contains a negation (e.g., 'lā kātib' or 'ghayr kātib'), it is ma'dūla.
Double negation (e.g., 'laysa Zaydun lā nāṭiqan') is not basīṭa.
3
Apply the Arabic linguistic test
If the copula 'huwa' is used, examine its position. If 'huwa' precedes the negation, the proposition is ma'dūla; if it follows, it is negative (basīṭa). If no copula is mentioned, ambiguity exists.
This test is from al-‘Aṭṭār's commentary.
4
Classify the proposition
Based on the above, label the proposition as muḥaṣṣala (affirmative), basīṭa (simple negative), or ma'dūla (modified negative or affirmative with modified predicate).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقضيَّة حينئذٍ تُسمَّى : - محصلة إنْ كانَتْ موجِبَةٌ . [الحمديَّةُ الموجَهَةُ] وبسيطةً إن كانت سالبةٌ . واعلَمْ أنَّ نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع إيجابية كانت أو سلبية، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَالْقَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ تُسَمَّى : مُحَصَّلَةٌ) أي : لتحصيلها للحكم واشتمالها عليه، وقد تُطلَقُ المحصلة على ما ليسَتْ مَعدولةٌ مُوجبة أو سالبة؛ لتحصيل طرفيها ؛ أي : وجودهما . (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوْجِبَةٌ) نحو : كلُّ إنسان حيوانٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ كَقولِكَ : ليس زيدٌ بِقائم ، وسُمِّيَتْ بسيطة؛ لأنَّه ليس هناك إلا سلبٌ واحدٌ، فَلَو قُلْتَ : ليسَ زيدٌ لا ناطقاً ; لم تكن بسيطة ; لتكرُّرِ السَّلْبِ. والمعنى: أنَّ عدمَ النُّطقِ مَسلوبٌ من زيد أو باعتبار أنَّ أجزاءَهَا ليسَتْ مُركَّبَةٌ ; بخلافِ مَا إذا جعلتها معدولةٌ ; فإِنَّ أجزاءها مُركَّبة. العطار تُفرِّقُ بينَهُما لفظاً، وإنْ كانَتْ عربيَّةً؛ فعلى تقدير جعل الحركة الإعرابية رابطة، فالفَرقُ بتخصيص الألفاظ لتخصيص لا كاتب أو غير كاتب بالعدول، وتخصيص ليس كاتباً بالسَّلْبِ، وعلى تقدير أنْ تجعل كلمة هو رابطةً، فإنْ كانَتِ القضيَّةُ ثنائيَّةٌ ولمْ تُذكَّر الرَّابطة ; فهي صالحة للعدولِ والسَّلْبِ بحسب الاعتبارِ وإِنْ ذُكرَتْ، فَإِنْ قُدِّمَتْ على حرفِ السَّلْبِ؛ فَمعدولةٌ، وإِنْ أُخْرَتْ، فَسالبةٌ .
The proposition then is called: muḥaṣṣala if it is affirmative, and basīṭa if it is negative. And know that the relationship of the predicate to the subject, whether affirmative or negative, [al-Dasūqī: His saying: 'The proposition then is called muḥaṣṣala' means because it obtains the judgment and includes it, and muḥaṣṣala is sometimes applied to what is not modified (ma'dūla), affirmative or negative, because its two extremes are obtained, i.e., their existence. His saying: 'If it is affirmative' like: Every human is an animal. His saying: 'If it is negative' like your saying: Zayd is not standing, and it is called basīṭa because there is only one negation; for if you said: Zayd is not not-speaking, it would not be basīṭa due to the repetition of negation. The meaning is that the absence of speech is negated from Zayd, or by considering that its parts are not composite, unlike when you make it modified, for its parts are composite. al-‘Aṭṭār: They differ verbally; and if it is Arabic, then on the assumption of making the inflectional movement the copula, the difference is by specifying the expressions: specifying 'lā kātib' or 'ghayr kātib' for modification, and specifying 'laysa kātiban' for negation. On the assumption of making the word 'huwa' the copula, if the proposition is bipartite and the copula is not mentioned, it is valid for both modification and negation by consideration; if it is mentioned, then if it precedes the particle of negation, it is modified, and if it follows, it is negative.]
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary distinguishing two types of categorical propositions: muḥaṣṣala (affirmative) and basīṭa (simple negative). The author begins by stating that a proposition is called muḥaṣṣala if it is affirmative, and basīṭa if it is negative. Then a commentator (al-Dasūqī) explains that muḥaṣṣala is so called because it obtains the judgment and includes it, and that the term is also used for propositions that are not modified (ma'dūla), whether affirmative or negative, because both extremes are obtained. He gives an example of an affirmative muḥaṣṣala: 'Every human is an animal'. For the basīṭa, he gives an example: 'Zayd is not standing', and explains that it is called basīṭa (simple) because it contains only one negation; if one said 'Zayd is not not-speaking', it would not be basīṭa due to the double negation. The meaning is that the absence of speech is negated from Zayd, or alternatively, its parts are not composite, unlike a modified (ma'dūla) proposition which has composite parts (e.g., 'Zayd is not-standing' or 'Zayd is non-standing'—these are considered composite because the predicate includes a negation or modification). Then al-‘Aṭṭār adds a further distinction between negation and modification in Arabic. He explains that if the inflectional movement (i‘rāb) is taken as the copula, then the difference is purely verbal: 'lā kātib' or 'ghayr kātib' indicate modification, while 'laysa kātiban' indicates negation. If the word 'huwa' (he) is taken as the copula, then if the proposition is bipartite (subject and predicate only) and the copula is not mentioned, it is ambiguous between modification and negation; if the copula is mentioned, its position determines the meaning: if it precedes the negation particle, the proposition is modified (e.g., 'Zayd huwa lā kātib'), and if it follows, it is negative (e.g., 'Zayd lā huwa kātib' or 'Zayd laysa huwa kātib'). This careful analysis shows the logical precision required to distinguish between simple negation and modified terms, a key distinction in propositional logic.

Key Terms
مُحَصَّلَة
بَسِيطَة
مَعْدُولَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zayd is not standing.
B) Zayd is not non-speaking.
C) Zayd is not knowledgeable.
D) Every human is not a stone.

Key Takeaway
The basīṭa proposition is defined by having a single negation, distinguishing it from both affirmative (muḥaṣṣala) and modified (ma'dūla) propositions, with Arabic syntactic markers to differentiate them.

Distinguishing Material from Formal Modality in Modal Propositions
Commentator defines modal proposition and distinguishes between material and formal modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'matter' (مادة) and 'formal modality' (جهة) of a modal proposition and evaluate whether the proposition is true or false according to the later logicians.

Core Idea
The matter is reality's modality; the formal modality is our expression—truth demands they match.

In this lesson, we explore a crucial distinction in modal logic: the difference between what is actually the case in reality (the 'matter' or material modality) and the modality we express in language or conceive in the mind (the 'formal modality' or جهة). The commentator al-Dasuqi, building on the Tadhheeb, explains how these two can diverge and what that means for the truth of a proposition. This distinction is foundational for understanding how logicians in the Islamic tradition analyzed propositions with necessity, possibility, and impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reference frame: nafs al-amr
The phrase 'related to the actual state of affairs' (نسبة إلى نفس الأمر) means we consider the relation between subject and predicate as it exists in reality, independent of our mental conception or linguistic expression. This is the objective standpoint.
Nafs al-amr is the thing in itself, not our perception of it.
2
Identify the two fundamental modalities
The passage states that the modality of the relation is confined to possibility (إمكان) and intellectual necessity (وجوب عقلي). Impossibility is derived from the negation of possibility. All other modalities branch from these two.
These are the basic kinds of modality in categorical propositions.
3
Distinguish matter (مادة) from formal modality (جهة)
The modality considered in itself, as realized in reality, is called the 'matter' (مادة) of the proposition. When that same modality is impressed in the mind or expressed in utterance, it is called the 'formal modality' (جهة). The matter is part of the proposition's four elements.
The matter is the real modality; the formal modality is the conceived or stated modality.
4
Apply the truth condition for later logicians
For later logicians, a modal proposition is true only if the formal modality corresponds to the matter. The text gives the example: 'Every human is an animal by possibility.' The real relation is necessity (animal necessarily belongs to human), so the proposition is false because the formal modality (possibility) does not match the matter (necessity).
This correspondence is the criterion of truth for modal propositions.
5
Contrast with the earlier logicians' terminology
Earlier logicians defined 'matter' as the modality of the affirmative relation (necessity, possibility, impossibility) and 'formal modality' as the word indicating that modality. They allowed the formal modality to differ from the matter even in true propositions, as long as the word appropriately indicates the intended modality. This reflects a shift in technical terminology.
Understanding this historical shift prevents confusion when reading older logical texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ إذا نُسِبَتْ إلى نفس الأمرِ. قَوْلُهُ: إِذَا نُسِبَتْ إِلَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أي : إذا نظر لها باعتبار ما في الواقع. واعلَمْ أنَّ كيفيَّةَ النِّسبة تنحصر في الإمكانِ والضَّرورة؛ أي: الوجوب، فَجميعُ الكيفيَّاتِ تتفرّعُ على هَذَيْنِ، والمراد بالوجوب: الوجوب العقلي. ثمَّ إِنَّ نسبة التّالي للمقدَّم أيضاً لا تخلو عن تلك الكيفية، لكنَّ عادة المتأخرين جرَتْ باعتبار اللزومِ والعنادِ والاتِّفاقِ بينَهُمَا؛ لا باعتبار تلك الجهاتِ. قَوْلُهُ: إِذَا نُسِبَتْ إِلَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أي: إذا نُظِرَ لِلنِّسبة المفهومةِ مِنَ القضيَّةِ باعتبار وجودِهَا في نفسِهَا؛ أي: تحقَّقها في الواقع بقطعِ النَّظَرِ عن فهمِنَا لَهَا مِنَ اللَّفْظ، فَنفس الأمر عبارةٌ عَنِ الشَّيء في نفسِهِ. قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُوْنَ هذا دليلُ جواب «إذا» المحذوفة، وتقديره: فَلَا بُدَّ لها مِن أحدِ الأمرين. قَوْلُهُ: مُكَيَّفَةٌ ... إِلَخ وهذهِ الكيفيَّةُ باعتبارِ تحقُّقِهَا في نفسِ الأمرِ تُسمَّى مادَّةَ القضيَّةِ وعنصرَهَا، والمادَّةُ وإنْ كانَت مُشتركةً بين الطرفين والنسبة وكيفيتها في نفس الأمرِ لِكَوْنِ كُلِّ منها جزءاً؛ لكنَّهُم خَصُّوهَا بالكيفيَّةِ، وتَسميتُهَا عُنصراً؛ لِكُونِهَا جزءاً مِنَ القضيَّةِ المربعةِ الأجزاء، والعناصر أربعة، وباعتبار ارتسامها في العقل أو ذكرها في العبارة؛ تُسمَّى جهةً، وَلَمَّا لم تجبْ مُطابقةُ ما في الذهن والعبارة لِمَا في نفس الأمر؛ جاز أن لا تكون الجهةُ مُطابقةٌ لِلمادَّةِ كَمَا إذا تَعمَّلْنَا أَنَّ نسبة الحيوانِ إلى الإنسان هو الإمكان وقُلْنَا: كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالإمكانِ، فَجِهَةُ القضيَّةِ هِيَ الإمكانُ؛ لأنَّهُ المتعقَّلُ في الذهن والمذكورُ في العبارة، ومادَّةُ القضيَّةِ هِيَ الضَّرورة؛ لأنَّها كيفيَّةُ نسبة الحيوان إلى الإنسان في نفس الأمرِ، فَالجهة قد تُخالِفُ المادَّةَ، لكن لا يكون ذلك إلا في القضيَّةِ الكاذبة، ويُعتبرُ في صدق الموجهة مطابقة الكيفيَّةِ لِلمادَّةِ على ما اعتبره المتأخّرونَ، وأمَّا على اصطلاح القدماء؛ فَالمادَّةُ كيفيَّةُ النّسبة الإيجابيَّةِ بِالوجوبِ أو الإمكان أو الامتناع، والجهةُ هِيَ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُّ على ما اعتبره المعتبر كيفيَّةٌ لِتلكَ النِّسبة؛ سواء كانَتْ هِي غير تلك المادَّةِ أو أعم منها أو أخص أو مبايناً، فالجهة على هذا قَدْ تُخالِفُ المادة في القضيَّةِ الصَّادقة
Section: On the modal proposition and its divisions. When it is related to the actual state of affairs (nafs al-amr). His saying: 'When it is related to the actual state of affairs' means: when it is considered with respect to what is in reality. And know that the modality of the relation is confined to possibility and necessity; i.e., obligatoriness, so all modalities branch off from these two. And by 'obligatoriness' is meant intellectual necessity. Then indeed the relation of the consequent to the antecedent is also not free from that modality, but the custom of the later scholars has proceeded according to consideration of entailment, incompatibility, and agreement between them, not according to those aspects. His saying: 'When it is related to the actual state of affairs' means: when the relation understood from the proposition is regarded with respect to its existence in itself; i.e., its realization in reality, disregarding our understanding of it from the utterance. So 'actual state of affairs' is an expression for the thing in itself. His saying: 'Either it is...' This is the evidence for the omitted answer of 'when', and its estimation is: so it must be one of the two things. His saying: 'modal... etc.' And this modality, with respect to its realization in the actual state of affairs, is called the matter (mādda) of the proposition and its element. Although the matter is shared between the two terms, the relation, and its modality in the actual state of affairs because each is a part, they have specified it to the modality. And its being called an element is because it is a part of the four-part proposition, as the elements are four. And with respect to its impression in the mind or its mention in the utterance, it is called the formal modality (jiha). And since the correspondence of what is in the mind and utterance to what is in the actual state of affairs is not necessary, it is possible that the formal modality does not correspond to the matter, as when we assume that the relation of 'animal' to 'human' is possibility and we say: 'Every human is an animal by possibility.' So the formal modality of the proposition is possibility because it is what is conceived in the mind and mentioned in the utterance, while the matter of the proposition is necessity because it is the modality of the relation of animal to human in the actual state of affairs. Thus the formal modality may differ from the matter, but this occurs only in a false proposition. And in the truth of a modal proposition, correspondence of the formal modality to the matter is considered according to what the later scholars held. As for the terminology of the earlier scholars, the matter is the modality of the affirmative relation by necessity, possibility, or impossibility, and the formal modality is the word indicating what the considerer considered as the modality for that relation, whether it is other than that matter, or more general, or more specific, or entirely different. So on this, the formal modality may differ from the matter in a true proposition.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from the gloss of al-Dasuqi explains the concept of modal propositions (قضية مكيفة) and clarifies the key distinction between the 'matter' (مادة) and the 'formal modality' (جهة) of a proposition. The commentary begins by defining the phrase 'related to the actual state of affairs' (نسبة إلى نفس الأمر), meaning that the relation between subject and predicate is considered as it exists in reality, independent of our mental conception or linguistic expression. The author then states that the modality of this relation is confined to two fundamental kinds: possibility (إمكان) and necessity (وجوب عقلي). All other modalities branch from these. He further notes that in conditional propositions, later logicians treat the relation between antecedent and consequent in terms of entailment, incompatibility, or mere conjunction, rather than in terms of the same modality categories used for categoricals. The central part of the lesson explains the distinction: the modality of the relation considered in itself (in reality) is called the 'matter' (مادة) and is considered an element of the proposition. When that same modality is considered as it is impressed in the mind or expressed in language, it is called the 'formal modality' (جهة). The author emphasizes that these two can differ, giving the example: 'Every human is an animal by possibility.' The real relation (matter) is necessity, because animal is necessarily predicated of human, but if we say 'by possibility' the formal modality is possibility, making the proposition false because the formal modality does not match the matter. Thus, for later logicians, truth requires correspondence. The passage concludes by contrasting the earlier terminology: for the earlier logicians, 'matter' refers to the modality of the affirmative relation (necessity, possibility, impossibility), while 'formal modality' is the word used to express that modality, and they allowed the formal modality to differ from the matter even in true propositions, as long as the word used appropriately indicates the intended modality. This highlights a shift in technical terminology within the logical tradition.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a modal proposition requires that the formal modality (جهة) matches the material modality (مادة) as it exists in reality (نفس الأمر), according to the later logicians; otherwise the proposition is false.

Distinguishing Material and Modal Quality of Propositions
Author explains the difference between madda (material) and jiha (modal) quality of propositional relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the material (mādda) and modal (jiha) quality of a proposition and determine when a modal proposition is true based on the correspondence of modality to matter.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth depends on whether its modal expression matches its real material quality.

Building on the components of a proposition, we now explore two key dimensions: the material quality (how the relation actually is in reality) and the modal quality (how it is expressed or conceived). Understanding the difference is crucial for evaluating modal propositions, as mismatches lead to falsehood. This passage from the glossators clarifies the definitions and the historical shift in terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate the Arabic terms mādda (المادة) and jiha (الجهة) in the passage. Note that they refer to the same quality (kaifiyya) but considered from different perspectives: in reality (mādda) vs. in mind or expression (jiha).
The passage states: 'في نفس الأمر تسمى مادة' and 'باعتبار ارتسامها في العقل أو ذكرها في العبارة تسمى جهة'.
2
Extract the key definitions
Write the definition of mādda: the quality of the relation as it actually exists in reality, one of the four elements of a proposition. Write the definition of jiha: the quality as it is impressed in the mind or mentioned in expression.
Mādda is part of the proposition's truth conditions; jiha is the modal indicator.
3
Apply the test of truth (later scholars)
According to the later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn), a modal proposition is true only if the jiha corresponds to the mādda. Analyse the example from the passage: 'Every human is an animal by possibility.' Here the jiha is possibility, but the mādda is necessity (a human must be an animal). Therefore the proposition is false because of mismatch.
The passage says: 'فالجهة قد تخالف المادة، لكن لا يكون ذلك إلا في القضية الكاذبة' – mismatch only in false propositions according to later scholars.
4
Compare the earlier terminology
The ancients (qudamā) defined mādda as the quality of the affirmative relation (obligation, possibility, impossibility) and jiha as any word indicating a considered quality. On their view, mismatch could occur even in true propositions, e.g., 'Human is an animal by general possibility' where the mādda is obligation but the jiha is possibility. The later scholars abandoned this because it was insufficient for the details of propositions.
The passage mentions: 'وأما على اصطلاح القدماء فالمادة كيفية النسبة الإيجابية بالوجوب أو الإمكان أو الامتناع، والجهة هي اللفظ الدال على ما اعتبره المعتبر كيفية لتلك النسبة'.
