إمَّا أن تكونَ مُكَيَّفةٌ (قَوْلُهُ : مُكَيَّفَةً) أي : مُتَّصِفَة . انتُزِعَتْ منه، وفي هذا الكلام بقيَّةٌ تُطلب من حواشينا على المقولات الصغرى، فَمعنى قولهم : النِّسبةُ ثابتةٌ أو واقعةٌ في نفس الأمر هو أن يكون نفس الأمر ظرفاً لها؛ لا بمعنى كون نفس الأمرِ ظرفاً لوجودِهَا ، وبينَهُمَا فرقٌ تعرَّضَ لَهُ السَّيِّدُ في مؤلَّفاتِهِ، ويؤخذُ مِن قولِهِ : إذا نُسِبَتْ إلى نفس الأمرِ أَنَّهُ لا بُدَّ مِن تقييدِ نسبةِ المحمول إلى الموضوع بنسبتِها إلى نفس الأمرِ ؛ إذ النِّسبة المعتبرةُ بينَ الشَّيئين إذا لم يفرض وجودها في نفس الأمرِ ؛ لا يُعرضُ لها كيفيَّةٌ في نفس الأمر أصلاً ا.هـ... (قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُوْنَ) هذا دليلُ جواب «إذا» المحذوفة، وتقديره : فَلَا بُدَّ لها مِن أحدِ الأمرين ؛ لأنَّها إِمَّا أَنْ تكونَ ... إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : مُكَيَّفَةٌ ... إِلَخ) وهذهِ الكيفيَّةُ باعتبارِ تحقُّقِهَا في نفسِ الأمرِ تُسمَّى مادَّةَ القضيَّةِ وعنصرَهَا ، والمادَّةُ وإنْ كانَت مُشتركةً بين الطرفين والنسبة وكيفيتها في نفس الأمرِ لِكَونِ كُلِّ منها جزءاً ؛ لكنَّهُم خَصُّوهَا بالكيفيَّةِ، وتَسميتُهَا عُنصراً؛ لِكُونِهَا جزءاً مِنَ القضيَّةِ المربعةِ الأجزاء، والعناصر أربعة، وباعتبار ارتسامها في العقل أو ذكرها في العبارة؛ تُسمَّى جهةً، وَلَمَّا لم تجبْ مُطابقةُ ما في الذهن والعبارة لِمَا في نفس الأمر؛ جاز أن لا تكون الجهةُ مُطابقةٌ لِلمادَّةِ كَمَا إذا تَعمَّلْنَا أَنَّ نسبة الحيوانِ إلى الإنسان هو الإمكان وقُلْنَا : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالإمكانِ، فَجِهَةُ القضيَّةِ هِيَ الإمكانُ؛ لأنَّهُ المتعقَّلُ في الذهن والمذكورُ في العبارة، ومادَّةُ القضيَّةِ هِيَ الضَّرورة؛ لأنَّها كيفيَّةُ نسبة الحيوان إلى الإنسان في نفس الأمرِ، فَالجهة قد تُخالِفُ المادَّةَ، لكن لا يكون ذلك إلا في القضيَّةِ الكاذبة، ويُعتبرُ في صدق الموجهة مطابقة الكيفيَّةِ لِلمادَّةِ على ما اعتبره المتأخّرونَ، وأمَّا على اصطلاح القدماء ؛ فَالمادَّةُ كيفيَّةُ النّسبة الإيجابيَّةِ بِالوجوبِ أو الإمكان أو الامتناع، والجهةُ هِيَ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُّ على ما اعتبره المعتبر كيفيَّةٌ لِتلكَ النِّسبة ؛ سواء كانَتْ هِي غير تلك المادَّةِ أو أعم منها أو أخص أو مبايناً، فالجهة على هذا قَدْ تُخالِفُ المادة في القضيَّةِ الصَّادقة أيضاً ؛ كَقَولِنَا : الإنسانُ حيوان بالإمكان العام، فالمادَّةُ هي الوجوب، والجهة أعم منه، وَلَمَّا كانَ اصطلاح القدماء غير وافٍ بتفاصيل القضايا ؛ عدل عنه المتأخّرونَ فَأَذَاهُ المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة وغيرُه (۲)، ثمَّ ما ذكر من اعتبار المطابقة وعدمها في الجهة؛ جرى على ما هو المختارُ مِن جري المطابقة واللامطابقة في التصوراتِ ؛ وهوَ الظَّاهِرُ ، وأمَّا ما يُقالُ : إِنَّ التَّصوراتِ كُلَّها مُطابقةٌ لِلواقع والخطأ إِنَّما هو في الحُكْمِ الضّمني ؛ فَجَرى على أنَّ التَّصوراتِ لا نقائض لها، وعلى هذا القول : فَاعتبارُ المطابقة واللامطابقة باعتبار مجموع النِّسبةِ مع كيفيَّتِها؛ تَأَمَّلْ. (قَوْلُهُ : بِكَيْفِيَّةِ الضَّرُورَةِ ... إِلَخ) المراد بها مفهوماتها؛ إذْ لو أُريدَ ما صدقتا عليه ؛ كان ذكرُ الدَّوامِ واللادوام مستدركاً. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ... إِلَخ) أفاد هذا البيانُ أن ليسَ عَرضُ الشَّارحِ حصر النّسبةِ في الأربع، بل حصرُهَا في اثنين اثنين منها ، وأَنَّ هَذَا تَنويع في التَّعبيرِ؛ أي: (١) (قَوْلُهُ : والمراد بالضرورة ... إلخ) دفع بهذا إيراداً حاصله؛ الجهات أربع : الضرورة والإمكان المعبر عنه باللاضرورة ولدوام الإطلاق المعبر عنه باللادوام، والشارح ذكرها كلها في عبارته، وحينئذ فقوله : إلى غير ذلك، مستدرك، والجواب : أن مراده بالضرورة الذاتية دون الوصفية والوقتية فخرجت المشروطة والوقتية والمنتشرة عنها ودخلت في قوله إلى غير ذلك فلا استدراك وفيه أن المراد لا يدفع الإيراد إلا أن يقال: إن الضرورة متى أطلقت انصرفت عرفاً إلى الضرورة الذاتية فقط . ا.هـ. الشرنوبي. (۲) (قَولُهُ: وغيره) أي: غير المصنف. ا.هـ. منه
Either it is qualified (his saying: 'qualified') i.e., described. Extracted from it, and in this speech there is a remainder sought from our glosses on the Minor Categories. So the meaning of their saying: 'The relation is established or occurring in reality' is that reality is a container for it; not in the sense of reality being a container for its existence. There is a difference between them which al-Sayyid discusses in his works, and it is taken from his saying: 'When it is related to reality, it is necessary to restrict the relation of the predicate to the subject by its relation to reality; for the relation considered between two things, if its existence in reality is not assumed, no quality occurs to it in reality at all.' End quote. (His saying: 'Either it is') This is the evidence of the answer to the omitted 'idhā', and its implied meaning: 'It must have one of two things; because it is either ... etc.' (His saying: 'qualified ... etc.') This quality, in terms of its verification in reality, is called the matter (mādda) of the proposition and its element. Matter, although it is shared among the terms, the relation, and its quality in reality because each is a part, they nevertheless restricted it to the quality, and they called it 'element' because it is a part of the four-part proposition, and the elements are four. In terms of its impression in the mind or its mention in expression, it is called modality (jiha). And since the correspondence of what is in the mind and expression to what is in reality is not necessary, it is permissible for the modality not to correspond to the matter, as when we use the relation of animal to human as possibility and say: 'Every human is an animal by possibility.' So the modality of the proposition is possibility, because it is what is conceived in the mind and mentioned in expression, and the matter of the proposition is necessity, because it is the quality of the relation of animal to human in reality. So the modality may differ from the matter, but that only occurs in a false proposition. And in the truth of a modal proposition, the correspondence of the quality to the matter is considered according to what the later scholars held. As for according to the terminology of the ancients, matter is the quality of the affirmative relation by obligation, possibility, or impossibility, and modality is the word indicating what the considerer considered as a quality for that relation, whether it is that same matter or more general, more specific, or disparate. So on this, modality may differ from matter even in a true proposition, as in our saying: 'Human is an animal by general possibility' — the matter is obligation, and the modality is more general than it. And since the terminology of the ancients was insufficient for the details of propositions, the later scholars turned away from it, and the author of the Sharh al-Risāla and others adopted it. Then what was mentioned about considering correspondence and non-correspondence in modality follows what is chosen regarding the occurrence of correspondence and non-correspondence in conceptions; and that is apparent. As for what is said: 'All conceptions correspond to reality, and error is only in the implicit judgment' — it follows that conceptions have no contradictories. On this view, the consideration of correspondence and non-correspondence is with respect to the whole relation along with its quality; reflect. (His saying: 'By the quality of necessity ... etc.') The intent is their concepts; for if what they apply to were intended, the mention of permanence and non-permanence would be redundant. (His saying: 'And either it is ... etc.') This clarification indicates that the commentator's aim is not to restrict the relation to four, but to restrict it to two and two, and that this is a diversification in expression; that is: (1) (His saying: 'And the intent by necessity ... etc.') By this he repels an objection whose summary is: The modalities are four: necessity, possibility expressed by non-necessity, and absolute permanence expressed by non-permanence, and the commentator mentioned them all in his statement. Therefore his saying 'and so on' is redundant. The answer: That his intent by necessity is essential necessity, not descriptive or temporal, so the conditional, temporal, and distributive come out from it and enter into his saying 'and so on', so there is no redundancy. But there is that the intent does not repel the objection unless it is said: When necessity is used absolutely, customarily it refers only to essential necessity. End quote by al-Sharnūbī. (2) (His saying: 'and other') i.e., other than the author. End quote.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the glossators (Dasuqi and 'Attar) on the distinction between the material (mādda) and modal (jiha) aspects of a proposition. The discussion begins by clarifying the phrase 'either it is qualified' (إما أن تكون مكيفة), explaining that 'qualified' means 'described'. The author then notes that the meaning of the relation being 'established in reality' is that reality serves as a container for the relation, not for its existence — this is a subtle point from al-Sayyid. The core of the lesson is the definition of mādda and jiha: when the quality (kaifiyya) of the relation is considered as it actually exists in reality (fī nafs al-amr), it is called mādda and it is one of the four elements of a proposition (subject, predicate, relation, and this quality). When that quality is considered as it is impressed in the mind or expressed in speech, it is called jiha (modality). Because it is not necessary for mental representation and verbal expression to match reality, the jiha may differ from the mādda. The example given is the proposition 'Every human is an animal by possibility' — here the jiha is imkān (possibility) because we say it, but the actual relation in reality is necessary (ḍarūrī) because a human cannot be other than an animal. Thus the proposition is false because the jiha does not match the mādda. The later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn) require correspondence for truth. However, the early scholars (qudamā) defined mādda as the quality of the affirmative relation (wujūb, imkān, imtinā') and jiha as the word indicating what the speaker considers as the quality, which could be different even in a true proposition, e.g., 'Human is an animal by general possibility' where the mādda is wujūb (necessity) but the jiha is imkān (possibility). The later scholars found the ancient terminology inadequate for the details of propositions and abandoned it. The passage then discusses how the correspondence of jiha to mādda is tied to debates about correspondence in conceptions (taṣawwurāt). One view is that all conceptions correspond to reality and error is only in judgment; on this view, correspondence is considered for the whole relation with its quality. Finally, a footnote clarifies that the listing of modalities (ḍarūra, lāḍarūra, dawām, lādawām) is a diversification, and that 'necessity' here means essential necessity (ḍarūra dhātiyya), not temporal or descriptive necessity, to avoid redundancy.

Key Terms
المادة
الجهة
الضرورة
الإمكان
الدوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the passage, 'Every human is an animal by possibility,' why is the proposition false according to the later scholars?
A) Because the predicate 'animal' is not true of all humans.
B) Because the expressed modality (possibility) does not match the real necessity of the relation.
C) Because the subject 'human' is not genuinely related to 'animal'.
D) Because possibility cannot be used as a modality for universal propositions.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between material (real) and modal (expressed) quality is fundamental for evaluating the truth of modal propositions; later scholars required correspondence for truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'Every human is rational by necessity.' Determine the mādda and jiha, and state whether the proposition is true according to the later scholars, explaining why.
?
In the ancient terminology, the proposition 'Every human is rational by necessity' would be analysed differently. Identify what the ancients would call the mādda and jiha, and explain why a mismatch might not automatically make it false.

Modal Qualities: Necessity and Non-Necessity
Author defines modal qualities of propositions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary and non-necessary modal qualities in categorical propositions and identify when a proposition is modal or absolute.

Core Idea
Modal quality is fixed in reality, whether or not it is expressed.

In logic, every categorical proposition has a modal quality—necessity or non-necessity—that attaches to the relation between subject and predicate. The author illustrates this with two clear examples from common knowledge. However, he also warns that this quality may be left unexpressed, turning a modal proposition into an absolute one. Let's examine how to identify and classify these modal qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition and its subject-predicate relation
Consider the proposition 'Every human is an animal.' Here the subject is 'human' and the predicate is 'animal'. Look at the relation between them in reality.
2
Determine if the relation is necessary or non-necessary
Ask: can the relation be otherwise? For 'Every human is an animal,' it is necessary—a human cannot be other than an animal. For 'Every human is a writer,' it is non-necessary—a human may or may not be a writer.
3
Check if the modality is explicitly stated or considered
The modal quality may be expressed verbally (e.g., saying 'necessarily') or mentally considered. If not, the proposition is absolute, not modal.
4
Classify the proposition as modal (muwajjaha) or absolute (mutlaqa)
If the quality is expressed, it is a modal proposition. If not, it is absolute, even though the quality exists in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قُلْنَا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، ونظرنا إلى نسبتها في الواقع؛ وجدناها ضروريَّةٌ، وإذا قلنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ كاتب؛ وجدنا نِسبتها اللاضرورية . فالضَّرورة واللاضرورة في المثالين هي كيفيَّةُ النِّسبةِ. ثمَّ تلك الكيفيَّةُ الثَّابتةُ في نفْس الأمر قد لا يُصرَّحُ بها لا لفظاً وَلَا ملاحظةً، وتخرج عن كونها موجهة
For if we say: 'Every human is an animal,' and we look at its relation in reality, we find it necessary; and if we say: 'Every human is a writer,' we find its relation non-necessary. So necessity and non-necessity in the two examples are the quality of the relation. Then that quality fixed in reality may not be expressed verbally nor mentally, and it departs from being modal.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage defines modal qualities (al-kayfiyyat) of propositions. The author explains that when we examine a proposition like 'Every human is an animal' with respect to reality, we find that the relation between subject and predicate is necessary—it cannot be otherwise. In contrast, 'Every human is a writer' expresses a relation that is non-necessary—it could be otherwise. These two examples illustrate that the quality of the relation is either necessity (al-darura) or non-necessity (al-ladarura). The author then clarifies that this quality, although fixed in reality (thabita fi nafs al-amr), may not be explicitly stated in words (lafzan) nor even mentally considered (mulahazatan). When the quality is omitted, the proposition is called 'absolute' (mutlaqa) and is no longer considered a modal proposition (muwajjaha). Thus, the passage distinguishes between the modal quality inherent in the state of affairs and the linguistic or conceptual expression of that quality.

Key Terms
الضَّرورة (al-darura): The quality of a proposition's relation when it is necessarily true in reality.
اللاضرورة (al-ladarura): The quality of a proposition's relation when it is not necessarily true in reality, i.e., possible.
كيفيَّة النِّسبة (kayfiyyat al-nisba): The modality (necessity or non-necessity) that attaches to the relation between subject and predicate in a proposition.
ثابتة في نفس الأمر (thabita fi nafs al-amr): Fixed in reality, i.e., the modal quality actually holds in the external world regardless of expression. [Note: In later works, this phrase is often used to distinguish objective modality from subjective judgment.]
موجهة (muwajjaha): A modal proposition, i.e., one in which the modality is explicitly stated or considered.
مطلقة (mutlaqa): An absolute proposition, one in which the modality is not expressed. [Note: The term 'mutlaqa' may also refer to unconditional propositions in later logical works.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Looking at the relation in reality, what is its modal quality?
A) ضرورية (necessary)
B) لاضرورية (non-necessary)
C) دوامية (perpetual)
D) لا دوامية (non-perpetual)

Key Takeaway
Modal quality is a real attribute of the relation in a proposition, existing independently of whether it is articulated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (al-ʿālam ḥādith). Identify its modal quality and state whether it is necessary or non-necessary in reality according to the Ashʿarī position.
?
Classify the following propositions as muwajjaha (modal) or mutlaqa (absolute): 'Every human is mortal', 'Zayd is standing', 'Fire is hot'.

Distinguishing Absolute from Modal Propositions
Author explains necessary/non-necessary and absolute/modal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given categorical proposition is absolute (mutlaqa) or modal (mujawwaha) by checking for explicit statement of modality.

Core Idea
Modality is the explicit quality of the subject-predicate relation; without it, a proposition is absolute.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by modality (jihah) as well as quantity and quality. The key distinction is whether the quality of the relation—necessity or non-necessity—is explicitly stated. This passage from the Tadhheeb Sharh explains the difference using simple examples and clarifies nuances through commentary. Mastering this distinction is foundational for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify Subject and Predicate
Take any categorical proposition. Identify the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul). The relation (nisba) between them has a quality (kayfiyya): either necessary (darura) or non-necessary (la-darura). For example, in 'Every human is an animal', the relation is necessary because 'animal' is essential to 'human'.
This quality exists in reality (madda) regardless of how the proposition is expressed.
2
Check for Explicit Modality
Look at the proposition's wording. Does it include a word like 'necessarily' (bil-darura) or 'permanently' (bil-dawam)? If not, check whether the modality is mentally observed (mulahazatan) in a conceptual proposition. If neither, the modality is absent.
Explicit statement can be verbal (lafzan) or mental (mulahazatan).
3
Classify as Absolute or Modal
If the modality is not stated explicitly in wording or mental observation, the proposition is absolute (mutlaqa). If it is stated, it is modal (mujawwaha). Example: 'Every human is an animal' (absolute). 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (modal).
A modal proposition can be false if the stated modality does not match the actual quality.
4
Understand the Role of 'Established in Reality'
The passage clarifies that 'established in reality' (thabita fi nafs al-amr) does not mean the proposition is true, but that the wording indicates such establishment. This allows modal propositions to be evaluated regardless of truth.
This subtlety ensures both true and false statements can be analyzed.
5
Recognize the Terminology
Modal propositions are also called munawwa'a (diversified) or rubai'iyya (quadripartite). Absolute propositions that lack both modality and quantifier are called muhmala. Understanding these terms helps navigate further texts.
The quantifier is not considered a modality, so a proposition with quantifier but without modality is still absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قُلْنَا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، ونظرنا إلى نسبتها في الواقع؛ وجدناها ضروريَّةٌ، وإذا قلنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ كاتب؛ وجدنا نِسبتها اللاضرورية . فالضَّرورة واللاضرورة في المثالين هي كيفيَّةُ النِّسبةِ. ثمَّ تلك الكيفيَّةُ الثَّابتةُ في نفْس الأمر قد لا يُصرَّحُ بها لا لفظاً وَلَا ملاحظةً، وتخرج عن كونها موجهة. (قَوْلُهُ : قَدْ لَا يُصرَّحُ بِهَا) أي: قد لا تُعتبرُ لا لفظاً وَلَا مُلاحظةٌ، وتُسمَّى القضيَّةُ حينئذٍ : مُطلقةً، وذلكَ كَقولِكَ : كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان، أو كلُّ إنسانٍ كاتب، فَهَذِهِ مُطلقة عن الجهة، فَلَا تكونُ مُوجّهة. (العطار) تنحصر باعتبارٍ في الضَّرورةِ واللاضرورة، وتنحصر باعتبارٍ آخَرَ في الدَّوامِ واللادوام، إلَّا أَنَّهُ يُشكِلُ عليهِ قَولُه : إلى غير ذلك من الكيفياتِ)؛ فلا وجةً : لزيادتِهِ، وقد يعتذرُ عنه بأنَّ المرادَ مِنَ الضَّرورةِ واللاضرورة : المفهوم؛ لا الماصدقُ ، فَتناولت تلك الزيادة الإطلاق بأقسامِهِ؛ تَأَمَّلْ. (قَوْلُهُ : الثَّابِتَةُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ) لا بمعنى أنَّ مدلوله النِّسبةُ المتَّصفةُ بالثبوتِ في نفس الأمر؛ بل بمعنى أَنَّهُ يُفهم منه ثبوتُ تلك الكيفيَّةِ في نفس الأمر؛ سواء كانَتْ ثابتةً فيها أو لا، ومُحصِّلُهُ أنَّ ذلكَ التُّبوتَ مِن حيثُ مُجرَّدُ دلالةِ اللَّفْظِ ؛ سواءٌ كَانَ ذلكَ حَقًّا في نفس الأمر أم لا ; فَيتناول القضيَّةَ الصَّادقة والكاذبة بمطابقة الجهة للكيفيَّةِ وعدمِهَا ؛ لأنَّ مدلول اللفظ لا يجب أن يكون واقعاً؛ إذ الدَّلالة اللفظية قد تتخلَّف . (قَوْلُهُ: لَا لَفْظَاً) أي: حتّى تكونَ الجهة ملفوظةٌ، وَلَا ملاحظة؛ أي: حتّى تكون معقولةٌ لِمَا سيقولُ ; فإنْ كانَتِ القضيَّةُ مَلفوظةٌ ... إلخ، قال الجلالُ : فَتِلكَ. وقد يُصرَّح بها إمَّا لفظاً أو ملاحظة كما قالَ: (وَقَدْ يُصَرَّحُ بِكَيْفِيَّةِ النِّسْبَةِ ; فَمُوَجَّهَةٌ)؛ أي: فالقضيَّة موجّهةٌ . (الدسوقي) (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يُصَرَّحُ) مُرادُهُ بالتَّصريح : الاعتبار لأجل قوله: (أو مُلاحظة)، وقد للتقليل . (قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا لَفْظَاً) أي : في القضيَّةِ اللَّفظيَّةِ، وقولُهُ: (أو مُلاحظة)؛ أي: كَمَا في القضيَّة العقلية . وقوله : (وقد يُصرَّح ... إلخ)؛ أي: وتُسمَّى القضيَّةُ حينئذٍ مُوجّهة; لاشتمالها على الجهة . (العطار) اللَّفْظَ : الكيفيَّاتُ الثَّابتةُ في نفس الأمرِ تُسمَّى : مادَّةَ القضيَّة، والصورة المعقولة منها في القضيَّةِ المعقولة، واللَّفظُ الدَّالُ عليها في الملفوظةِ يُسمَّى : جهةٌ، فَإِنْ كَانَتِ القضيَّةُ خاليةً عنهما ; تُسمَّى مُهملةٌ .ا.هـ. فعلم أنَّ الشارح أرادَ بالتصريح: ما يشملُ التَّلفظَ كما في الملفوظة، والملاحظة كما في المعقولة، فيكون استعمل في معنى كُلّيّ صادق عليهِمَا مِن قَبيلِ عُموم المجاز، وهو الاعتبار؛ أي: لم تعتبر في اللفظ بأنْ يُصرّح بها، ولا في الملاحظة بأن يحكم بها العقل، وقال البعضُ : يحتملُ أنْ يكونَ المراد من قوله : (أو مُلاحظة: الجهة المعقولة، ويحتمل ـ وهو الأظهرُ ـ أن تكونَ الجهة الملفوظةُ : المحذوفة مِنَ اللَّفْظِ لقرينة، وَعلى كُلِّ ; فَتَسليطُ التّصريح عليه مشاكلة ا.هـ. وفيه أنَّ الدَّلالة على الجهةِ المحذوفة بالقرائن، وجعلُ القضيَّةِ مُوجّهةً باعتباره لا يُعوّلون عليه، واصطلاحهم يُخالفه، فإنَّ القولَ بالتَّقدير والحذفِ وأمثالهما مِنَ الاعتبارات اللفظية اصطلاح أهل العربيَّةِ، فَالأظهَرُ حذف الأظهر . (قَوْلُهُ : فَمُوَجَّهَةٌ) وتُسمَّى المنوَّعةَ والرُّباعيَّة أيضاً، قال صاحبُ المطالِع: ولمْ تُسمَّ باعتبارِ السُّورِ خُماسِيَّةٌ ; لأنَّ السُّورَ غيرُ لازم; بخلافِ الجهةِ، قالَهُ العصام .
If we say: 'Every human is an animal', and we look at its relation in reality, we find it necessary. If we say: 'Every human is a writer', we find its relation non-necessary. So necessity and non-necessity in the two examples are the quality of the relation. Then that quality established in reality may not be explicitly stated, neither in wording nor in observation, and it leaves being modal. (His statement: 'It may not be explicitly stated') means: it may not be considered, neither in wording nor in observation, and then the proposition is called 'absolute', such as your saying: 'Every human is an animal' or 'Every human is a writer'. These are absolute with respect to modality. (al-Attar) They are confined from one perspective to necessity and non-necessity, and confined from another perspective to permanence and non-permanence, except that his statement 'etc. of qualities' poses a problem; so there is no justification for his addition. It may be excused by saying that by necessity and non-necessity he means the concept, not the instances, so that addition includes the absolute with its divisions; consider. (His statement: 'established in reality') is not in the sense that its signification is the relation characterized by establishment in reality; rather, in the sense that it is understood from it the establishment of that quality in reality, whether it is established there or not. Its gist is that that establishment is in terms of mere indication of the word, whether that is true in reality or not, so it includes true and false propositions, matching or not matching the modality with the quality, because the signification of the word need not be actual, since verbal signification may fail. (His statement: 'neither in wording') means: so that the modality is not uttered, 'nor in observation' means: so that it is not conceptually considered. So if the proposition is uttered... etc. Then the same. And it may be explicitly stated, either in wording or in observation, as he said: 'And it may be explicitly stated as the quality of the relation, so it is modal'; i.e., so the proposition is modal. (al-Dasuqi) (His statement: 'And it may be explicitly stated') his intent by 'explicit statement' is consideration, because of his statement 'or observation', and 'may' is for paucity. (His statement: 'either in wording') means: in the verbal proposition, and his statement 'or observation' means: as in the mental proposition. (His statement: 'and it may be explicitly stated... etc.') means: and then the proposition is called modal because it includes the modality. (al-Attar) The established qualities in reality are called the 'matter' of the proposition, the conceptual form of them in the mental proposition, and the word indicating them in the verbal proposition is called 'modality'. If the proposition is free from both, it is called 'absolute'. Therefore, it is known that the commentator intended by 'explicit statement' what includes utterance as in the verbal proposition and observation as in the mental proposition, so he used it in a universal sense applying to both, as a kind of metaphorical extension, i.e., consideration; meaning: it is not considered in the wording by being explicitly stated, nor in the observation by being judged by the mind. Some said: it is possible that the meaning of his statement 'or observation' is the mental modality, and it is possible—and this is more apparent—that the verbal modality is the one deleted from the wording by a context. In any case, applying 'explicit statement' to it is a congenial usage. But it is adopted that indicating the deleted modality by evidence and making the proposition modal by virtue of it is not relied upon, and their convention contradicts it, for the usage of verbal estimation and deletion and the like are conventions of the grammarians, so the more apparent is to delete the more apparent. (His statement: 'so it is modal') And it is also called 'diversified' and 'quadripartite'. The author of al-Mutali' said: It is not called 'quinquepartite' by considering the quantifier, because the quantifier is not necessary, unlike the modality. Al-Isam said it.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the concept of modality (jihah) in categorical propositions. The author begins with two examples: 'Every human is an animal' (necessary relation) and 'Every human is a writer' (non-necessary relation). He explains that the quality of the relation (kayfiyya al-nisba) is either necessity (darura) or non-necessity (la-darura). This quality, even when established in reality (thabita fi nafs al-amr), may not be explicitly stated in the proposition—neither in wording (lafzan) nor in mental observation (mulahazatan). Such a proposition is called 'absolute' (mutlaqa), free from specification of modality. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: the quality is said to be confined to necessity and non-necessity, yet the text mentions 'etc. of qualities'. The rejoinder is that the terms 'necessity' and 'non-necessity' are taken conceptually (mafhum) rather than extensionally (masadaq), thus covering absolute and its divisions. Next, the passage clarifies that 'established in reality' does not mean the proposition has the quality in actuality; rather, the wording implies establishment regardless of truth. This distinction ensures that modal propositions can be false if the stated modality does not match the actual quality. The discussion then turns to explicit statement of modality: when the quality is explicitly stated (either in wording or in mental consideration), the proposition is called 'modal' (mujawwaha). The commentator distinguishes between verbal and mental modalities, noting that the explicit statement includes both. Finally, the modal proposition is also called 'diversified' (munawwa'a) or 'quadripartite' (rubai'iyya), as opposed to 'quinquepartite' which would include the quantifier—but the quantifier is not necessary for modality. This passage is important because it establishes the criteria for distinguishing absolute from modal propositions, a foundational concept in Islamic logic used in evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Key Terms

لا ضرورة
كيفية النسبة
جهة
مطلقة
موجهة
مادة القضية
مهملة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a modal (mujawwaha) proposition?
A) 'Every human is an animal' (without specifying necessity)
B) 'Every human is necessarily an animal'
C) 'Some human is a writer' (without specifying possibility)
D) 'No stone is a tree' (without specifying necessity)

Key Takeaway
A proposition is absolute when its modality is not explicitly stated; it becomes modal only when the quality of the relation is explicitly indicated, either in wording or in mental observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the proposition 'العالم حادث بالضرورة' (The world is necessarily temporally originated), determine whether it is absolute or modal. Explain your reasoning by identifying the explicit modality and the actual quality it indicates.
?
Task 2: Construct a modal proposition from a fiqh ruling, e.g., 'الزكاة واجبة على من ملك النصاب' (Zakat is obligatory on one who owns the nisab). Add an appropriate modality to make it modal, and justify your choice.

Definition of Modality (Jiha) in Propositions
Defines jiha as the modality specifying the quality of the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the modality (jiha) in a proposition and distinguish between verbal and mental modality.

Core Idea
The modality of a proposition is the quality of its relation, expressed either in words or in the mind.

In classical logic, every proposition not only asserts a relation between subject and predicate but also specifies the quality of that relation—whether it is necessary, possible, impossible, etc. This quality is called the jiha (modality). This passage defines jiha and explains how it manifests differently in verbal and mental propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'jiha' in the Arabic. The passage states that 'that by which clarification occurs' is jiha. This is the definiendum.
The term is introduced with (ما) i.e. 'that which'.
2
Extract the genus
The genus (jins) is 'that which obtains clarification of the quality' (ما يحصل به بيان الكيفية). This is the broader category: anything that clarifies the quality.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia (fasl) is the distinction between verbal and mental propositions. For a verbal proposition (qadiyya malfuza), the jiha is the actual word (e.g., 'necessarily'). For a mental proposition (qadiyya ma'qula), the jiha is the mind's judgment about the quality.
This differentia covers both modes of existence of a proposition.
4
Test the definition's completeness
Check whether the definition covers all instances: any proposition is either uttered or conceived. So the definition accounts for both cases. It also excludes non-propositions (e.g., commands) because they lack a truth-evaluable relation.
The definition is a complete real definition (hadd haqiqi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(ما)؛ أي: الَّذي يحصلُ بِهِ البَيَانُ؛ أي: بيانُ الكيفيَّةِ كالضَّرورةِ والاضرورةِ في المثالين المذكورين (جِهَةٌ) للقضيَّةِ، فَإِنْ كَانَتِ القضيَّةُ ملفوظةٌ، فجهَتُها لفظُ الضَّرورةِ والَّا ضرورة، وإن كانت معقولةٌ؛ فجهْتُهَا حكم العقل بأنَّ النِّسبةَ مكيّفةٌ بكيفية كذا.
And (ma) i.e. that by which clarification occurs, i.e. clarification of the quality, like necessity and non-necessity in the two mentioned examples, is a modality (jiha) of the proposition. If the proposition is verbal, its modality is the word of necessity and non-necessity; and if it is mental, its modality is the judgment of the mind that the relation is characterized by such a quality.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'jiha' (modality) in logic. The author explains that 'jiha' is that which clarifies the quality of the relation in a proposition, using examples of necessity and non-necessity. The definition distinguishes two cases: for a verbal proposition, the modality is the actual word (e.g., 'necessarily' or 'not necessarily'); for a mental proposition, the modality is the mind's judgment about the quality. Thus, modality can be either linguistic or conceptual, depending on whether the proposition is expressed or merely entertained in the mind.

قضية ملفوظة
قضية معقولة

Key Takeaway
The jiha of a proposition is the quality of its relation, expressed as a word in verbal propositions or as a mental judgment in mental propositions.

Classification of Modal Propositions: Simple vs Composite
Enumeration and classification of modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple from composite modal propositions and identify the absolute time-relative and absolute spread modalities.

Core Idea
Modal propositions vary; the key division is simple (single modality) vs composite (combined).

In Islamic logic, modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha) are classified in various ways. This passage from the commentary on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq examines how many modal propositions are typically studied and how they are categorized. The core distinction is between simple and composite modalities, and the author includes absolute time-relative and absolute spread as subtypes. Understanding this classification is foundational for analyzing modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main classifications
The passage gives two main categories: simple (basita) and composite (murakkaba). Note that according to Abu al-Fath, six are simple and seven are composite.
Simple means a single modality; composite means two or more combined.
2
Note the variation in number
The passage mentions that the commonly studied number is 13, but it can exceed 20, and the author specifies 15 by including absolute time-relative and absolute spread. This shows that the count is conventional, not fixed.
Why would numbers differ? Because scholars differ on which subtypes to include.
3
Understand the absolute subtypes
The author adds 'absolute time-relative' (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa) and 'absolute spread' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) as parts of time-relative and spread. These are special cases without additional restrictions.
Absolute time-relative: e.g., 'Zaid is standing at noon today' without any further condition.
4
Apply the classification to an example
Consider the proposition 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (al-insan hayawanun bi-d-darura). This is a simple modal proposition because it contains only the modality of necessity. If we said 'Every human is necessarily an animal and perpetually so', it would be composite.
Practice thinking of real Islamic theological arguments using modal propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القضايا الموجّهةُ التي يُبحَثُ عنها وعن أحكامها من العكس. قال أبو الفتح: المشهورُ أنَّ القضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادةُ بالبحث عنها: ثلاث عشرَ؛ سِةٌ مِنها بسائط، وسَبعٌ مُركَّبات، ولهم مُوجَّهاتٌ أُخرى يبحثون عنها على سبيل الندرة دون العادة، وارتقى عددُهَا إلى أكثر من عشرين على ما عدَّه المصنِّفُ وغيره، وأما الموجهات الغير المبحوث عنها؛ فهي غيرُ مَحصورةٍ في عددٍ والمصنِّفُ جعل الموجهاتِ المبحوث عنها هَهُنا خمسة عشر، وعدَّ منها الوقتيّة المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، اللَّتَيْنِ هُمَا جُزاً الوقتية والمنتشرة، والأمرُ في ذلكَ هَيِّنٌ.
Then the modal propositions which are studied regarding their rules of conversion. Abu al-Fath said: The well-known modal propositions that are customarily studied are thirteen: six of them simple, seven composite. They have other modalities which they study rarely, not customarily, and their number reaches more than twenty according to what the author and others count. As for the modalities not studied, they are unlimited in number. The author here made the modalities studied fifteen, and included among them the absolute time-relative and the absolute spread, which are parts of the time-relative and the spread. The matter is easy.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage enumerates the modal propositions that are typically studied in logic. The commentator cites Abu al-Fath stating that the common practice is to study thirteen modal propositions: six simple and seven composite. There are additional modalities studied rarely, and the total can exceed twenty. The author (likely of the matn) specifies fifteen modalities, including the 'absolute time-relative' and 'absolute spread' as subtypes. The passage concludes by downplaying the significance of the exact count, indicating it is a matter of convention.

بسيطة
مركبة
الوقتية المطلقة
المنتشرة المطلقة

B) Thirteen
C) Twenty
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The number of studied modal propositions is conventional, but the distinction between simple and composite is fundamental.

Definition of Jiha (Modality) in Propositions
Definition of jiha as clarifying quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jiha and identify its two forms (uttered and intellectual) with examples from Arabic propositions.

Core Idea
Jiha is the modality that qualifies the relation, whether expressed in words or judged in the mind.

Before we can analyze modal propositions in Islamic logic, we must understand what jiha (modality) is. The base text defines it as that by which the quality of the relation is clarified, and distinguishes between uttered and intellectual modalities. This distinction is essential for correctly interpreting modal claims in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the term 'jiha' in the Arabic passage. Recognize that it is what is being defined by the preceding phrase 'ma yahsulu bihi al-bayan'.
The definiendum is at the end of the first sentence.
2
Identify the function of jiha
The passage states that jiha is that by which clarification of the quality (al-kayfiyya) is obtained. The quality is specifically 'necessity' (darura) or 'non-necessity' (la darura).
These are the two basic modalities.
3
Distinguish the two manifestations
Notice the conditional: if the proposition is uttered (malftuza), the jiha is the word of necessity/non-necessity; if intellectual (ma'qula), the jiha is the mind's judgment (hukm al-'aql) that the relation is qualified.
This shows jiha is not confined to language.
4
Connect to modal examples
Consider the example 'The world is necessarily originated' (al-alam hadithun bid-darura). Its jiha as an uttered proposition is the word 'necessarily'; as a mental proposition, it is the mind's judgment of necessity.
This will be used in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(ما)؛ أي: الَّذي يحصلُ بِهِ البَيَانُ ؛ أي : بيانُ الكيفيَّةِ كالضَّرورةِ واللاضرورةِ في المثالين المذكورين (جِهَةٌ) للقضيَّةِ، فَإِنْ كَانَتِ القضيَّةُ ملفوظةٌ، فجهَتُها لفظُ الضَّرورةِ واللَّا ضرورة، وإن كانت معقولةٌ ؛ فجهْتُهَا حكم العقل بأنَّ النِّسبةَ مكيّفةٌ بكيفية كذا .
الدسوقي
ثم القضايا الموجّهةُ التي يُبحَثُ عنها وعن أحكامها من العكس (قَوْلُهُ : كَالضَّرُوْرَةِ) أي : أَوْ مَا يقوم مقامها كَقَطعاً، وقوله: (وَاللاضرورة)، أو ما يقومُ مَقامَها كَليس بلازم كذا
(قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْقُوْلَةٌ) أي : بأَنْ جَرَتِ القضيَّةُ فِي الذَّهنِ دونَ تَلفُظ بها
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَي الَّذِي يَحْصَلُ ... إلخ) لم يجعل ما واقعةً على لفظ؛ لقصوره على الجهة الملفوظة، فيكونُ البيانُ قاصِراً، وحينئذ ؛ فالمرادُ بِمَا يحصلُ بِهِ البيانُ : مَا يتناولُ حكم العقل واللفظ ، والبيانُ في اللفظ ظاهر، وأما في حكم العقلِ فَكَذلِكَ لأنَّ حُكْمَ العقل عبارةٌ عن الصُّورةِ الذَّهنيَّةِ ، والصُّوَرُ الذهنيَّةُ دالَّةٌ عَلى مَا في نفسِ الأمر.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَجِهَتُهَا حُكْمُ الْعَقْل) اعترضَهُ شارح القسطاس بأن جعل حكم العقل جهةً مسامحة، والجهة هي الكيفية المعقولة المحكوم بِهَا عند العقل، ولكن في شرح المطالع والمفتاح وغيرهما : أنَّ الجهة هي حُكْمُ العقل المذكور، قاله العصام.
And (ma) is that by which clarification is obtained, i.e., clarification of the quality, like necessity and non-necessity in the two mentioned examples, is the modality of the proposition. So if the proposition is uttered, its modality is the word of necessity or non-necessity; and if it is intellectual, its modality is the judgment of the mind that the relation is qualified with such a quality. Al-Dasuqi: Then the modal propositions that are investigated with respect to their rulings from conversion: his statement: 'like necessity,' i.e., or what takes its place like 'certainly,' and his statement: 'and non-necessity,' or what takes its place like 'not necessarily such.' His statement: 'and if it is intellectual,' i.e., that the proposition proceeds in the mind without utterance. Al-Attar: His statement: 'that by which clarification is obtained,' etc.: He did not make 'ma' refer to the word, because it would be limited to uttered modality, so the clarification would be deficient. Therefore, what is meant by 'that by which clarification is obtained' is that which encompasses the judgment of the mind and the word. Clarification in the word is apparent, and similarly in the judgment of the mind, because the judgment of the mind is the mental form, and mental forms indicate what is in the mind itself. His statement: 'so its modality is the judgment of the mind.' The commentator of al-Qistas objected that making the judgment of the mind a modality is merely metaphorical; the modality is the intellectual quality of which the mind judges. But in Sharh al-Matali' and al-Miftah and others, the modality is that very judgment of the mind, as al-Isam said.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'jiha' (modality) in the context of modal propositions. The commentators explain that jiha is that by which the quality of the relation—necessity or non-necessity—is clarified. For an uttered proposition, the jiha is the word expressing necessity (like 'necessarily') or non-necessity (like 'not necessarily'). For a mental proposition, the jiha is the mind's judgment that the relation possesses a certain quality. Al-Dasuqi then clarifies that 'necessity' and 'non-necessity' can be replaced by equivalent expressions. Al-Attar deepens the analysis by noting that the word 'ma' in the base text does not refer only to the uttered word, because that would restrict modality to uttered propositions; rather, 'ma' includes both mental and verbal aspects. He also addresses an objection that calling the mind's judgment a 'jiha' is loose language, but confirms that in standard works like Sharh al-Matali' and al-Miftah, the jiha is indeed identified as that judgment.

ضرورة
لا ضرورة
حكم العقل

Jiha is the modality that clarifies the quality of the relation in a proposition, and it manifests as either an uttered word or a mental judgment.

Commentators on Jiha: Uttered and Mental Aspects
Commentators refine jiha to include mental judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how al-Attar expands the definition of jiha to include both uttered and mental judgments, and evaluate the objection that this is merely metaphorical.

Core Idea
Jiha encompasses both the uttered word and the mind's judgment, as confirmed by major commentaries.

The base definition of jiha required further clarification: does 'that by which clarification is obtained' refer only to words? Al-Attar and later commentators argue that it includes the mental judgment as well. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and addresses an objection from Sharh al-Qistas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base claim
The base text states that jiha is 'that by which clarification is obtained'. Some might think this refers only to the uttered word, since 'bayan' often means verbal clarification.
Al-Attar corrects this misunderstanding.
2
Follow al-Attar's reasoning
Al-Attar argues that 'ma' is not limited to the word; it must encompass both the word and the mind's judgment. Otherwise, the definition would be deficient for intellectual propositions.
He says: 'the clarification in the word is apparent, and similarly in the judgment of the mind' because mental forms indicate what is in the mind.
3
Analyze the objection
The commentator of al-Qistas objects that calling the mind's judgment a 'jiha' is metaphorical (musamaha). The true jiha is the intellectual quality itself, not the judgment.
This is a subtle point about what exactly the modality is.
4
Resolve with standard works
Al-Attar counters by citing Sharh al-Matali', al-Miftah, and al-Isam: in those works, the jiha is explicitly defined as the mind's judgment. Thus, the objection is overruled.
This shows the mainstream position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(ما)؛ أي: الَّذي يحصلُ بِهِ البَيَانُ ؛ أي : بيانُ الكيفيَّةِ كالضَّرورةِ واللاضرورةِ في المثالين المذكورين (جِهَةٌ) للقضيَّةِ، فَإِنْ كَانَتِ القضيَّةُ ملفوظةٌ، فجهَتُها لفظُ الضَّرورةِ واللَّا ضرورة، وإن كانت معقولةٌ ؛ فجهْتُهَا حكم العقل بأنَّ النِّسبةَ مكيّفةٌ بكيفية كذا .
الدسوقي
ثم القضايا الموجّهةُ التي يُبحَثُ عنها وعن أحكامها من العكس (قَوْلُهُ : كَالضَّرُوْرَةِ) أي : أَوْ مَا يقوم مقامها كَقَطعاً، وقوله: (وَاللاضرورة)، أو ما يقومُ مَقامَها كَليس بلازم كذا
(قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْقُوْلَةٌ) أي : بأَنْ جَرَتِ القضيَّةُ فِي الذَّهنِ دونَ تَلفُظ بها
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَي الَّذِي يَحْصَلُ ... إلخ) لم يجعل ما واقعةً على لفظ؛ لقصوره على الجهة الملفوظة، فيكونُ البيانُ قاصِراً، وحينئذ ؛ فالمرادُ بِمَا يحصلُ بِهِ البيانُ : مَا يتناولُ حكم العقل واللفظ ، والبيانُ في اللفظ ظاهر، وأما في حكم العقلِ فَكَذلِكَ لأنَّ حُكْمَ العقل عبارةٌ عن الصُّورةِ الذَّهنيَّةِ ، والصُّوَرُ الذهنيَّةُ دالَّةٌ عَلى مَا في نفسِ الأمر.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَجِهَتُهَا حُكْمُ الْعَقْل) اعترضَهُ شارح القسطاس بأن جعل حكم العقل جهةً مسامحة، والجهة هي الكيفية المعقولة المحكوم بِهَا عند العقل، ولكن في شرح المطالع والمفتاح وغيرهما : أنَّ الجهة هي حُكْمُ العقل المذكور، قاله العصام.
And (ma) is that by which clarification is obtained, i.e., clarification of the quality, like necessity and non-necessity in the two mentioned examples, is the modality of the proposition. So if the proposition is uttered, its modality is the word of necessity or non-necessity; and if it is intellectual, its modality is the judgment of the mind that the relation is qualified with such a quality. Al-Dasuqi: Then the modal propositions that are investigated with respect to their rulings from conversion: his statement: 'like necessity,' i.e., or what takes its place like 'certainly,' and his statement: 'and non-necessity,' or what takes its place like 'not necessarily such.' His statement: 'and if it is intellectual,' i.e., that the proposition proceeds in the mind without utterance. Al-Attar: His statement: 'that by which clarification is obtained,' etc.: He did not make 'ma' refer to the word, because it would be limited to uttered modality, so the clarification would be deficient. Therefore, what is meant by 'that by which clarification is obtained' is that which encompasses the judgment of the mind and the word. Clarification in the word is apparent, and similarly in the judgment of the mind, because the judgment of the mind is the mental form, and mental forms indicate what is in the mind itself. His statement: 'so its modality is the judgment of the mind.' The commentator of al-Qistas objected that making the judgment of the mind a modality is merely metaphorical; the modality is the intellectual quality of which the mind judges. But in Sharh al-Matali' and al-Miftah and others, the modality is that very judgment of the mind, as al-Isam said.

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'jiha' (modality) in the context of modal propositions. The commentators explain that jiha is that by which the quality of the relation—necessity or non-necessity—is clarified. For an uttered proposition, the jiha is the word expressing necessity (like 'necessarily') or non-necessity (like 'not necessarily'). For a mental proposition, the jiha is the mind's judgment that the relation possesses a certain quality. Al-Dasuqi then clarifies that 'necessity' and 'non-necessity' can be replaced by equivalent expressions. Al-Attar deepens the analysis by noting that the word 'ma' in the base text does not refer only to the uttered word, because that would restrict modality to uttered propositions; rather, 'ma' includes both mental and verbal aspects. He also addresses an objection that calling the mind's judgment a 'jiha' is loose language, but confirms that in standard works like Sharh al-Matali' and al-Miftah, the jiha is indeed identified as that judgment.

Enumeration and Division of Modal Propositions
Author enumerates fifteen modal propositions and divides them into simple and composite types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the fifteen modal propositions and classify each as simple or composite based on the definition provided.

Core Idea
Fifteen modals, eight simple and seven composite: simple means wholly affirmative or wholly negative.

This passage from the Tadhheeb Sharh presents the standard enumeration of modal propositions in Islamic logic. The commentators clarify the count (fifteen) and the division into simple and composite, linking it to the definition of modality as judgment of the mind. Understanding this classification is essential for later discussions on conversion and contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total number of modal propositions mentioned
The author states that the investigated modal propositions are fifteen. Look for the explicit mention of 'خمسة عشر' in the Arabic.
The number is given in the section heading and repeated by al-Dasuqi.
2
Locate the division into simple and composite
The passage says 'منها بسيطة' (of them simple) and then defines simple as either affirmation only or negation only. The remainder are composite.
Al-Dasuqi clarifies that eight are simple and seven are composite.
3
Understand the definition of simple
Simple modal propositions are those whose meaning is either wholly affirmative or wholly negative. Al-Attar raises an objection: in a negative necessary proposition like 'No human is a stone of necessity,' there is both a negative content and an affirmative modal judgment, making it composite.
This objection shows that the definition must be applied carefully.
4
Note the additional modals mentioned by some
Al-Dasuqi adds that some scholars include four more modals (al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina, al-ḥīniyya al-muṭlaqa, al-mumkina al-dā'ima, al-mumkina al-waqtiyya), raising the total to nineteen.
These will be discussed later in the context of contradiction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القضايا الموجهة التي يُحث عنها وعن أحكامها من العكس (قوله : كالضرورة) أي: أو ما يقوم مقامها كقطعاً، وقوله: (واللاضرورة)، أو ما يقوم مقامها كليس بلازم كذا
(قوله: وإن كانت معقولة) أي: بأن جرت القضية في الذهن دون تلفظ بها
العطار
(قوله: أَيّ الَّذِي يَحْصَلُ ... إلخ) لم يجعل ما واقعةً على لفظ؛ لقصوره على الجهة الملفوظة، فيكون البيان قاصراً، وحينئذ فالمراد بما يحصل به البيان: ما يتناول حكم العقل واللفظ، والبيان في اللفظ ظاهر، وأما في حكم العقل فكذلك لأن حكم العقل عبارة عن الصورة الذهنية، والصوَر الذهنية دالة على ما في نفس الأمر.
(قوله: فجهتها حكم العقل) اعترضه شارح القسطاس بأن جعل حكم العقل جهة مسامحة، والجهة هي الكيفية المعقولة المحكوم بها عند العقل، ولكن في شرح المطالع والمفتاح وغيرهما: أن الجهة هي حكم العقل المذكور، قاله العصام.
(قوله: التي يبحَثُ عنها) أي: تذكر أحكامها، قال أبو الفتح: المشهور أن القضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها: ثلاث عشرَ؛ ستة منها بسائط، وسبع مركبات، ولهم موجهات أُخرى يبحثون عنها على سبيل الندرة دون العادة، وارتقى عددها إلى أكثر من عشرين على ما عدّه المصنِّف وغيره، وأما الموجهات الغير المبحوث عنها فهي غير محصورة في عدد. والمصنِّف جعل الموجهات المبحوث عنها ههنا خمسة عشر، وعدّ منها الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، اللتين هما جزءا الوقتية والمنتشرة، والأمر في ذلك هيّن.
[فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ]
والتناقض خمسة عشر:
[تقسِيمُ المُوجَّهَةِ إِلَى بَسِيطَةٍ وَمُرَكَّبَةٍ]
وهي التي يكون معناها: إمَّا إيجاباً فقط، أو سلباً فقط؛ منها بسيطة: وهي
الدسوقي
(قوله: خمسة عشر) المناسب: خمس عشرة، وزاد بعضهم: أربعة وهي: الحينية الممكنة، والحينية المطلقة، والممكنة الدائمة، والممكنة الوقتية. وستأتي هذه الأربعة في التناقض، فالجملة حينئذ: تسعة عشر.
(قوله: منها بسيطة) أي: وهي ثمانية، والباقي وهو: سبعة مركبة.
(قوله: إما إيجاباً فقط) أي: ذا إيجاب؛ أي: إما نسبة ذات إيجاب؛ هذا إذا كان مدلول القضية النسبة. وأما على القول بأن مدلولها إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أو لا، فالمراد من الإيجاب: إدراك أنها واقعة ومن السلب: إدراك أنها ليست بواقعة.
بقوله: وقد أشار ابن مرزوق في نظمه لجمل الخونجي لضابط البسيط منها والمركب
العطار
(قوله: خمسة عشر) لا يخفى أن المعدود هنا مؤنثة، وهو قضية، فكان يجب تجريد خمسة من التاء؛ لأنها تجري على خلاف القياس، ويجب إلحاق التاء لعشرة؛ لأنها عند التركيب تجري على القياس، وقد يوجه إلحاق التاء بخمسة هنا؛ بأن المعدود محذوف ومحل مخالفة القياس إذا ذكر المعدود.
(قوله: أو سلباً فقط) أورد عليه أنا إذا قلنا في السالبة الضرورية: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة مثلاً؛ تحقق قضيتان؛ سالبة: هي لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، وموجبة: هي أن هذه النسبة السلبية ضرورية، فيختل التعريفان طرداً.
Then the modal propositions that are investigated with respect to their rulings from conversion: his statement: 'like necessity,' i.e., or what takes its place like 'certainly'; and his statement: 'and non-necessity,' or what takes its place like 'not necessarily such.' His statement: 'and if it is intellectual,' i.e., that the proposition proceeds in the mind without utterance. Al-Attar: His statement: 'that by which clarification is obtained,' etc.: why he did not make 'ma' refer to the word, because it would be limited to uttered modality, so the clarification would be deficient. Therefore, what is meant by 'that by which clarification is obtained' is that which encompasses the judgment of the mind and the word. The clarification in the word is apparent, and similarly in the judgment of the mind, because the judgment of the mind is the mental form, and mental forms indicate what is in the mind itself. His statement: 'so its modality is the judgment of the mind.' The commentator of al-Qistas objected that making the judgment of the mind a modality is merely metaphorical; the modality is the intellectual quality of which the mind judges. But in Sharh al-Matali' and al-Miftah and others, the modality is that very judgment of the mind, as al-Isam said. His statement: 'which are investigated,' i.e., their rulings are mentioned. Abu al-Fath said: The well-known (modal propositions) that are customarily investigated are thirteen: six of them simple, seven composite. They have other modals that they investigate on rare occasions, not customarily. Their number has risen to more than twenty as counted by the author and others. As for the non-investigated modals, they are not confined to a number. The author here made the investigated modals fifteen, and included among them the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa) and the absolute spread (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), which are parts of the temporal and the spread. The matter is easy. [Section: On the definition and its divisions] And contradiction (tanāquḍ) is fifteen: [Division of the modal into simple and composite] And it is that whose meaning is either affirmation only, or negation only. Of them, simple: and they are... Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'fifteen' — the appropriate (form) is 'fifteen' (with feminine agreement). Some added four: the possible durational, the absolute durational, the possible perpetual, and the possible temporal. These four will come in contradiction; then the total is nineteen. His statement: 'of them simple' i.e., they are eight, the remaining seven are composite. His statement: 'either affirmation only' i.e., possessing affirmation; i.e., a relation with affirmation; this if the import of the proposition is the relation. On the view that its import is the awareness that the relation exists or not, then the meaning of affirmation is the awareness that it exists, and of negation is the awareness that it does not exist. He said: Ibn Marzuq in his versification of the summaries of al-Khunaji indicated the rule for the simple and composite. Al-Attar: His statement: 'fifteen' — it is not hidden that the counted noun here is feminine, i.e., 'qadiyya', so it was necessary to drop the tā' from 'five' because it deviates from the norm, and it is necessary to attach the tā' to 'ten' because it follows the norm in compound. It may be justified to attach the tā' to 'five' here by considering the counted noun omitted and the place of deviation from the norm when the counted noun is mentioned. His statement: 'or negation only' — it is objected to it that if we say in the necessary negative: 'No human is a stone of necessity' for example, two propositions are realized: a negative one, which is 'No human is a stone', and a positive one, which is 'this negative relation is necessary'. So the two definitions would be vitiated per se (tardan).
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and taxonomic division of modal propositions. The commentators first clarify that the definition of modality applies to both uttered and mental judgments, referencing earlier discussions. Then they turn to the list of modal propositions. According to Abu al-Fath, the commonly studied modals are thirteen: six simple and seven composite. However, the author of this commentary (the muṣannif) counts fifteen, including two absolute types. Some scholars add four more, raising the total to nineteen. The passage then introduces a new section 'On the definition and its divisions' but quickly ties it to contradiction and the division of modals into simple and composite. Al-Dasuqi explains the expected feminine form of 'fifteen' and lists the added four: al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina, al-ḥīniyya al-muṭlaqa, al-mumkina al-dā'ima, and al-mumkina al-waqtiyya. He also notes that simple modals are eight, composite are seven. Al-Attar elaborates on the grammatical point and addresses an objection: when negating with necessity, the definition of simple as 'affirmation only or negation only' fails because the negative proposition 'No human is a stone' involves both a negative content and an affirmative modal judgment. The passage is dense with grammatical and logical details.

موجَّهة
بسيطة
مركَّبة
الوقتية المطلقة
المنتشرة المطلقة

The Number of Contradictory Pairs of Modal Propositions
Enumerating contradictory pairs and the addition of four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the total number of contradictory pairs for modal propositions and identify the additional four types added by some scholars.

Core Idea
Modal contradictions: 15 base pairs, 4 added make 19.

The author begins by stating that there are fifteen contradictory pairs for modal propositions. He then notes a grammatical correction and mentions that some scholars add four more types, bringing the total to nineteen. This enumeration is foundational for understanding how modal propositions are used in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Find the base claim
Locate the statement that contradiction is 'fifteen' (خمسة عشر). Note that the author indicates the grammatically correct form is 'khams 'ashara' (feminine).
The passage is a commentary on the base text.
2
Identify the added four
Find the four additional types: al-hayniyya al-mumkina, al-hayniyya al-mutlaqa, al-mumkina al-da'ima, al-mumkina al-waqtiyya. These are modal propositions with specific temporal or possibility qualifications.
These four are said to appear later in the section on contradiction.
3
Calculate the total
Add the four to the original fifteen to get nineteen. This is the total number of contradictory pairs according to some scholars.
The commentator explains this addition explicitly.
4
Understand the division
Notice that the nineteen pairs are then divided into simple (8) and compound (7) based on their meaning. This division is explained in the next part of the passage.
The simple and compound refer to the modal propositions themselves, not the pairs.
5
Apply to Islamic reasoning
Think of a modal argument in kalam, e.g., 'The world is necessarily temporally originated' vs. 'The world is not necessarily temporally originated'. This is one of the 15 pairs. The added four would involve combinations with temporal conditions like 'always possible' or 'sometimes possible'.
The tool helps ensure you don't miss a valid contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتناقض خمسة عشر: [تقسيمُ المَوَجَّهةِ إلى بسيطة، ومركَّبَةٍ] وهي التي يكون معناها : إمَّا إيجاباً فقط، أو سلباً فقط منها بسيطة : وهي (قَوْلُهُ : خَمْسَةَ عَشَرَ) المناسب : خمس عشرة، وزادَ بعضُهم : أربعة وهي : الحينيَّةُ الممكنة، والحينيَّةُ المطلقة، والممكنة الدائمة، والممكنة الوقتيّة . وستأتي هذه الأربعةُ في التَّناقض (۱) ، فالجملة حينئذ : تسعة عشر . (قَوْلُهُ : مِنْهَا بَسِيْطَةٌ) أي : وهي ثمانية، والباقي وهو : سبعةٌ مُركَّبة. (قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا إِيْجَابَاً فَقَطْ) أي : ذا إيجاب؛ أي: إما نسبةٌ ذاتُ إيجاب؛ هذا إذا كانَ مَدلول القضيَّةِ النِّسبة. وأمَّا على القول (۲) بأنَّ مدلولها إدراك أنَّ النِّسبة واقعةٌ أَوْ لَا ، فَالمرادُ مِنَ الإيجاب: إدراك أنَّها واقعةٌ وَمِنَ السَّلْبِ : إدراكُ أَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ بواقعة. ومنها مركبة : وهي التي معناها : مركب من إيجاب وسَلْبٍ.
And contradiction is fifteen: [Division of the modal into simple and compound]. The simple is that whose meaning is either affirmation only or negation only. From them, simple: (His statement: 'fifteen') the appropriate is 'khams 'ashara', and some added four: al-hayniyya al-mumkina, al-hayniyya al-mutlaqa, al-mumkina al-da'ima, and al-mumkina al-waqtiyya. These four will come in the contradiction, so the total then is nineteen. (His statement: 'from them simple') i.e. they are eight, and the remaining are seven compound. (His statement: 'either affirmation only') i.e. possessing affirmation; i.e. either a relation possessing affirmation; this if the meaning of the proposition is the relation. But according to the view that its meaning is the perception that the relation occurs or not, then the intended by affirmation is the perception that it occurs, and by negation is the perception that it does not occur. And from them compound: which is that whose meaning is composed of affirmation and negation.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage defines the types of modal propositions by their contradiction. It first states that there are fifteen contradictory pairs. Then it classifies modals into simple (having one modality, either affirmative or negative) and compound (having both). The commentator notes that the number should be feminine, and that some add four types making nineteen. He explains that simple propositions are eight and compound seven. He then clarifies the meaning of 'simple' in terms of the logical content: either a relational content (the relation itself) or the judgment of the relation's occurrence. The example from Islamic logic: the simple affirmative modal 'Every human is necessarily an animal' vs. the simple negative 'No human is necessarily a stone'. The compound modal would be like 'Every human is possibly not a stone'? No, compound has both affirmation and negation in its meaning, likely referring to a proposition that includes both modalities, e.g., 'Every human is necessarily not a stone' combines affirmation of the negative? Actually, the text defines compound as meaning composed of affirmation and negation, which is a single proposition that contains both aspects, possibly like 'Every human is possibly not an animal'? But the exact example is not given; the point is the classification.

Simple and Compound Modal Propositions
Defining simple and compound modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a simple (basīṭa) and a compound (murakkaba) modal proposition based on its meaning and identify the two interpretations of that meaning.

Core Idea
Simple modal: meaning is only affirmative or only negative. Compound modal: meaning mixes both.

After enumerating the contradictory pairs, the author divides modal propositions into simple and compound. A simple modal has a meaning that is either entirely affirmative or entirely negative. A compound modal has a meaning composed of both affirmation and negation. The commentator also clarifies two views on what 'meaning' refers to: the relation itself or the perception of the relation. This classification is key to understanding how modal propositions function in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of simple
Find the statement: 'the simple is that whose meaning is either affirmation only or negation only.' Underline that this applies to the meaning of the proposition, not its wording.
2
Understand the two interpretations of meaning
The commentator provides two views: (1) the meaning is the relation between subject and predicate (e.g., 'is' or 'is not'); (2) the meaning is the perception (i.e., judgment) that the relation occurs or not. Both lead to the same classification of simple and compound.
These two interpretations are standard in Islamic logic (relation vs. mental judgment).
3
Find the definition of compound
Locate 'compound is that whose meaning is composed of affirmation and negation.' This means the proposition includes both a positive and a negative aspect in its content.
An example would be: 'It is not the case that every human is necessarily an animal'? Actually, that is simple negation. A true compound might be something like 'Every human is possibly a stone and not possibly a stone'? That is contradictory. The exact nature of compound modals is debated; the text simply defines it as containing both affirmation and negation.
4
Apply the numbers
The commentator states that there are eight simple modals and seven compound modals among the total pairs. This shows that most are simple.
The numbers 8 and 7 sum to 15 (or 19 if the additional four are included).
5
Test a sample
Take a modal proposition like 'The world is necessarily temporally originated.' Determine: is its meaning purely affirmative (affirming the relation of temporality) — yes, so it is simple. Then try 'The world is not necessarily eternal' — this is negative, so also simple. Can you think of a compound modal?

Source Text

The Absolute Necessary Proposition (الضرورية المطلقة)
Definition of the absolute necessary proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute necessary proposition and distinguish it from the eternal necessary proposition using the condition of the subject's existence.

Core Idea
The absolute necessary proposition's necessity is conditional on the subject's existence.

This lesson introduces the first of eight simple modal propositions: al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa (الضرورية المطلقة). Understanding this modal is essential for evaluating necessity claims in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where distinguishing between conditional and eternal necessity often determines the validity of arguments about God's attributes or rulings on created beings.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Identify what is being defined: the absolute necessary proposition (الضرورية المطلقة). It is the first of the eight simple modals mentioned by the author.
The passage begins by stating that the simples are eight, then defines the first.
2
Extract the condition of necessity
The definition states that the judgment is necessary 'as long as the subject's essence exists' (ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة). This condition restricts the necessity to the duration of the subject's existence.
This is the key differentia separating it from the eternal necessary.
3
Contrast with the eternal necessary
The eternal necessary (الضرورية الأزلية) holds regardless of temporal conditions. The absolute necessary applies only while the subject exists. For example, 'Every human is rational' is necessary only as long as humans exist.
In al-Isharat, the eternal necessary is contrasted with essential necessity.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Test the definition: 'Every human is a rational animal' is an absolute necessary proposition because rationality is necessary for humans as long as humans exist. If humans ceased to exist, the proposition would not be false, but its necessity would not apply.
For God, the subject (God) is eternal, so the condition is always met, making the proposition eternal necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسامُ المَوَجَّهَةِ البسيطة. أمَّا البسائط فثمان كما أشار إلى تعدادها وتعريفها بقوله: (فَإِنْ كَانَ الحُكْم) في القضيَّة (بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ الْإِيْجَابِيَّةِ أو السَّلبيَّةِ مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ المَوْضُوع) موجودة؛
Sections of the simple modal proposition. As for the simples, they are eight, as he indicated to their enumeration and definition by his saying: 'For if the judgment in the proposition is by necessity of the affirmative or negative relation as long as the subject's essence exists.'
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of simple modal proposition: the absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة). The author first states that simple modals are eight, then gives the definition. The definition states that the judgment in the proposition must affirm or deny a predicate of the subject with necessity, but this necessity is conditional: it holds only as long as the subject's essence exists. This means that the necessity is tied to the duration of the subject's existence, not necessarily eternal. For example, the proposition 'Every human is rational' is necessary as long as humans exist, but if humans cease to exist, the necessity may no longer apply. This distinguishes it from the eternal necessary (الضرورية الأزلية) which holds at all times. The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar explains nuances, such as the issue with God's attributes where the subject (God) is eternal, so the condition 'as long as the subject exists' is always true, leading to the eternal necessary. This passage is foundational for understanding modal propositions in logic.

Key Terms

الموجَّهة

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition's necessity is conditional on the existence of the subject's essence.

Understanding Necessary and Absolute Propositions
Author defines ضرورية and مطلقة propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary (ضرورية) and absolute (مطلقة) propositions and identify an example of each from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Necessary and absolute propositions are two names for the same thing: an essential, unconditional judgement.

This passage introduces two key terms in Islamic logic: ضرورية (necessary) and مطلقة (absolute). The author explains that they are called 'necessary' because they contain necessity, and 'absolute' because the judgement is not restricted by any additional description or time. The example given—'Every human is an animal necessarily'—illustrates a proposition whose truth is inherent and constant as long as the subject's essence exists. Understanding these foundational terms is crucial for analyzing more complex propositions in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms and their etymological reasons
The passage states that 'necessary' (ضرورية) is named for containing necessity (الضرورة), and 'absolute' (مطلقة) is named because the judgement is unrestricted by any description or time. Note the parallel: both refer to the same type of proposition from different angles.
2
Analyze the condition 'unrestricted by description or time'
The author specifies that the judgement is not tied to a specific attribute (وصف) or temporal frame (وقت). This means the truth of the proposition does not depend on any transient condition; it is constant as long as the subject's essence exists.
This distinguishes absolute propositions from conditional or temporal ones.
3
Examine the examples given
Two examples are provided: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' (affirmation) and 'No human is a stone necessarily' (negation). Both are claimed to be necessary as long as the human essence exists. Reflect on why these are essential—animality is part of human essence, and stonehood is incompatible.
The examples show that both affirmative and negative universal propositions can be necessary.
4
Identify the role of essence (ذات)
The necessity is tied to the essence (ذات) of the subject. As long as the essence exists, the judgement holds. This implies that the necessity is not temporal but ontological—it follows from what the subject is.
This is a key metaphysical commitment: essential predicates are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ إنَّما سميت ضروريَّةٌ ؛ لاشتمالها على الضَّرورة، وإنَّما سمّيت مطلقةً ؛ لأنَّ الحكم فيها غيرُ مقيَّد بوصف أو وقتٍ كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، فإنَّ ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان وسلب الحجريَّة عنه ضروري ما دام ذاتُ الإنسان موجودةً.
Chapter: On the definition and its divisions. They were called necessary because they contain necessity, and they were called absolute because the judgement in them is not restricted by a description or time, as we say: 'Every human is an animal necessarily', and 'No human is a stone necessarily'. For the affirmation of animality for human and the negation of stonehood from it are necessary as long as the essence of human exists.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage defines two types of propositions: ضرورية (necessary) and مطلقة (absolute). The author explains that they are called 'necessary' because they include the concept of necessity in the judgement. They are also called 'absolute' because the judgement is not tied to any extra condition (وصف) or time (وقت). The example given is the relationship between human and animal: it is necessarily true that every human is an animal, and necessarily false that any human is a stone, as long as the human essence exists. This illustrates that the necessity is inherent, not conditional.

Key Terms
ضرورية
مطلقة
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A necessary proposition is one whose truth is inherent and unconditional, and it is also called absolute because it lacks extra restrictions.
