The General Conditioned Proposition
Author defines the general conditioned proposition with condition on description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general conditioned proposition and distinguish its condition from absolute necessity.

Core Idea
A general conditioned proposition binds necessity to the subject's attribute, not its essence.

In this passage, the author defines the second type of necessary proposition: the general conditioned. Unlike the absolute necessary that holds as long as the subject exists, this proposition's necessity depends on a description (attribute) of the subject. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the scope of claims in Islamic logic and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase 'as long as its description'
In the Arabic, 'ما دام وصفه' is a conjunction to 'as long as the essence of the subject'. Recognize that the condition here is tied to the subject's description, not its essence.
The description (وصف) is an attribute like 'writer' or 'speaker'.
2
Understand the condition
The necessity of the proposition holds only as long as the description exists. For example, 'Every writer moves his fingers' is only necessary while he is a writer. When he stops writing, the necessity ceases.
This contrasts with absolute necessity, which holds as long as the subject exists, regardless of description.
3
Analyze the example propositions
The author gives two examples: affirmative ('Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer') and negative ('No writer is still of fingers as long as he is a writer'). Note that both are necessary only under the condition of writing.
The examples demonstrate the form: 'ma dama wasfahu' meaning 'as long as his description exists'.
4
Distinguish from absolute necessary
Contrast this with the earlier type (absolute necessary) where the judgment holds as long as the subject's essence exists. Here, the judgment depends on the subject's attribute, not its essence.
This distinction is fundamental for complex logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ مَا دَامَ وَصْفُهُ) عطفٌ على قوله : ما دام ذاتُ الموضوع»؛ أي : إن كانَ الحكم بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع موجوداً؛ أي: بشرط وصفِ الموضوع ; (فَمَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ) . كقولنا بالضَّرورةِ : كلُّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً»، وبالضَّرورة: لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً»، فإنَّ ثبوت التَّحرُّكِ للكاتب وسلب الشكون عنه ليس ضروريا ما دام ذاته موجودةً، بل ضروري بشرط الوصف وهو الكتابة.
Or 'as long as its description' is a conjunction to his words 'as long as the essence of the subject'. That is: if the judgement is with the necessity of the relation, as long as the description of the subject exists, i.e., with the condition of the subject's description, then it is a general conditioned proposition. As we say, necessarily: 'Every writer is a mover of fingers as long as he is a writer', and necessarily: 'No writer is a still of fingers as long as he is a writer'. For the affirmation of movement for the writer and the negation of stillness from him are not necessary as long as his essence exists, but necessary with the condition of the description, which is writing.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of necessary proposition: المشروطة العامة (the general conditioned). Unlike the absolute necessary which is based on essence, this proposition's necessity depends on a condition, specifically the subject's description (وصف). The author explains that the judgement 'every writer moves his fingers' is only necessarily true as long as the person is a writer; once he stops writing, the necessity no longer applies. Similarly, the negation of stillness applies only as long as he is writing. The phrase 'general conditioned' indicates that the condition is not a specific time but the continuation of the attribute.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة

B) The subject's description (attribute)

A general conditioned proposition requires the continued existence of the subject's attribute for its necessity to hold.

Two Interpretations of the Conditioned Proposition
Commentators discuss two possible interpretations of المشروطة العامّة.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of المشروطة العامّة (general conditioned proposition) and explain why the commentators debated which meaning the author intended.

Core Idea
A tiny ambiguity in definition—'as long as' vs. 'with the condition of'—can change the entire logical force of a proposition.

In this passage, the commentators Dusuqi, Atar, and Sharnubi dissect the definition of the general conditioned proposition (المشروطة العامّة). The author’s phrasing 'as long as the description exists' seems at first to mean 'with the condition of the description', but a closer look reveals it could also mean a purely temporal duration without formal condition. This ambiguity is not trivial; it affects how we interpret necessity in conditional propositions. Let's walk through their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Find the key expression in the Arabic: 'ما دام وصف الموضوع' (as long as the description of the subject). This phrase appears in the definition of المشروطة العامّة and is the focus of the debate.
The phrase is ambiguous because it could be interpreted either as a condition or as a temporal duration.
2
Extract the two interpretations
The first interpretation: 'بشرط الوصف' (with the condition of the description) — the necessity holds only if the description is present. The second interpretation: 'ما دام الوصف' (as long as the description exists) — the necessity holds for the duration of the description's existence, without treating the description as a formal condition.
Dusuqi notes that the author's gloss suggests the first, but later the author says both are probable.
3
Trace the commentators' arguments
Dusuqi argues that the first meaning is apparent and suitable for the name 'conditioned'. Atar and Abd al-Hakim debate whether the phrase 'as long as the essence' in the definition of the absolute proposition serves as a restriction. Some commentators suggest deleting the gloss 'i.e., with the condition' to preserve the ambiguity. Sharnubi criticizes the commentator for erring, asserting the second meaning is correct based on the author's other work.
Notice how different commentators appeal to consistency with other texts and the appropriateness of the term 'conditioned'.
4
Evaluate the resolutions
Assess which interpretation seems more consistent with the author's overall system. Sharnubi's criticism relies on a specific passage from the author's commentary on al-Shamsiyya. Weigh the reasons: the first meaning fits the name but may be too restrictive; the second is more general but may not fit the term.
This step requires synthesizing the arguments presented in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الضَّرُورَةِ) أي: لفظاً في القضيَّةِ اللفظية، وحكم العقل بالضرورة في القضيَّةِ العقلية. (قَوْلُهُ : بِالضَّرُوْرَةِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَا فرق بين تقديم لفظ بالضَّرورة أو تأخيرِهِ (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: بِشَرْطِ ... إِلَخ) يقتضي أنَّ المراد بالمشروطة العامَّةِ أحد المعنيين الآتيين، وهو المعنى الأوَّلُ الآتي، وقوله في آخِرِ السَّوادة: واعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَا ذكره المصنِّفُ في تعريف المشروطة؛ مُحتمل لكِلا المعنيين إلخ، يُنافي ذلك العطار (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الحُكْمَ فِيْهَا غَيْرُ مُقَيَّدٍ ... إِلَخ) وأمَّا ذِكْرُ مَا دَامَ الذَّاتُ ; فَلِإبقاءِ الضَّرورةِ على عُمومِهَا ; لا لِلتَّقييدِ ، قالَهُ العِصامُ، وردَّهُ عبد الحكيم بأنَّ هذا التوجية مبني على عدم الفرق بين اعتبار القيدِ في المفهوم وفيما صدق عليه المفهوم ولم يفهم أنَّهُ في التَّعريفِ لِلإخراج، فكيف لا يكونُ تَقييداً؟ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ بِشَرْطِ وَصْفِ الْمَوْضُوع سيأتي لهُ أنَّ المشروطة قد تُقَالُ على الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورة النّسبة في جميع أوقاتِ ثبوتِ الوصف للموضوع، وحينئذ ; فَالمناسب أن يقول هنا :أي بشرط وصف الموضوع، أو في جميع أوقات وصف الموضوع، إلَّا أنَّهُ راعى ظاهر كلام المصنفِ ; حيثُ اقتصر على المعنى الأوَّلِ، وإن قال أَخِراً : إنّ كلامه يحتملُ كِلَا المعنيين. والدسوقي وَيُجابُ : بأنَّ قولَهُ: (أي : بشرط . . . إلخ) بناءً على ظاهرِ المثْنِ، فَلَا يُنافي أنَّ فيه احتمالاً آخر . احتمال, والحاصل (1) : أنَّ الشَّارِحَ إِنَّما حمل المصنّف على هذا المعنى; مَعِ لأمرَيْنِ كَمَا ذكرَ الشَّارِحُ في آخِرِ السَّوادة; لأنَّ هذا المعنى هو الظاهر من المصنفِ ; ولأنَّهُ المناسب للتّسمية بالمشروطة، بخلافِ المعنى الثَّاني، فإِنَّهُ لَا يناسب التسمية بالمشروطة. وقال بعضُهم : الأَوْلَى حذفُ قوله : أي : بشرط الوصف، ويبقى المصنِّفُ على ظاهرِهِ مِنَ احتمالِهِ لِلأمرَيْنِ، فَإِنَّ قولَهُ : مَا دامَ وَصْفُ الموضوع يحتمل أَنْ يُرادَ بِهِ بشرط الوصفِ، ويحتمل أَنْ يُرادَ بِهِ مَا دامَ الوصفُ مِنْ غيرِ اعتبارِ الاشتراط، فَعَلَى الاحتمال الأوَّلِ ; يكونُ إشارة للمشروطة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ، وعلى الاحتمال الثاني; يكونُ إشارة للمشروطة بالمعنى الثاني. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مَو متحرك ... إلخ، محمول، وقوله : ما دام... إلخ، لما كان ضرورة تحرُّكِ الأصابع للكاتب ليسَ مُقيَّداً في الواقع بدوامِ ذاتِ الكاتب; بلْ بمِدَّةِ الكتابةِ ; قيّد ذلكَ بِدوامِ وَصْفِ الموضوع. العطار (۱) (قوله : والحاصل ... إلخ) فيه أن الشارح أخطأ المنصوص وكان الواجب أن يفتر المتن بالمعنى الثَّاني فقط ويجعل المعنى الأوَّل مقابلاً له، فإن المصنف في شرح الشمسية جعل العبارة المحتملة (باعتبار وصف (كذا أما ما دام كذا كما هنا فجعله القطب مقابلاً بشرط كذا الذي هو المعنى الأول . . . هـ . الشَّرنوبي.
Dusuqi: His saying 'because they contain necessity' means verbally in the verbal proposition, and the mind's judgement of necessity in the mental proposition. His saying 'necessarily: every human is an animal' — no difference between placing the word 'necessarily' before or after. His saying 'i.e., with the condition...' implies that the intended meaning of the general conditioned is one of the two meanings, which is the first meaning. And his saying at the end of the margin: 'Know that what the author mentioned in the definition of the conditioned is probable for both meanings' contradicts that. Atar: His saying 'because the judgement in it is not restricted...' — as for mentioning 'as long as the essence', it is to preserve the universality of necessity, not for restriction, said by Isam. Abd al-Hakim refuted that this explanation is based on not differentiating between considering the restriction in the concept and in what the concept applies to, and he did not understand that it is in the definition for exclusion, so how can it not be a restriction? His saying 'i.e., with the condition of the subject's description' — he will state that the conditioned may be said of that in which the judgement is the necessity of the relation in all times of the existence of the description for the subject. Then it is appropriate to say here: 'i.e., with the condition of the subject's description' or 'in all times of the subject's description', except that he considered the apparent meaning of the author's words, where he restricted to the first meaning, although he said later that his words are probable for both meanings. Dusuqi: And it is answered that his saying (i.e., with the condition...) is based on the apparent meaning of the text, so it does not contradict that there is another probability. The summary: the commentator only interpreted the author according to this meaning for two reasons, as the commentator mentioned at the end of the margin: because this meaning is apparent from the author, and because it is suitable for the name 'conditioned', unlike the second meaning, which is not suitable. And some said: It is better to delete his words 'i.e., with the condition of the description' and leave the author's words as they appear with their probability for both. For his words 'as long as the description of the subject' can be understood as 'with the condition of the description' and can be understood as 'as long as the description' without considering the conditioning. On the first probability, it refers to the conditioned in the first meaning, and on the second, it refers to the conditioned in the second meaning. His saying 'every writer is moving fingers...' — since the necessity of movement of fingers for the writer is not actually restricted to the duration of the writer's essence, but to the duration of writing, he restricted it to the duration of the subject's description. Atar: (His saying: 'the summary...') in it is that the commentator erred in what is stated, and it was necessary to interpret the text by the second meaning only and make the first meaning its opposite. For the author in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya made the probable expression (by considering the description) as for 'as long as' as here, and al-Qutb made it an opposite to 'with the condition' which is the first meaning... etc. Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentators Dusuqi, Atar, and Sharnubi on the interpretation of the definition of المشروطة العامّة. The issue is whether the phrase 'as long as the description of the subject' means 'with the condition of the description' (a conditional interpretation) or 'during the time the description exists' (a temporal interpretation). Dusuqi points out that the definition given by the author seems to imply the first meaning, but later the author says his words are probable for both, causing a contradiction. Atar then discusses a subtle point about why the author mentioned 'as long as the essence' in the definition of the absolute proposition: according to Isam, it is to preserve the universality of necessity, not to restrict. Abd al-Hakim refutes this, arguing that it is indeed a restriction because it excludes cases where the essence is negated. Then they discuss the appropriate formulation. The summary states that the commentator chose the first meaning because it is apparent and suitable for the name. Some suggested deleting the gloss 'i.e., with the condition' to keep the author's text ambiguous. Finally, Sharnubi criticizes the commentator for misunderstanding the text, arguing that the correct interpretation should be the second meaning only, based on the author's other work. This lesson shows the precision of the logicians in analyzing definitions.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة
بشرط الوصف
ما دام الوصف

Key Takeaway
The same definitional phrase can carry two distinct logical interpretations (conditional vs. temporal), and commentators must resolve such ambiguities to apply the definition correctly in Islamic sciences.

Two Interpretations of the Conditioned Proposition (المشروطة)
Commentators refine the meaning of 'المشروطة' and differentiate two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of 'as long as the description of the subject' in a conditioned proposition and identify which interpretation a commentator is adopting.

Core Idea
The phrase 'as long as the description' can mean either a strict condition or mere duration.

In Islamic logic, the conditioned proposition (المشروطة) is essential for quantifying necessity. This passage from the commentary on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq reveals a subtle disagreement: does 'as long as the description of the subject' imply a condition (بشرط الوصف) or just a temporal frame? The difference affects how we evaluate the necessity in propositions like 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Let's unpack the two interpretations and see why the commentator was criticized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
Find the phrase 'ما دام وصف الموضوع' (as long as the description of the subject) in the Arabic. This is the phrase that carries two possible meanings.
2
Recognize the two interpretations
The first interpretation takes it as 'with the condition of the description' (بشرط الوصف), meaning the necessity holds only while the description is present. The second interpretation takes it as mere duration without conditioning the necessity on the description.
The passage says the phrase can bear both meanings.
3
Analyze the example
The example 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' shows that the necessity of movement is tied to the description 'writing', not to the writer's essence. This supports the first interpretation, but the phrase itself is ambiguous.
The necessity is not 'by essence' but 'by condition of the description'.
4
Evaluate the commentator's choice
The commentator restricted the meaning to بشرط الوصف. The passage gives two reasons: it is the apparent meaning and it fits the name 'mashruta'. However, some argued this restricts the author's intent, as the author likely allowed both interpretations.
The super-commentator criticizes this restriction.
5
Consider the alternative
Some suggest deleting the commentator's clarifying phrase 'بشرط الوصف' to preserve the ambiguity. This shows the importance of not over-specifying in logical definitions.
The passage ends with al-Sharnubi's note on the commentator's error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كقولنا بالضَّرورةِ : كلُّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً»، وبالضَّرورة: لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً»، فإنَّ ثبوت التَّحرُّكِ للكاتب وسلب الشكون عنه ليس ضروريا ما دام ذاتهُ موجودةً، بل ضروري بشرط الوصف وهو الكتابة.
وَيُجابُ : بأنَّ قولَهُ: (أي : بشرط . . . إلخ) بناءً على ظاهرِ المثْنِ، فَلَا يُنافي أنَّ فيه احتمالاً آخر .
والحاصل : أنَّ الشَّارِحَ إِنَّما حمل المصنّف على هذا المعنى؛ لأمرَيْنِ كَمَا ذكرَ الشَّارِحُ في آخِرِ السَّوادة؛ لأنَّ هذا المعنى هو الظاهر من المصنفِ ؛ ولأنَّهُ المناسب للتّسمية بالمشروطة، بخلافِ المعنى الثَّاني، فإِنَّهُ لَا يناسب التسمية بالمشروطة.
وقال بعضُهم : الأَوْلَى حذفُ قوله : أي : بشرط الوصف، ويبقى المصنِّفُ على ظاهرِهِ مِنَ احتمالِهِ لِلأمرَيْنِ، فَإِنَّ قولَهُ : مَا دامَ وَصْفُ الموضوع يحتمل أَنْ يُرادَ بِهِ بشرط الوصفِ، ويحتمل أَنْ يُرادَ بِهِ مَا دامَ الوصفُ مِنْ غيرِ اعتبارِ الاشتراط، فَعَلَى الاحتمال الأوَّلِ ؛ يكونُ إشارة للمشروطة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ، وعلى الاحتمال الثاني؛ يكونُ إشارة للمشروطة بالمعنى الثاني.
قوله: كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ متحرك ... إلخ، محمول، وقوله : ما دام... إلخ، لما كان ضرورة تحرُّكِ الأصابع للكاتب ليسَ مُقيَّداً في الواقع بدوامِ ذاتِ الكاتب؛ بلْ بمِدَّةِ الكتابةِ ؛ قيّد ذلكَ بِدوامِ وَصْفِ الموضوع.
(قوله : والحاصل ... إلخ) فيه أن الشارح أخطأ المنصوص وكان الواجب أن يفتر المتن بالمعنى الثَّاني فقط ويجعل المعنى الأوَّل مقابلاً له، فإن المصنف في شرح الشمسية جعل العبارة المحتملة (باعتبار وصف (كذا أما ما دام كذا كما هنا فجعله القطب مقابلاً بشرط كذا الذي هو المعنى الأول . . . هـ . الشَّرنوبي.
For example, we say necessarily: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer,' and necessarily: 'No writer is still his fingers as long as he is a writer.' For the affirmation of movement for the writer and the negation of stillness from him are not necessary as long as his essence exists, but necessary with the condition of the description, which is writing. And it is answered: That his saying (i.e., 'with the condition of the description, etc.') is based on the apparent meaning of the text, so it does not preclude that there is another possibility. And the conclusion: That the commentator only carried the author to this meaning for two reasons, as the commentator mentioned at the end of the page: because this meaning is apparent from the author, and because it is appropriate for the naming 'conditioned' (mashruta), unlike the second meaning, for it is not appropriate for the naming 'conditioned'. And some said: It is better to delete his saying 'i.e., with the condition of the description' and leave the author on its apparent meaning of accommodating both possibilities. For his saying 'as long as the description of the subject' may be intended to mean 'with the condition of the description' and may be intended to mean 'as long as the description' without considering the condition. So on the first probability, it is an indication of the conditioned proposition in the first meaning; and on the second probability, it is an indication of the conditioned proposition in the second meaning. His saying: 'Every writer moves... etc.' is being considered, and his saying 'as long as... etc.' — since the necessity of fingers moving for the writer is not in reality restricted to the continuance of the writer's essence, but to the duration of writing, he restricted it to the continuance of the description of the subject. (His saying: 'And the conclusion... etc.') Contains that the commentator erred in the text and should have fixed the text to the second meaning only and made the first meaning correlative to it. For the author in the commentary on al-Shamsiyya made the expression that is likely (by considering description) whereas 'as long as such' as here, al-Qutb made it correlative to 'with the condition of such' which is the first meaning... etc. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an earlier commentary on the 'conditioned proposition' (المشروطة). The discussion begins with an example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' and its contradictory. The point is that the necessity of the relation (movement) is not inherent to the writer's essence (ذات) but depends on the description 'writing'. This creates a distinction between two interpretations of 'as long as the description of the subject' (ما دام وصف الموضوع). The first interpretation takes it to mean 'with the condition of the description' (بشرط الوصف), so the proposition is true only while the description holds. The second interpretation takes it to mean merely 'as long as the description exists' without requiring the description to be a condition. The commentator (شارح) is criticized for adopting only the first interpretation, while the author (مصنف) likely intended both possibilities. Some argue that the commentator's phrase 'بشرط الوصف' should be deleted to preserve the ambiguity. The passage then examines the example: the necessity of finger movement for a writer is actually restricted to the duration of writing, not to the writer's essence. Finally, al-Sharnubi notes that the commentator made an error by not fixing the text to the second meaning alone, referencing the author's explanation in the commentary on al-Shamsiyya. This is typical of hashiya literature, where the super-commentator corrects or refines the earlier gloss.

Key Terms
المشروطة
بشرط الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the issue with the commentator's interpretation of 'ما دام وصف الموضوع'?
A) He ignored the example.
B) He restricted it to only one of the two possible meanings.
C) He confused writer with writing.
D) He used the wrong example.

Key Takeaway
The conditioned proposition (المشروطة) can be understood in two ways: with the condition of the description or merely as long as the description holds, and commentators must be careful not to prematurely restrict its meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Explain using the two interpretations from the passage.

Propositions

Four Interpretations of Subject and Predicate in Universal Propositions
Author explains four interpretations of subject/predicate and identifies the correct one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four interpretations of subject and predicate is correct in a universal categorical proposition and explain why the other three are invalid.

Core Idea
In a valid universal proposition, the subject refers to individuals, the predicate to the concept.

This passage from the Tadhheeb al-Mantiq discusses a foundational issue: how do we interpret the terms 'subject' and 'predicate' in a universal statement like 'Every human is an animal'? Four possibilities exist, but only one yields correct predication. Understanding this is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the four possible interpretations
According to the passage, the four possibilities are: (1) both subject and predicate refer to the concept (mafhūm); (2) both refer to the referents (masdaq); (3) subject refers to the concept, predicate to the referents; (4) subject refers to the referents, predicate to the concept.
These are the logical possibilities for a universal proposition like 'Every J is B'.
2
Identify which interpretation is correct
The author states that only the fourth interpretation is valid: the subject term refers to its individual instances (e.g., Zayd, Amr, Bakr for 'human'), and the predicate term refers to the universal concept (e.g., 'rational animal' for 'animal'). This is because the judgment in a universal proposition is about the individuals, and the predicate describes them by a concept.
3
Analyze why the other three are invalid
Case (1) would restrict predication to natural propositions (judging the nature, not individuals). Case (2) would make every proposition necessary (since subject and referent would be identical in extension, leading to self-predication). Case (3) is possible but not used because the judgment should be on individuals, not the nature.
The passage emphasizes that in the sciences, the correct interpretation is essential for meaningful predication.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the proposition 'Every human is an animal'. According to the correct interpretation, 'human' refers to individual humans (Zayd, Amr, etc.), and 'animal' refers to the universal concept that applies to each individual human. This avoids the problems of the other interpretations.
The same analysis applies to any universal proposition in Islamic texts, such as 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يُرادَ مِنْهُمَا الأفراد فقط، أو الماهية أو مِنَ الأَوَّلِ الأفرادُ، وَمِنَ الثَّاني الماهيَّةُ، والعكسُ ، وَلَا يصح الحمل في ثلاثة، ويصح في واحد، وهو أنْ يُرادَ مِنَ الأوَّلِ الأفرادُ، وَمِنَ الثَّاني المفهوم، وهذا هو المعتبر (قَوْلُهُ: مِنَ الْأَفْرَادِ) بيانُ لـ «ما»، فإذا قُلْتَ : كلُّ إنسان حيوان؛ فَذات الإنسانِ أفرادُهُ مِنْ (زيد وعمرو وبكر)، ومفهوم الإنسان؛ أي: حقيقته، وهو حيوان ناطق ؛ يُقالُ لهُ: وَصْفُ الموضوع؛ أي: وَصْفُ أفراد الموضوع؛ لاتصافها بالإنسانية.
قيلَ : كُلُّ أبيضَ كَذَا؛ دخل فيه الزّنجي مطلقاً عند الفارابي، وبشرط أن يفرضَهُ العقلُ أبيض بالفعل عند الشَّيخِ، قال المحققون : والعرفُ واللُّغةُ إِنَّما يُستعمل فيهما القضايا الفعليَّاتُ، فَإِنَّ غالب أخبارِهِم حكاياتٌ لِمَا وقعَ أو يقعُ، وبحمل الإمكان على مقابل لامتناع ؛ لا تفسيره بالقوَّةِ المقابل للفعل يندفع عن الفارابي مَا يُقالُ إِنَّه يلزم على مذهبِهِ كذبُ كُلّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورةِ؛ لأنَّ النُّطْفةَ مِمَّا يمكن أن يكون إنساناً ولَيسَتْ حيواناً بالضَّرورةِ، فإنَّ هذا الإيراد مبناه حملُ الإمكان في كلامِهِ بمعنى القوَّةِ المقابلة للفعل، ما قرَّرَهُ الشَّارِحُ مِن هذا الحكم إنَّما هو في القضايا المستعملة في العلوم، وإلا ؛ فَالطَّبيعيّةُ والشَّخصيَّةُ لا يجري فيهما ما ذكر، فليس هذا الحكمُ كُلِّيًّا
(قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الأَفْرَادِ) بيان لما صدق الموضوع ، والمراد بها ما يشمل الأشخاص والأنواع، قال المصنِّفُ : إذا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ ج ب ؛ فَذاتُ ج يُسمَّى ذات الموضوعِ، وَج وصفه وعنوانه، أمَّا ذاتُ الموضوع ؛ فَنعني بـ (ج) مثلاً : مَا يصدقُ عليهِ جِ مِنَ الجزئيَّاتِ الشَّخصيَّةِ إِنْ كانَ ج نوعاً أو فصلاً أو خاصَّةً، والجزئيَّاتُ الشَّخصيَّةُ والنَّوعيَّةُ إنْ كانَ جِنساً أو فصل جنس أو عرضاً عاماً ؛ لأنَّ هذا هو المفهوم اللُّغةِ والعُرفِ .ا.هـ. ثمَّ إِنَّ الاحتمالاتِ أربعةُ ؛ الأَوَّلُ : أَنْ يُراد المفهومُ مِن كُلُّ منهما وهو باطل، وإلا ؛ لانحصر الحملُ في القضايا الطَّبيعيَّةِ الثَّاني : أنْ يُراد الماصدق منهما، وهو أيضاً باطل لأنَّ ما صدق عليه الموضوع؛ هو بعينه ما صدق عليه المحمول؛ سواءٌ انحصر ما صدق عليه المحمول فيما صدق عليه الموضوع بحسب
كَمَا في المحمول المساوي؛ أو لم ينحصرْ كَمَا في الأعمّ، فَيلزمُ ثبوتُ الشَّيء لنفسِهِ وهو ضَروري، فتنحصر القضايا في الضَّروريَّةِ الثَّالثُ : أَنْ يُرادَ مِنَ الموضوع : المفهومُ، وَمِنَ المحمول : الماصدقُ ، وهذا الاحتمالُ وإِنْ صح؛ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ ليسَ مِنَ القضايا المعتبرةِ لِمَا عُلِمَ أَنَّ الحُكْمَ على الأفراد فيها دونَ الطَّبيعة، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يُرادَ مِنَ الموضوع: الأفراد، وَمِنَ المحمول : المفهوم، وهو المطلوب، وقد أفردْنَا هذا المحلَّ برسالةٍ كبيرةٍ تكلَّمْنَا فيها على عقد الوضع والحمل في الحمليَّاتِ، فَمَنْ أرادَ الزِّيادةَ على مَا هُنَا ، فَلْيَرجِعْ إليها، فإنَّ فيها نفائس وفوائد. (قَوْلُهُ: يُسَمَّى ذَاتَ الْمَوْضُوعِ المرادُ مِنَ الموضوع هنا : الوصف، وفي قوله: وصف الموضوع الذات، أو ما يُقالُ لهُ موضوع فى الجملة في الموضِعَين؛ فَلَا يرد أنَّهُ إِنْ أُريدَ منهُ فيهما معاً الوصفُ ؛ لزمَ إضافةُ الشَّيء لِنَفْسِهِ فِي الثَّانِي، أَو الذَّات؛ لزم ذلك في الأوَّلِ ما لم تكن الإضافة بيانيَّةٌ .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَمَنْهُوْمُ الْمَوْضُوعِ) أي : الأمرُ الكُلِّيُّ الصَّادقُ على تلك الأفراد. (قَوْلُهُ: وَعُنْوَانَهُ سُمِّي بذلك لأنَّ بِهِ تعرفُ ذات الموضوع؛ أي: أفرادهُ لَمَّا أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّ مِرآة لمشاهدة أفرادِهِ، كَمَا يُعرَفُ الكتاب بعنوانِهِ.
Because it is either intended from both of them individuals only, or the essence, or from the first individuals and from the second the essence, and the opposite. And predication is not valid in three, and is valid in one, which is that from the first individuals is intended and from the second the concept, and this is the considered one. (His statement: 'from the individuals') is an explanation of 'ma'. So if you say: Every human is an animal; then the essence of human is its individuals from (Zayd and Amr and Bakr), and the concept of human; i.e., its reality, and it is 'rational animal'; it is said to be: description of the subject; i.e., description of the individuals of the subject, because of their being described by humanness.
It was said: Every white thing is such; the black person enters into it absolutely according to al-Farabi, and with the condition that the mind supposes it actually white according to the Shaykh. The verifiers said: Custom and language only employ actual propositions, for most of their statements are narrations of what occurred or will occur. And by interpreting 'imkan' as opposite of impossible, not explaining it as potentiality opposite actuality, what is said against al-Farabi—that on his view the statement 'Every human is an animal' would necessarily be false because the sperm-drop can possibly be human and is not necessarily an animal—is repelled. For this objection is based on interpreting 'imkan' in his speech as potentiality opposite actuality. What the commentator established of this ruling is only in propositions used in the sciences; otherwise, natural and individual propositions do not apply to them what was mentioned, so this ruling is not universal.
(His statement: 'from the individuals') is an explanation of what the subject is true of, and what is intended by it includes persons and species. The author said: When we say: Every J is B; then the essence of J is called the essence of the subject, and J is its description and its title. As for the essence of the subject, we mean by (J) for example: what J is true of from the individual particulars if J is a species or differentia or property, and from the specific and generic particulars if J is a genus or differential genus or universal accident, because this is the linguistic and customary concept. End quote. Then the probabilities are four: First: that the concept is intended from each of them, and it is false, otherwise predication would be restricted to natural propositions. Second: that the referent is intended from both, and it is also false because what the subject is true of is exactly what the predicate is true of; whether what the predicate is true of is confined to what the subject is true of, as in coextensive predicate, or not confined as in the more general, so it would follow that the thing is affirmed of itself, which is necessary, so propositions would be restricted to necessary ones. Third: that from the subject the concept is intended and from the predicate the referent; this probability, even if sound, is not among the considered propositions because it is known that judgment in them is upon individuals, not upon the nature. So it is determined that from the subject the individuals are intended, and from the predicate the concept, and that is what is sought. And we have devoted a large treatise to this topic in which we spoke about the contract of position and predication in categoricals, so whoever wants more than what is here should refer to it, for it contains precious gems and benefits. (His statement: 'is called the essence of the subject') The intended from the subject here is the description, and in his statement 'description of the subject' is the essence, or what is called subject in the sentence in both places; so it is not objected that if from it in both places the description is intended, then the addition of the thing to itself would follow in the second, or the essence, it would follow in the first unless the addition is explicative.
(His statement: 'and the concept of the subject') i.e., the universal thing that is true of those individuals. (His statement: 'and its title') It is called that because by it the essence of the subject is known; i.e., its individuals, since the universal is a mirror for observing its individuals, just as a book is known by its title.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the proper interpretation of subject and predicate terms in universal categorical propositions (al-qadaya al-hamliyya al-kulliyya). The author begins by listing the four possible interpretations of the terms in a proposition like 'كل ج ب' (Every J is B): both terms refer to individuals only; both refer to the essence (mahiya); subject refers to individuals and predicate to essence; or subject refers to essence and predicate to individuals. He states that only the third possibility—subject as individuals, predicate as concept—is correct for valid predication. He then illustrates with the example 'كل إنسان حيوان' (Every human is an animal): the 'essence' of the subject 'human' is its individual instances (Zayd, Amr, Bakr), while its 'concept' is the reality 'rational animal', which is called the 'description' (wasf) of the subject.

A subtle objection is raised: in a proposition like 'كل أبيض كذا' (Every white thing is such-and-such), a black person would be included according to al-Farabi (because potential whiteness suffices), but according to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) only if the mind supposes him actually white. The verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) respond that such propositions in Arabic usage are actual (fi'liyya), narrating what occurred or will occur. The objection against al-Farabi (that his view would make 'Every human is an animal' false because a sperm-drop is potentially human but not actually an animal) is repelled by understanding 'imkan' (possibility) as the opposite of impossibility, not as potentiality opposite actuality. The commentator clarifies that this ruling applies only to propositions used in the sciences, not to natural or individual propositions, so it is not universal.

Returning to the main discussion, the author explains that 'من الأفراد' in the original text clarifies what the subject term is true of (masdaq), which includes both individuals (persons) and types (species). He then repeats the four probabilities with more detail: (1) both terms as concept—false because it restricts predication to natural propositions; (2) both as referent—false because it makes every proposition necessary (since subject and predicate would be identical in extension); (3) subject as concept, predicate as referent—though logically possible, it is not considered because the judgment in categoricals is about individuals, not the nature; (4) subject as individuals, predicate as concept—this is the correct, considered interpretation. The author refers to a larger treatise he wrote on this topic.

Finally, he clarifies the terminology: 'ذات الموضوع' (essence of the subject) means the individuals of which the subject term is true, and 'مفهوم الموضوع' (concept of the subject) is the universal that applies to those individuals, also called 'وصف' (description) or 'عنوان' (title). The term 'عنوان' is explained by analogy: just as a book is known by its title, the universal ('concept') is a mirror through which its individual instances are known.

Key Terms

الماصدق
ذات الموضوع
وصف الموضوع
العنوان

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'Every human is an animal' (كل إنسان حيوان), which interpretation of 'human' and 'animal' is considered valid according to this passage?
A) 'Human' refers to the concept, 'animal' refers to individuals.
B) 'Human' refers to individuals, 'animal' refers to the concept.
C) Both refer to individuals.
D) Both refer to concepts.

Key Takeaway
In a valid universal proposition, the subject term stands for its individual referents (masdaq), while the predicate term stands for the universal concept (mafhūm) that applies to those individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Meanings of General Conditional
Explaining two meanings of general conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of the general conditional and identify which meaning applies to a given proposition.

Core Idea
The general conditional has two faces: one where description conditions necessity, another where description is necessary.

In this passage from al-Tadhheeb, al-Dasuqi clarifies that the term 'general conditional' (المشروطة العامة) actually covers two distinct interpretations. This distinction is essential for correctly analysing categorical propositions in syllogistic logic, especially when dealing with temporal or accidental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning
Under the first meaning, the predicate is necessary for the subject only while the description exists; the description may be temporary. Example: 'Every walking thing is an animal as long as it walks.'
The description is a condition but not necessarily a permanent attribute.
2
Identify the second meaning
Under the second meaning, the predicate is necessary for the subject at all times when the description applies, and the description is a necessary attribute of the subject. Example: 'Every human is rational at all times.'
The description is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of the subject.
3
Apply to examples
For instance, 'Every sleeping person is alive while asleep' fits the first meaning (sleeping is temporary). 'Every human is a rational animal' fits the second meaning (rationality is essential).
4
Check overlap and divergence
The two meanings coincide when the description both conditions necessity and is necessary. They diverge when the description is only a condition without being necessary.
Al-Dasuqi highlights this relationship as 'عموم من وجه' (generality from one perspective).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تُقالُ : المشروطة العامة على القضيَّةِ الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورةِ
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : ... إلخ) أي : وقد تُطلَقُ ، وحاصله : أنَّ المشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ ؛ مَا كانَ ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا؛ بشرط وجودِ الوصفِ ؛ كان ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوعِ فِي وَقْتِ مِنَ الأوقاتِ أم لا، فَوُجودُ الوصْفِ فيها مُعتبرُ شَرطاً في ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ .
وأمَّا ثبوتُ الوصْفِ لأفرادِ الموضوع ؛ فَتارةً يكون ضروريا، وتارة يكونُ ممكناً، وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني ؛ فَهِيَ مَا كانَ ثبوت المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا في جميع أوقاتِ وَصْفِ الموضوع؛ لِكَوْنِ ذلكَ الوصفِ لازماً لأفرادِ الموضوع ؛ كانَ وجود الوصفِ قيداً في ضرورةِ النّسبة أم لا .
وحينئذ؛ فبين المشروطتين عمومٌ مِن وجه يجتمعان فيما إذا كان الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، وَكانَ ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع، وينفرد المعنى الأوَّلُ فيما إذا كانَ الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورة النّسبةِ، وَكانَ الوصفُ ليسَ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقات.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : المَشْرُوْطَةُ ... إلخ) هذه المشروطة التي لم يعتبر فيها أن يكونَ لِوَصفِ موضوعهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها كَمَا يأتي في كلامِهِ، وبقي عليهِ معنى ثالثُ لَهَا ؛ ذَكَرَهُ في شرح المطالع ، وَهِيَ المشروطةُ الَّتي وصفُ موضوعها منشأُ ضَرورة محمولها، وهي أخصُّ مِنَ المشروطة التي قبلها ؛ أي: مشروطة الشَّرطِ الَّتي اعتبر فيها أنْ يكونَ لِوصفِ مَوضعِهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها ؛ لأنَّ الوصفَ إذا كانَ منشأ الضَّرورةِ؛ كانَ له دخل فيها ، بخلافِ العَكْسِ، فَإِنَّهُ يصدقُ في الذهن الحارّ بعضُ الحارّ ذائبُ بالضَّرورة ما دامَ حارا؛ أي: بشرط الحرارة، وَلَا يصدقُ لأجل الحرارةِ؛ لأنَّ ذاتَ الذَّهنِ لَوْ لم يكن له دخل في الذوبان وكانت الحرارة كافية لكانَ الحجرُ ذائباً، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّ المدخليَّة المعتبرة في مشروطة الشَّرطِ إِنْ كانَت بمعنى المدخليَّةِ التَّامَّةِ لِضرورة المحمول؛ كَذَبَتْ في المثالِ أيضاً، وإن كانَتْ بمعنى مُطلق المدخليَّةِ فيُقالُ : المراد من المنشئيَّةِ أيضاً المذكور
It is also said: the general conditional is the proposition in which a judgment is made with necessity. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'And it is said... etc.' i.e., it is used. Its summary: that the general conditional in the first meaning is that in which the predicate's belonging to the subject is necessary conditioned upon the existence of the description, whether that description is concomitant to the individuals of the subject at some time or not. So the existence of the description is considered a condition in the necessity of the relation. As for the belonging of the description to the subject's individuals, it is sometimes necessary and sometimes possible. As for the conditional in the second meaning, it is that in which the predicate's belonging to the subject is necessary in all times of the description's belonging to the subject, because that description is concomitant to the subject's individuals, whether the existence of the description is a condition in the necessity of the relation or not. Hence, between the two conditionals there is a generality from one perspective: they coincide when the description is a condition in the necessity of the relation and that description is concomitant to the subject's individuals. The first meaning is distinct when the description is a condition in the necessity of the relation but the description is not concomitant to the subject's individuals at any time. Al-Attar: His statement: 'And it is said: the conditional... etc.' This conditional is the one in which it is not considered that the description of its subject has a role in the necessity of its predicate, as will come in his speech. There remains a third meaning for it, mentioned in Sharh al-Matali', which is the conditional whose subject's description is the source of the necessity of its predicate. It is more specific than the conditional before it; i.e., the conditional of condition (shart) in which it is considered that the description of its subject has a role in the necessity of its predicate, because if the description is the source of necessity, it has a role in it, contrary to the converse. For example, it is true that 'some hot mind is necessarily melting as long as it is hot', i.e., conditionally upon heat, but it is not true because of heat, for if the mind's essence had no role in melting and heat were sufficient, then stone would melt. There is a consideration in this: because the role considered in the conditional of condition, if it is in the sense of complete role in the necessity of the predicate, then it is false in the example as well; if it is in the sense of absolute role, then it is said: the intended from 'source' also (is absolute role)... (text ends)
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on conditional propositions, specifically the 'general conditional' (المشروطة العامة). The commentator Al-Dasuqi explains that this term has two meanings. The first meaning: a proposition where the predicate holds necessarily for the subject on the condition that the description (e.g., 'hot' or 'walking') exists at the time of judgment. The description itself may or may not be a permanent attribute of the subject. For example, 'Every walking thing is an animal' is necessary only as long as it walks; the description 'walking' may not be a necessary attribute. The second meaning: a proposition where the predicate holds necessarily for the subject at all times when the description applies, and the description is a necessary (لازم) attribute of the subject. For instance, 'Every human is a rational animal' – the description 'rational' is a necessary attribute. These two meanings have a partial overlap: they coincide when the description both conditions the necessity and is a necessary attribute. The first meaning is unique when the description conditions necessity but is not a necessary attribute (e.g., 'walking' for humans). Al-Attar then adds a third meaning found in Sharh al-Matali', where the description is the source (منشأ) of the necessity, not just a condition. He illustrates with an example: 'Some hot mind is necessarily melting as long as it is hot' – here heat is a condition, but not the source, because if heat were the source, then any hot thing would melt, which is false (e.g., hot stone does not melt). He notes a nuance: if 'role' is interpreted as complete role, then even the example fails; if interpreted as absolute role, then the term 'source' may also be absolute. This shows the complexity of classifying conditional propositions in logic.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
الوصف
الضرورة

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the general conditional in the first meaning (description conditions necessity but is not necessarily concomitant)?
A) Every human is rational as long as he exists.
B) Every walking thing is an animal as long as it walks.
C) Every human is a rational animal at all times.
D) Some hot mind is melting as long as it is hot.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition has at least two meanings: one where the description conditions necessity, another where the description is a necessary attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following propositions as first or second meaning of the general conditional and justify your answer: (a) 'Every sleeping person is alive while asleep.' (b) 'Every human is a rational animal at all times.'

The Third Meaning of General Conditional: Description as Source of Necessity
Explaining the third meaning of general conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third meaning of the general conditional, where the description is the source of necessity, and distinguish it from the first two meanings.

Core Idea
Beyond conditioning necessity, the description can be the very source and cause of the necessity.

Al-Attar, building on al-Dasuqi, introduces a third interpretation of the general conditional: the description is not just a condition but the source (منشأ) of the necessity. This subtle distinction has important implications for causal reasoning in logic, as illustrated by the example of 'hot mind' and 'stone'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third meaning
Under the third meaning, the description is the source (منشأ) of the necessity, meaning the predicate's necessity is caused by the description itself, not merely conditioned by it.
This is more specific than the second meaning: if description is source, it has a role; the converse is not necessarily true.
2
Compare with first and second meanings
First meaning: description conditions necessity (e.g., 'while walking'). Second meaning: description is necessary attribute (e.g., 'rationality'). Third meaning: description is the cause of necessity (e.g., heat causing melting).
The third meaning requires a causal link.
3
Analyse the example
Al-Attar gives the example: 'Some hot mind is necessarily melting as long as it is hot.' Here heat is a condition, but not the source of melting, because if it were the source, then any hot object (like stone) would melt. The mind's own essence also plays a role.
The example shows that not every condition is a source.
4
Note the nuance about 'role'
Al-Attar discusses whether 'role' (مدخليّة) in the conditional of condition means complete role or absolute role. This affects whether the example fits the third meaning.
If complete role, the example fails; if absolute role, the third meaning may also be absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تُقالُ : المشروطة العامة على القضيَّةِ الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورةِ
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : ... إلخ) أي : وقد تُطلَقُ ، وحاصله : أنَّ المشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ ؛ مَا كانَ ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا؛ بشرط وجودِ الوصفِ ؛ كان ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوعِ فِي وَقْتِ مِنَ الأوقاتِ أم لا، فَوُجودُ الوصْفِ فيها مُعتبرُ شَرطاً في ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ .
وأمَّا ثبوتُ الوصْفِ لأفرادِ الموضوع ؛ فَتارةً يكون ضروريا، وتارة يكونُ ممكناً، وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني ؛ فَهِيَ مَا كانَ ثبوت المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا في جميع أوقاتِ وَصْفِ الموضوع؛ لِكَوْنِ ذلكَ الوصفِ لازماً لأفرادِ الموضوع ؛ كانَ وجود الوصفِ قيداً في ضرورةِ النّسبة أم لا .
وحينئذ؛ فبين المشروطتين عمومٌ مِن وجه يجتمعان فيما إذا كان الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، وَكانَ ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع، وينفرد المعنى الأوَّلُ فيما إذا كانَ الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورة النّسبةِ، وَكانَ الوصفُ ليسَ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقات.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : المَشْرُوْطَةُ ... إلخ) هذه المشروطة التي لم يعتبر فيها أن يكونَ لِوَصفِ موضوعهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها كَمَا يأتي في كلامِهِ، وبقي عليهِ معنى ثالثُ لَهَا ؛ ذَكَرَهُ في شرح المطالع ، وَهِيَ المشروطةُ الَّتي وصفُ موضوعها منشأُ ضَرورة محمولها، وهي أخصُّ مِنَ المشروطة التي قبلها ؛ أي: مشروطة الشَّرطِ الَّتي اعتبر فيها أنْ يكونَ لِوصفِ مَوضعِهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها ؛ لأنَّ الوصفَ إذا كانَ منشأ الضَّرورةِ؛ كانَ له دخل فيها ، بخلافِ العَكْسِ، فَإِنَّهُ يصدقُ في الذهن الحارّ بعضُ الحارّ ذائبُ بالضَّرورة ما دامَ حارا؛ أي: بشرط الحرارة، وَلَا يصدقُ لأجل الحرارةِ؛ لأنَّ ذاتَ الذَّهنِ لَوْ لم يكن له دخل في الذوبان وكانت الحرارة كافية لكانَ الحجرُ ذائباً، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّ المدخليَّة المعتبرة في مشروطة الشَّرطِ إِنْ كانَت بمعنى المدخليَّةِ التَّامَّةِ لِضرورة المحمول؛ كَذَبَتْ في المثالِ أيضاً، وإن كانَتْ بمعنى مُطلق المدخليَّةِ فيُقالُ : المراد من المنشئيَّةِ أيضاً المذكور
It is also said: the general conditional is the proposition in which a judgment is made with necessity. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'And it is said... etc.' i.e., it is used. Its summary: that the general conditional in the first meaning is that in which the predicate's belonging to the subject is necessary conditioned upon the existence of the description, whether that description is concomitant to the individuals of the subject at some time or not. So the existence of the description is considered a condition in the necessity of the relation. As for the belonging of the description to the subject's individuals, it is sometimes necessary and sometimes possible. As for the conditional in the second meaning, it is that in which the predicate's belonging to the subject is necessary in all times of the description's belonging to the subject, because that description is concomitant to the subject's individuals, whether the existence of the description is a condition in the necessity of the relation or not. Hence, between the two conditionals there is a generality from one perspective: they coincide when the description is a condition in the necessity of the relation and that description is concomitant to the subject's individuals. The first meaning is distinct when the description is a condition in the necessity of the relation but the description is not concomitant to the subject's individuals at any time. Al-Attar: His statement: 'And it is said: the conditional... etc.' This conditional is the one in which it is not considered that the description of its subject has a role in the necessity of its predicate, as will come in his speech. There remains a third meaning for it, mentioned in Sharh al-Matali', which is the conditional whose subject's description is the source of the necessity of its predicate. It is more specific than the conditional before it; i.e., the conditional of condition (shart) in which it is considered that the description of its subject has a role in the necessity of its predicate, because if the description is the source of necessity, it has a role in it, contrary to the converse. For example, it is true that 'some hot mind is necessarily melting as long as it is hot', i.e., conditionally upon heat, but it is not true because of heat, for if the mind's essence had no role in melting and heat were sufficient, then stone would melt. There is a consideration in this: because the role considered in the conditional of condition, if it is in the sense of complete role in the necessity of the predicate, then it is false in the example as well; if it is in the sense of absolute role, then it is said: the intended from 'source' also (is absolute role)... (text ends)
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on conditional propositions, specifically the 'general conditional' (المشروطة العامة). The commentator Al-Dasuqi explains that this term has two meanings. The first meaning: a proposition where the predicate holds necessarily for the subject on the condition that the description (e.g., 'hot' or 'walking') exists at the time of judgment. The description itself may or may not be a permanent attribute of the subject. For example, 'Every walking thing is an animal' is necessary only as long as it walks; the description 'walking' may not be a necessary attribute. The second meaning: a proposition where the predicate holds necessarily for the subject at all times when the description applies, and the description is a necessary (لازم) attribute of the subject. For instance, 'Every human is a rational animal' – the description 'rational' is a necessary attribute. These two meanings have a partial overlap: they coincide when the description both conditions the necessity and is a necessary attribute. The first meaning is unique when the description conditions necessity but is not a necessary attribute (e.g., 'walking' for humans). Al-Attar then adds a third meaning found in Sharh al-Matali', where the description is the source (منشأ) of the necessity, not just a condition. He illustrates with an example: 'Some hot mind is necessarily melting as long as it is hot' – here heat is a condition, but not the source, because if heat were the source, then any hot thing would melt, which is false (e.g., hot stone does not melt). He notes a nuance: if 'role' is interpreted as complete role, then even the example fails; if interpreted as absolute role, then the term 'source' may also be absolute. This shows the complexity of classifying conditional propositions in logic.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
الوصف
الضرورة
منشأ الضرورة

C) Description is the source of necessity.

Key Takeaway
The third meaning of the general conditional makes the description the cause of the predicate necessity, not just a condition.

Propositions
advanced
15 min
Two Meanings of the General Conditional
Dusuqi and 'Attar define two meanings of general conditional and explain their relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Dusuqi's two meanings of the general conditional and identify the general-special relation between them.

Core Idea
The general conditional has two senses: description as condition vs. description as necessary attribute.

In this passage, Dusuqi and 'Attar refine the definition of the general conditional (mashrutah 'ammah), clarifying that it has two distinct meanings. The first takes the description as a condition for the necessity of the predication, regardless of whether the description is necessary for the subject's individuals. The second takes the description as a necessary attribute of the subject, making the predicate necessary in all times the description holds. Understanding these two meanings is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate where Dusuqi states 'al-ma'na al-awwal' (first meaning) and 'al-ma'na al-thani' (second meaning). Note the key phrases: 'bisharti wujud al-wasf' (on condition of the description's existence) for the first, and 'fi jami'i awqati wasf al-mawdu' li-kawni dhalika al-wasf laziman li-afradi al-mawdu'' (in all times of the description because the description is necessary for the individuals of the subject) for the second.
The Arabic text has these definitions clearly demarcated.
2
Compare the role of the description
In the first meaning, the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication; it may or may not be necessary for the subject's individuals. In the second, the description is necessary for the subject's individuals, and the necessity of the predication holds in all times the description is present, regardless of whether the description is a condition.
3
Analyze the general-special relation
The passage states there is 'umum min wajh' (general-special from one aspect) between the two. Identify the three cases: (1) coincidence: when the description is both a condition and necessary for individuals; (2) first exclusive: when it is a condition but not necessary; (3) second exclusive: when it is necessary but not a condition. Use the examples from the passage to illustrate.
For the first exclusive, think of a description that is a condition but not inherent, e.g., 'writer' (not all humans are always writing).
4
Test with an example
Consider the proposition 'Some hot thing is necessarily melting as long as it is hot'. Determine which meaning(s) apply. According to the passage, this proposition is true under both meanings? Actually, it fits the first meaning if 'hot' is a condition for melting, but not necessarily true for all hot things. It fits the second meaning if 'hot' is a necessary attribute of that hot thing, but since not all hot things melt, it fails the second. So it is only valid under the first meaning. This shows the difference.
The passage later critiques a third meaning using this example.

وقد تُقالُ : المشروطة العامة على القضيَّةِ الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورةِ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : ... إلخ) أي : وقد تُطلَقُ ، وحاصله : أنَّ المشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ ؛ مَا كانَ ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا؛ بشرط وجودِ الوصفِ ؛ كان ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوعِ فِي وَقْتِ مِنَ الأوقاتِ أم لا، فَوُجودُ الوصْفِ فيها مُعتبرُ شَرطاً في ضرورة النِّسبةِ . وأمَّا ثبوتُ الوصْفِ لأفرادِ الموضوع ؛ فَتارةً يكون ضروريا، وتارة يكونُ ممكناً. وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني ; فَهِيَ مَا كانَ ثبوت المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا في جميع أوقاتِ وَصْفِ الموضوع؛ لِكَوْنِ ذلكَ الوصفِ لازماً لأفرادِ الموضوع ; كانَ وجود الوصفِ قيداً في ضرورةِ النّسبة أم لا . وحينئذ; فبين المشروطتين عمومٌ مِن وجه يجتمعان فيما إذا كان الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، وَكانَ ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع، وينفرد المعنى الأوَّلُ فيما إذا كانَ الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورة النّسبةِ، وَكانَ الوصفُ ليسَ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقات. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : المَشْرُوْطَةُ ... إلخ) هذه المشروطة التي لم يعتبر فيها أن يكونَ لِوَصفِ موضوعِهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها كَمَا يأتي في كلامِهِ، وبقي عليهِ معنى ثالثُ لَهَا ; ذَكَرَهُ في شرح المطالع ، وَهِيَ المشروطةُ الَّتي وصفُ موضوعها منشأُ ضَرورة محمولها، وهي أخصُّ مِنَ المشروطة التي قبلها ; أي: مشروطة الشَّرطِ الَّتي اعتبر فيها أنْ يكونَ لِوصفِ مَوضعِهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها ; لأنَّ الوصفَ إذا كانَ منشأ الضَّرورةِ; كانَ له دخل فيها ، بخلافِ العَكْسِ، فَإِنَّهُ يصدقُ في الذهن الحارّ بعضُ الحارّ ذائبُ بالضَّرورة ما دامَ حارا; أي: بشرط الحرارة، وَلَا يصدقُ لأجل الحرارةِ; لأنَّ ذاتَ الذَّهنِ لَوْ لم يكن له دخل في الذوبان وكانت الحرارة كافية لكانَ الحجرُ ذائباً، وفيه نظر; لأنَّ المدخليَّة المعتبرة في مشروطة الشَّرطِ إِنْ كانَت بمعنى المدخليَّةِ التَّامَّةِ لِضرورة المحمول; كَذَبَتْ في المثالِ أيضاً، وإن كانَتْ بمعنى مُطلق المدخليَّةِ فيُقالُ : المراد من المنشئيَّةِ أيضاً كذلك، فَلا فرق، فالحقُ رجوعُهُما لمعنى واحدٍ عندَ التَّدبرِ، وَلِذلكَ عبَّرَ الشَّارِحُ وغيرُه في مشروطةِ الشَّرط بالمدخلية دون المنشيئةِ; فَلْيتأمل. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْمَعْنَيَيْن . . . إِلَخ) مَا ذكرَهُ الشَّارحُ هنا فرَّعَهُ السَّيِّدُ على كلام ذَكَرَهُ قبلَهُ فقالَ : إِنَّ المشروطة إذا اعتبرت بشرط الوصفِ; كانَ ضرورة نسبة (1) (قَوْلُهُ: والأمثلة ... إلخ) مثال الشارح لما تنفرد فيه المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني لم يكن وصف الموضوع لازماً لأفراده; إذ الكناية ليست لازمة لأفراد الكاتب في وقت من الأوقات، فكيف يكون المحمول ضرورياً لأفراد الموضوع في جميع الأوقات وهو تابع للموضوع، فإن أجيب بأن المراد بالكاتب الكاتب بالقوة لزم عدم صحة مثال ما تنفرد به المشروطة بالمعنى الأوّل ; لأنه حينئذ يكون لازماً في جميع الأوقات، فيكون من المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني، اللَّهمَّ إلا أن يقال : الكاتب في المثال الأوَّل بالقوة، وفي المثال الأخير بالفعل، وسيشير إلى ذلك المحشي. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
It has been said: the general conditional is the proposition in which the judgment is of necessity. Dusuqi: (His statement: 'And it has been said: ... etc.') i.e., it is used, and its summary is that the general conditional in the first sense is that in which the affirmation of the predicate to the subject is necessary, on condition of the existence of the description; whether that description is necessary for the individuals of the subject at some time or not. So the existence of the description is considered a condition for the necessity of the predication. As for the affirmation of the description for the individuals of the subject, sometimes it is necessary, sometimes possible. As for the conditional in the second sense, it is that in which the affirmation of the predicate to the subject is necessary in all times of the description of the subject, because that description is necessary for the individuals of the subject, whether the existence of the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication or not. So between the two conditionals there is a general-special relation (from one aspect): they coincide when the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication and that description is necessary for the individuals of the subject. The first meaning is exclusive when the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication and the description is not necessary for the individuals of the subject at any time. 'Attar: (His statement: 'And it has been said: the conditional ... etc.') This conditional is the one in which it is not considered that the description of its subject has an influence in the necessity of its predicate, as will come in his speech. And there remains a third meaning for it, which he mentioned in Sharh al-Matali', and it is the conditional whose subject's description is the source (munsha') of the necessity of its predicate. It is more specific than the conditional before it, i.e., the conditional of the condition which considers that the description of its subject has an influence in the necessity of its predicate, because if the description is the source of necessity, then it has influence in it, contrary to the reverse. For example, it is true that 'some hot thing is necessarily melting as long as it is hot,' i.e., on condition of heat, but it is not true because of heat, for if the essence of the hot thing had no influence in melting and heat were sufficient, then a stone would be melting. And there is a consideration in this: because the influence considered in the conditional of the condition, if it is meant as complete influence for the necessity of the predicate, then it is false in the example as well; and if it is meant as absolute influence, then it is said that the meaning of 'being the source' is also like that, so there is no difference. So the truth is that they return to one meaning upon reflection. And for that reason, the commentator (al-Sharh) and others expressed in the conditional of the condition by 'influence' (al-madkhaliyya) and not by 'being the source' (al-munsha'iyya); so let it be considered. (His statement: 'And the difference between the two meanings ... etc.') What the commentator mentioned here, al-Sayyid derived from his previous speech, saying: The conditional, if considered with the condition of the description, then the necessity of the predication is ... (1) (His statement: 'And the examples ... etc.') An example of what the commentator gives for that in which the conditional in the second meaning is exclusive: the description of the subject is not necessary for its individuals; for metaphor (kinaya) is not necessary for the individuals of 'writer' at any time, so how can the predicate be necessary for the individuals of the subject at all times while it is dependent on the subject? If it is answered that what is meant by 'writer' is the potential writer, then the example of what is exclusive to the first conditional would become unsound, because then it would be necessary at all times, thus falling under the second conditional. Unless it is said: the writer in the first example is potential, and in the last example is actual, and the commentator will point to that. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentators Dusuqi and 'Attar on the definition of the general conditional (mashrutah 'ammah). They begin by clarifying that the general conditional is sometimes said to be a proposition where the predication is necessary. Dusuqi summarizes two meanings. The first meaning: the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject on condition of the existence of a description, regardless of whether that description is necessarily true of the subject's individuals or not. The condition is only on the necessity of the predication, not on the description itself. The second meaning: the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject in all times when the description holds, because the description is necessary for the subject's individuals, irrespective of whether the description is a condition for the necessity. The difference is that in the first meaning the description is a condition for necessity but may not be necessary for the individuals; in the second meaning the description is necessary for the individuals but may not be a condition for necessity. They then explain that between these two there is a general-special relation (عموم من وجه): they coincide when the description is both a condition and necessary for the individuals; the first is exclusive when the description is a condition but not necessary; the second is exclusive when the description is necessary but not a condition. Next, 'Attar introduces a third meaning from Sharh al-Matali', where the description is the source (منشأ) of the necessity of the predicate, i.e., it is the cause. This third meaning is more specific than the 'conditional of condition' (which considers influence, not source). 'Attar gives an example: 'some hot thing is necessarily melting as long as it is hot' – this is true because of heat being a condition (شرط), but not because heat is the source, since if heat were the source, then any hot thing would melt, which is false for stones. However, 'Attar then critiques this: if 'influence' means complete influence, then the example fails; if it means absolute influence, then 'source' also means absolute influence, so there is no real difference. The correct view is that they reduce to one meaning. Therefore, the commentator (al-Sharh) and others used 'influence' instead of 'source'. Finally, a footnote from al-Sharnubi discusses an example problem: for the second meaning, the description 'writer' is not necessary for individuals (since a person may not be writing at a given time), so how can the predicate be necessary for the subject at all times? If 'writer' is potential, then the example for the first meaning fails. The resolution is that in the first example 'writer' is potential, in the second actual. This passage demonstrates the technical precision required in defining logical propositions, especially the role of conditions and necessity.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
وصف الموضوع
ضرورة النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dusuqi's first meaning of the general conditional, the description (wasf) is:
A) The cause of the necessity of the predication
B) A condition for the necessity, but not necessarily necessary for the subject's individuals
C) Necessarily true of all individuals of the subject
D) Both a condition and necessary for the subject

Key Takeaway
The general conditional has two distinct meanings: one where the description is a condition for necessity, and another where the description is a necessary attribute of the subject; they are related by a general-special from one aspect relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the proposition: 'Every human is rational as long as he is a human.' Identify which of Dusuqi's two meanings applies and explain why, using the criteria from the passage.

Given the proposition 'Some hot thing melts necessarily as long as it is hot', state whether this fits the first or second meaning or both, and give the reason from the passage.

Propositions
