The Third Meaning and Its Critique
'Attar critiques the third meaning and shows it reduces to the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate 'Attar's critique of the third meaning (source) and explain why the term 'influence' is preferred over 'source' in defining the conditional of condition.

Core Idea
The distinction between 'influence' and 'source' collapses; only two meanings remain.

'Attar introduces a third meaning of the general conditional from Sharh al-Matali', where the description is the source (munsha') of the necessity of the predicate. He then critiques this meaning, arguing that if 'influence' means complete influence, the example fails, and if it means absolute influence, it is equivalent to 'source'. Consequently, the third meaning reduces to the second. This refinement shows the precision required in logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third meaning
Find where 'Attar says: 'wa baqiya 'alayhi ma'nan thalithun laha, dhakarahu fi Sharh al-Matali', wa hiya al-mashrutatu allati wasfu mawdu'iha munsha'u darurati mahmuliha' – there is a third meaning where the description is the source of necessity.
This meaning is claimed to be more specific than the previous 'conditional of condition'.
2
Understand the critique
'Attar gives an example: 'some hot thing is necessarily melting as long as it is hot' – true under condition, but not because heat is the source, since stones are hot but don't melt. This shows that 'source' is not equivalent to 'influence' if influence means absolute. However, he then says: if 'influence' means complete influence, the example also fails; if absolute, then 'source' also means absolute, so no difference.
The key is the distinction between complete and absolute influence.
3
Analyze the resolution
'Attar concludes: 'fa al-haqqu ruju'uhuma li-ma'nan wahidin 'inda al-tadabbur' – the truth is they return to one meaning upon reflection. Therefore, the third meaning is redundant. Hence, the commentator uses 'madkhaliyya' (influence) not 'munsha'iyya' (source).
4
Apply to a new example
Consider a fiqh example: 'Prayer is obligatory on every Muslim as long as he is accountable (mukallaf).' Is accountability a source or condition? Using 'Attar's reasoning, if accountability is a source, then the obligation would arise from accountability itself. But since prayer is not obligatory on all accountable beings (e.g., jinn?), maybe it is a condition. The critique shows that 'source' adds nothing beyond 'influence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تُقالُ : المشروطة العامة على القضيَّةِ الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورةِ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : ... إلخ) أي : وقد تُطلَقُ ، وحاصله : أنَّ المشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ ؛ مَا كانَ ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا؛ بشرط وجودِ الوصفِ ؛ كان ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوعِ فِي وَقْتِ مِنَ الأوقاتِ أم لا، فَوُجودُ الوصْفِ فيها مُعتبرُ شَرطاً في ضرورة النِّسبةِ . وأمَّا ثبوتُ الوصْفِ لأفرادِ الموضوع ؛ فَتارةً يكون ضروريا، وتارة يكونُ ممكناً. وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني ; فَهِيَ مَا كانَ ثبوت المحمول للموضوع فيها ضروريا في جميع أوقاتِ وَصْفِ الموضوع؛ لِكَوْنِ ذلكَ الوصفِ لازماً لأفرادِ الموضوع ; كانَ وجود الوصفِ قيداً في ضرورةِ النّسبة أم لا . وحينئذ; فبين المشروطتين عمومٌ مِن وجه يجتمعان فيما إذا كان الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، وَكانَ ذلك الوصفُ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع، وينفرد المعنى الأوَّلُ فيما إذا كانَ الوصفُ شرطاً في ضرورة النّسبةِ، وَكانَ الوصفُ ليسَ لازماً لأفراد الموضوع في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقات. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ تُقَالُ : المَشْرُوْطَةُ ... إلخ) هذه المشروطة التي لم يعتبر فيها أن يكونَ لِوَصفِ موضوعِهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها كَمَا يأتي في كلامِهِ، وبقي عليهِ معنى ثالثُ لَهَا ; ذَكَرَهُ في شرح المطالع ، وَهِيَ المشروطةُ الَّتي وصفُ موضوعها منشأُ ضَرورة محمولها، وهي أخصُّ مِنَ المشروطة التي قبلها ; أي: مشروطة الشَّرطِ الَّتي اعتبر فيها أنْ يكونَ لِوصفِ مَوضعِهَا دخل في ضرورة محمولها ; لأنَّ الوصفَ إذا كانَ منشأ الضَّرورةِ; كانَ له دخل فيها ، بخلافِ العَكْسِ، فَإِنَّهُ يصدقُ في الذهن الحارّ بعضُ الحارّ ذائبُ بالضَّرورة ما دامَ حارا; أي: بشرط الحرارة، وَلَا يصدقُ لأجل الحرارةِ; لأنَّ ذاتَ الذَّهنِ لَوْ لم يكن له دخل في الذوبان وكانت الحرارة كافية لكانَ الحجرُ ذائباً، وفيه نظر; لأنَّ المدخليَّة المعتبرة في مشروطة الشَّرطِ إِنْ كانَت بمعنى المدخليَّةِ التَّامَّةِ لِضرورة المحمول; كَذَبَتْ في المثالِ أيضاً، وإن كانَتْ بمعنى مُطلق المدخليَّةِ فيُقالُ : المراد من المنشئيَّةِ أيضاً كذلك، فَلا فرق، فالحقُ رجوعُهُما لمعنى واحدٍ عندَ التَّدبرِ، وَلِذلكَ عبَّرَ الشَّارِحُ وغيرُه في مشروطةِ الشَّرط بالمدخلية دون المنشيئةِ; فَلْيتأمل. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْمَعْنَيَيْن . . . إِلَخ) مَا ذكرَهُ الشَّارحُ هنا فرَّعَهُ السَّيِّدُ على كلام ذَكَرَهُ قبلَهُ فقالَ : إِنَّ المشروطة إذا اعتبرت بشرط الوصفِ; كانَ ضرورة نسبة (1) (قَوْلُهُ: والأمثلة ... إلخ) مثال الشارح لما تنفرد فيه المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني لم يكن وصف الموضوع لازماً لأفراده; إذ الكناية ليست لازمة لأفراد الكاتب في وقت من الأوقات، فكيف يكون المحمول ضرورياً لأفراد الموضوع في جميع الأوقات وهو تابع للموضوع، فإن أجيب بأن المراد بالكاتب الكاتب بالقوة لزم عدم صحة مثال ما تنفرد به المشروطة بالمعنى الأوّل ; لأنه حينئذ يكون لازماً في جميع الأوقات، فيكون من المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني، اللَّهمَّ إلا أن يقال : الكاتب في المثال الأوَّل بالقوة، وفي المثال الأخير بالفعل، وسيشير إلى ذلك المحشي. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
It has been said: the general conditional is the proposition in which the judgment is of necessity. Dusuqi: (His statement: 'And it has been said: ... etc.') i.e., it is used, and its summary is that the general conditional in the first sense is that in which the affirmation of the predicate to the subject is necessary, on condition of the existence of the description; whether that description is necessary for the individuals of the subject at some time or not. So the existence of the description is considered a condition for the necessity of the predication. As for the affirmation of the description for the individuals of the subject, sometimes it is necessary, sometimes possible. As for the conditional in the second sense, it is that in which the affirmation of the predicate to the subject is necessary in all times of the description of the subject, because that description is necessary for the individuals of the subject, whether the existence of the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication or not. So between the two conditionals there is a general-special relation (from one aspect): they coincide when the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication and that description is necessary for the individuals of the subject. The first meaning is exclusive when the description is a condition for the necessity of the predication and the description is not necessary for the individuals of the subject at any time. 'Attar: (His statement: 'And it has been said: the conditional ... etc.') This conditional is the one in which it is not considered that the description of its subject has an influence in the necessity of its predicate, as will come in his speech. And there remains a third meaning for it, which he mentioned in Sharh al-Matali', and it is the conditional whose subject's description is the source (munsha') of the necessity of its predicate. It is more specific than the conditional before it, i.e., the conditional of the condition which considers that the description of its subject has an influence in the necessity of its predicate, because if the description is the source of necessity, then it has influence in it, contrary to the reverse. For example, it is true that 'some hot thing is necessarily melting as long as it is hot,' i.e., on condition of heat, but it is not true because of heat, for if the essence of the hot thing had no influence in melting and heat were sufficient, then a stone would be melting. And there is a consideration in this: because the influence considered in the conditional of the condition, if it is meant as complete influence for the necessity of the predicate, then it is false in the example as well; and if it is meant as absolute influence, then it is said that the meaning of 'being the source' is also like that, so there is no difference. So the truth is that they return to one meaning upon reflection. And for that reason, the commentator (al-Sharh) and others expressed in the conditional of the condition by 'influence' (al-madkhaliyya) and not by 'being the source' (al-munsha'iyya); so let it be considered. (His statement: 'And the difference between the two meanings ... etc.') What the commentator mentioned here, al-Sayyid derived from his previous speech, saying: The conditional, if considered with the condition of the description, then the necessity of the predication is ... (1) (His statement: 'And the examples ... etc.') An example of what the commentator gives for that in which the conditional in the second meaning is exclusive: the description of the subject is not necessary for its individuals; for metaphor (kinaya) is not necessary for the individuals of 'writer' at any time, so how can the predicate be necessary for the individuals of the subject at all times while it is dependent on the subject? If it is answered that what is meant by 'writer' is the potential writer, then the example of what is exclusive to the first conditional would become unsound, because then it would be necessary at all times, thus falling under the second conditional. Unless it is said: the writer in the first example is potential, and in the last example is actual, and the commentator will point to that. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentators Dusuqi and 'Attar on the definition of the general conditional (mashrutah 'ammah). They begin by clarifying that the general conditional is sometimes said to be a proposition where the predication is necessary. Dusuqi summarizes two meanings. The first meaning: the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject on condition of the existence of a description, regardless of whether that description is necessarily true of the subject's individuals or not. The condition is only on the necessity of the predication, not on the description itself. The second meaning: the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject in all times when the description holds, because the description is necessary for the subject's individuals, irrespective of whether the description is a condition for the necessity. The difference is that in the first meaning the description is a condition for necessity but may not be necessary for the individuals; in the second meaning the description is necessary for the individuals but may not be a condition for necessity. They then explain that between these two there is a general-special relation (عموم من وجه): they coincide when the description is both a condition and necessary for the individuals; the first is exclusive when the description is a condition but not necessary; the second is exclusive when the description is necessary but not a condition. Next, 'Attar introduces a third meaning from Sharh al-Matali', where the description is the source (منشأ) of the necessity of the predicate, i.e., it is the cause. This third meaning is more specific than the 'conditional of condition' (which considers influence, not source). 'Attar gives an example: 'some hot thing is necessarily melting as long as it is hot' – this is true because of heat being a condition (شرط), but not because heat is the source, since if heat were the source, then any hot thing would melt, which is false for stones. However, 'Attar then critiques this: if 'influence' means complete influence, then the example fails; if it means absolute influence, then 'source' also means absolute influence, so there is no real difference. The correct view is that they reduce to one meaning. Therefore, the commentator (al-Sharh) and others used 'influence' instead of 'source'. Finally, a footnote from al-Sharnubi discusses an example problem: for the second meaning, the description 'writer' is not necessary for individuals (since a person may not be writing at a given time), so how can the predicate be necessary for the subject at all times? If 'writer' is potential, then the example for the first meaning fails. The resolution is that in the first example 'writer' is potential, in the second actual. This passage demonstrates the technical precision required in defining logical propositions, especially the role of conditions and necessity.

Key Terms
المدخلية
المنشئية
المشروطة العامة
وصف الموضوع

Two Meanings of the Conditional Proposition
Definition and distinction of two meanings of conditional proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of the conditional proposition (al-mashruta) and classify a given example accordingly.

Core Idea
The conditional proposition has two meanings differing by whether the attribute causes necessity or is inherent.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial distinction in modal logic: the two meanings of the conditional proposition. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze temporal conditions in arguments, especially when evaluating essential versus accidental attributes. Let's walk through the distinction step by step.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the central terms
Identify the key terms: 'conditional proposition' (المشروطة) and 'necessity of predication' (ضرورة النسبة). The passage discusses how the attribute (وصف الموضوع) affects this necessity.
2
Understand the criterion
The crux is whether the attribute has a role (دخل) in realizing the necessity. If it has no role, it's the first meaning; if it has a role, it's the second meaning. This is the main distinction.
3
Examine the second meaning's condition
The second meaning holds only when the attribute does not cause the necessity (it's not a condition) but is necessary (لازم) for the subject's individuals. That is, the attribute is inherent, not accidental.
4
Analyze the example
The example 'Necessarily, every writer is a human as long as he is a writer' illustrates the second meaning. 'Writer' is an attribute inherent to the subject, not a condition for the necessity of 'human'. The necessity comes from the essence, not the attribute.
5
Test the dependency
If the attribute is necessary for its individuals, the predication inherits necessity; if the attribute is merely possible, the predication becomes possible, and the second meaning does not apply. This determines when a proposition qualifies as second-meaning mashruta.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرَفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. النسبة في جميع أوقات ثبوت الوصف للموضوع. والفرق بين المعنيين: أنَّ وصف الموضوع إن لم يكن له دخل في تحقق ضرورة النسبة؛ كان من المشروطة بالمعنى الأوّل، وإن كان له دخل؛ كان من المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني، وينفرد المعنى الثاني فيما إذا كان وجود الوصف ليس شرطاً في ضرورة النسبة، وكان الوصف لازماً لأفراد الموضوع. ومثال ما تنفرد به المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني: قولنا "بالضرورة كل كاتب إنسان ما دام كاتبا"، فإنه حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع في جميع أوقات وصف الموضوع. ثم إن ثبوت المحمول للموضوع في جميع الأوقات إنما يكون ضروريا إذا كان وصف الموضوع الحاصل بها ضروري الثبوت لأفراده. فإذا كان ضروري الثبوت لأفراده؛ كانت نسبة المحمول للموضوع تابعة له في الضرورة، وإن كان ثبوتها لأفراده ممكنا؛ كانت نسبة المحمول للموضوع التابعة له ممكنة، فلا تصدق المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني.
Section: On definition and its divisions. The predication occurs in all times of the attribute's subsistence for the subject. The difference between the two meanings is: that if the attribute of the subject has no role in the realization of the necessity of the predication, then it is of the conditional proposition in the first meaning; and if it has a role, then it is of the conditional proposition in the second meaning. And the second meaning is unique to the case where the existence of the attribute is not a condition in the necessity of the predication, and the attribute is necessary for the individuals of the subject. An example of what is unique to the conditional proposition according to the second meaning is our statement: 'Necessarily, every writer is a human as long as he is a writer,' for it rules therein the necessity of the predication of the predicate to the subject in all times of the attribute of the subject. Then, the subsistence of the predicate for the subject in all times is necessitous only if the attribute of the subject which occurs in these times is necessarily subsistent for its individuals. So if it is necessarily subsistent for its individuals, then the predication of the predicate to the subject is dependent on it in necessity; and if its subsistence for its individuals is possible, then the dependent predication of the predicate to the subject is possible, so the conditional proposition according to the second meaning is not true.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two meanings of the conditional proposition (المشروطة) in logic. The author begins by stating the section title: 'On definition and its divisions.' He then identifies the first key point: 'The predication occurs in all times of the attribute's subsistence for the subject.' This sets up the context for distinguishing two interpretations. The difference between the two meanings is as follows: if the attribute (وصف) of the subject has no role in bringing about the necessity of the predication (ضرورة النسبة), then the proposition belongs to the first meaning of the conditional proposition. Conversely, if the attribute does have a role in that necessity, it belongs to the second meaning. The second meaning is specifically applicable when the existence of the attribute is not a condition for the necessity, yet the attribute is essential and permanent (لازم) for the individuals of the subject. For example, the statement 'Necessarily, every writer is a human as long as he is a writer' illustrates the second meaning: the necessity of the predication (human) holds in all times when the subject (writer) exhibits the attribute of writing, but the attribute itself is not a condition for the necessity; rather, being human is inherent to the writer. The author then explains that for the predication to be necessary in all times, the attribute itself must be necessarily true of the subject's individuals. If the attribute is necessary for them, the predication inherits that necessity; if the attribute is only possible, the dependent predication becomes possible, disqualifying the conditional proposition in the second sense. This distinction is crucial for understanding how temporal conditions affect the modality of propositions in logic, especially in discussions of essential versus accidental attributes.

Key Terms
المشروطة
ضرورة النسبة
وصف الموضوع
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the conditional proposition (المشروطة) according to the second meaning?
A) 'Every writer is a human as long as he is a writer.'
B) 'Every man is an animal as long as he is rational.'
C) 'Every moving thing is changing as long as it moves.'
D) 'Every entity is either existent or non-existent.'

Key Takeaway
The two meanings of al-mashruta are distinguished by whether the attribute plays a role in the necessity of predication, with the second meaning requiring the attribute to be necessary for the subject's individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition into the first or second meaning of al-mashruta: 'Every human is an animal as long as he is a human.' Explain your reasoning using the passage's criteria.
?
Construct an original example of a proposition that fits the first meaning and one that fits the second meaning, using concepts from fiqh (e.g., 'Every prayer is obligatory as long as it is valid').

Truth Conditions of the Restricted Universal Proposition
Author explains truth conditions of restricted universal in second sense with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the first and second sense of the restricted universal proposition and determine when each is true.

Core Idea
The restricted universal is true in the second sense when the description merely marks time, not a condition of necessity.

In logic, propositions can be restricted by temporal conditions. The 'restricted universal' (al-mashruta al-'amma) is a universal conditional where the predicate is affirmed of the subject only during the presence of a description. But there are two senses: one where the description is a condition of the necessity itself, and one where it only indicates the time. This lesson unpacks the second sense using a concrete example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses
The author distinguishes between the first sense (description is a condition of necessity) and the second sense (description only indicates the time of judgment). Note the key phrase: 'تصدق المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني دون الأول'.
This distinction is crucial for understanding truth conditions of temporal propositions.
2
Parse the example
Examine 'بالضرورة كل كاتب إنسان ما دام كاتباً'. The subject is 'writer', the description is 'being a writer', the predicate is 'human'. The proposition asserts necessity of the predicate for the subject during the time the subject is described as a writer.
The phrase 'ما دام' (as long as) introduces the temporal condition.
3
Test the necessity
Determine whether the predicate holds necessarily for the subject at all times or only conditionally. Here, 'human' is necessarily true of the writer's essence at all times, not just while writing. The necessity does not depend on the description.
If the predicate were only necessary during the description, it would be the first sense.
4
Conclude the sense
Since the description is not a condition of the necessity, the proposition is true in the second sense. The description only marks the timeframe when the proposition is asserted.
The author adds a further nuance: if the description does play a role, then either it is necessary for the subject at some time or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرورةِ النِّسبةِ؛ صدقتِ المشروطة العامَّةُ بالمعنى الثَّاني دونَ الأوَّل، كقولنا: بالضرورة كلُّ كاتب إنسان ما دام كاتباً، فإنَّه حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع في جميع أوقاتِ وصف الموضوع. فإنَّ ثبوت الإنسانيَّةِ لذاتِ الكاتب ضروري في جميع أوقات وصفه بالكتابة، لكن ليس ضروريا له بشرط وصفِ الكتابة، فتصدقُ المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني دونَ الأوَّل. وإن كان لوصف الموضوع دخل في تحقق ضرورةِ النِّسبة، فلا يخلو إمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الوصفُ ضروريًا لذات الموضوع في وقت من الأوقات، أو لا يكون.
The necessity of the relation; the universal conditional proposition is true in the second meaning, not the first, as in our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer is a human as long as he is a writer.' For it judges in it the necessity of affirming the predicate to the subject in all times of the subject's description. For the affirmation of humanity to the essence of the writer is necessary in all times of his description by writing, but it is not necessary for him on the condition of the description of writing. So the conditional proposition is true in the second meaning, not the first. And if the description of the subject has a role in the realization of the necessity of the relation, then it is either that description is necessary for the essence of the subject at some time or not.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions under which the 'restricted universal proposition' (al-mashruta al-'amma) is true in its second sense as opposed to the first. The author begins by stating that the proposition is true in the second sense, giving the example: 'Necessarily, every writer is a human as long as he is a writer.' This example asserts that being human is necessary for any writer at every time that they are writing. However, this necessity does not depend on the condition of being a writer; rather, the description (writing) merely indicates the times during which the necessity holds. The author then distinguishes between two cases: if the description has a role in realizing the necessity, then either that description is necessary for the subject's essence (e.g., being human is necessary for a writer's essence regardless) or it is not. The point is to clarify when the restricted universal is true without the description being a condition of the necessity itself, a key distinction in logic for analyzing temporal propositions.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
ضرورة النسبة
وصف الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Necessarily, every writer is a human as long as he is a writer', why is the proposition true in the second sense but not the first?
A) Because the predicate 'human' is necessary for the subject 'writer' only during the time of writing.
B) Because the necessity of the predicate is conditional upon the subject being a writer.
C) Because the predicate holds for the subject at all times, but the description 'writer' is not a condition of the necessity.
D) Because the proposition is not actually necessary but only contingent.

Key Takeaway
The restricted universal proposition is true in the second sense when the predicate holds necessarily for the subject during the time of the description, but the description is not a condition of that necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a restricted universal proposition in the second sense about a ruling in fiqh (e.g., 'Every fasting person must abstain from food as long as he is aware of the time') and explain why it is the second sense.
?
Find a proposition from a matn of Islamic logic (e.g., from Tadhheeb al-Mantiq) that is a restricted universal and determine whether it is first or second sense.

Propositions

5 min
Two Meanings of the Conditional Proposition
Dassuqi explains when the conditional proposition is true with or without the condition of the subject's description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of the conditional proposition (mashruta) and determine which meaning applies to a given proposition based on the role of the subject's description.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition's truth depends on whether the predicate is necessary due to the subject's description.

In this passage, Dassuqi unpacks the two meanings of the conditional proposition (qadiyya mashruta) found in the Tadhheeb tradition. The distinction hinges on whether the predicate's necessity is tied to a specific description of the subject. This precision is crucial for evaluating logical arguments in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its description
Find the subject (e.g., 'writer') and the description (e.g., 'writing') that conditions the proposition. In the example 'Every writer is human,' the description is 'writer' (the act of writing).
The description is often expressed as a condition in the proposition.
2
Determine if the predicate is necessary without the description
Check if the predicate (e.g., 'human') is always true of the subject regardless of the description. If yes, the proposition would be true in the first meaning (without condition). If not, it falls under the second meaning (with condition).
First meaning: predicate is necessary in all times the subject exists. Second meaning: predicate is necessary only when the description holds.
3
Check if the description itself is necessary at some time
If the description is necessarily true of the subject at some time (e.g., eclipse for the moon), then the proposition may be true in both meanings. Otherwise, it is true only in the second meaning.
This is the key distinction from the 'eclipsed' example.
4
Apply to a new proposition
Take a proposition like 'Every judge is just while judging.' The description 'judging' is not always true of the judge, so the proposition is true only in the second meaning (with condition).
Practice with other examples from logic or fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
فإنَّ ثبوت الإنسانيَّةِ لذاتِ الكاتب ضروري في جميع أوقات وصفيه بالكتابة، لكن ليس ضروريا له بشرط وصفِ الكتابة، فتصدقُ المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني دونَ الأوَّل.
وإِنْ كانَ لوَصْفِ الموضوع دخل في تحقق ضرورةِ النِّسبة، فلا يخلو إمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الوصفُ ضروريًا لذات الموضوع في وقت من الأوقات، أو لا يكون.
فإن كان ضروريا في وقتٍ من الأوقات؛ صدقتِ المشروطة بالمعنييْنِ، كَقَولِنَا : كلُّ مُنخَسِفَ مُظلِمٌ ما دام مُنخَسِفاً))، سواءٌ أُريدَ بشرط كونِه مُنخَسِفاً ، أو بلا اعتبار الاشتراط .
Chapter: On the Conditional and its Divisions. For indeed, the existence of humanity for the essence of the writer is necessary in all times of his description with writing, but it is not necessary for him with the condition of the description of writing. So the conditional proposition is true with the second meaning and not the first. And if the description of the subject has an involvement in the realization of the necessity of the predication, then it is either that that description is necessary for the essence of the subject at some time, or it is not. For if it is necessary at some time, then the conditional proposition is true with both meanings, like our saying: 'Everything that is eclipsed is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' whether one intends with the condition of its being eclipsed or without consideration of the condition.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage from the Tadhheeb Sharh, with commentary by Dassuqi, defines the two meanings of the conditional proposition (qadiyya mashruta). The discussion centers on the relationship between the subject's description (like 'writer') and the predicate (like 'human'). The commentator explains that the proposition 'Every writer is human' is true when we consider the description 'writer' as a condition for the necessity of humanity. However, it is not true without that condition, because humanity is necessary for the subject in all times, not specifically when he is a writer. The passage then introduces a second example, 'Everything eclipsed is dark,' which is true both with and without the condition, because the eclipse itself is a necessary attribute at the time it occurs. This illustrates the logical precision of the mashruta propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway
The conditional proposition has two meanings: one without condition on the subject's description (first) and one with condition (second); the choice depends on whether the predicate's necessity is tied to the description.

Distinguishing the Two Meanings of Conditional Propositions
Dasuqi and 'Attar refine the distinction between two types of conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional proposition into its first or second meaning by assessing whether the description is necessary for the subject at some time and apply this to examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The second meaning demands necessity; the first only requires concomitant truth during the description.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction in Islamic logic: the difference between 'conditional in the first meaning' and 'conditional in the second meaning' (al-mashrutah bi al-ma'nayn). The commentators Dasuqi and 'Attar refine this distinction by explaining the role of necessity. They also critique an earlier example and clarify the relationship between the two meanings. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to evaluate conditional statements in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject, description, and predicate
In a conditional proposition like 'Every S described by D is P as long as it is described by D', isolate the subject (S), the description (D), and the predicate (P). For example: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed' – subject: moon, description: eclipsed, predicate: dark.
2
Assess whether the description is necessary for the subject at some time
Ask: Is it possible for the subject to exist without this description at some other time, or is it impossible for the subject to exist without it during the time the description applies? If the description is necessary for the subject at some time, the conditional may be true in the second meaning. If it is merely possible, only the first meaning applies.
Check if the description entails the predicate necessarily.
3
Classify the proposition accordingly
If the description is necessary for the subject during the time it applies, the conditional is true in both meanings (second meaning holds). If the description is not necessary (i.e., possible), the conditional is only true in the first meaning. Remember the moon example: eclipsing is necessary for the moon at the time of interposition, so both meanings hold.
4
Apply to the criticized example
Dasuqi points out that 'Every writer is a human' is not a good example for the exclusive second meaning because writing is not necessary for a human at any time. A better example is 'Every rational is a human' (if we take 'rational' as essential). This clarifies the necessity condition.
Potential writer (al-katib bi al-quwwa) might be different.

الدسوقي
الموضوع، ضروريَّةٌ في جميع أوقات هذا الوصف، وَهِيَ أوقـات كـونـه ضروريـا لـذاتِ فتصدقُ المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني؛ بخلافِ ما إذا لم يكن ضروريا لهُ في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقاتِ؛ بل كانَ مُمكناً، فإنَّ نسبة المحمول للموضوع التَّابعةِ لَهُ تكونُ مُمكنةٌ، فَلَا تصدقُ المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني، بل بالمعنى الأول.

(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ مُنْخَسِف... إلخ) الانحسافُ : ذَهابُ ضَوء القمرِ؛ أي: الَّذِي يستفادُ مِن نورِ الشَّمس، وذلكَ يكونُ إذا كانَتِ الشَّمسُ تحت الأرض، والقمرُ فوق

مَا كانَ إنساناً، بخلافها من جانبِ الذَّاتِ المخصوصة؛ فإِنَّكَ تقولُ : لو لم يكن الموضوعُ الذَّات المخصوصة ؛ أي: زيدٌ وعَمْرُو إلى آخر الأفراد؛ مَا كانَ إنساناً .
(قَوْلُهُ : صَدَقَتِ الْمَشْرُوْطَةُ بِالمَعْنَتَيْنِ وجه صدقهما فيما ذكرَ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا كَانَ لِوصفِ الموضوع دخل في ضرورة المحمول ؛ كانَ ذلكَ مُصححاً لِكونِها مَشروطةٌ الطَّرفِ، فَإِنَّ الظَّرف لا يوجبُ كونَ المظروفِ ضروريًّا؛ إلا إذا كان هو ضروريا في نفسه

الدسوقي
الأرضِ؛ لأنَّ نور القمرِ مُستفادٌ مِن نورِ الشَّمس، فإذا حالَتِ الأَرضُ بينَهُما ؛ ذهب نورُ القمرِ، وَصارَ القمرُ مُظلماً لا ضوء فيهِ ؛ أي: فيعودُ إلى حالتِهِ الأُوْلَى . وقولُه : كلُّ مُنخسف؛ أي: كلُّ فردٍ مِنَ الأفرادِ الَّتي تقبل الانخسافَ مُظلم ؛ أي : فإنَّ الانخسافَ لِلقمر ضروري عندَهُم ، وَلَا بُدَّ منه وقت حيلولة الأرض بينَهُ وبينَ الشَّمس، وهو غير ضروري لهُ في غيرِ ذلك الوقت . فَبِاعتبارِ أَنَّهُ ضروريٌّ لِذَاتِ الموضوع في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقاتِ؛ تصدقُ المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني، وباعتبارِ أَنَّهُ ليس ضروريا لهُ في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقاتِ؛ تصدقُ بالمعنى الأول.
والحاصلُ : أنَّ الحكماء يقولونَ: إِنَّ جِرْمَ القمرِ مُظلم، وإِنَّ نورَهُ مُستفادٌ مِن نورِ الشَّمسِ، وَلَا بُدَّ لِلقمرِ مِن حلولِهِ في درجةٍ مِنَ الفلكِ؛ بحيثُ تحولُ الأَرضُ بينَهُ وبينَ الشَّمسِ، فَيذهبُ ما فيه مِنَ النُّورِ ، وهو المسمَّى بالانخسافِ، وَإِذَا لَمْ تَحُلِ الأَرضُ بينَهُ وبينَ الشَّمس ؛ فَلَا يكونُ مُنخسفاً، فالانخسافُ عندَهُم أمرُ ضروري وقتَ الحيلولة، وغير ضروري في غير ذلك الوقت .
العطار
نسبة الإظلام إلى مجموع القمرِ ووصف الانخسافِ كانَ ضروريا له، وأنَّ نِسبته إلى ذاتِ القمرِ كَانَ أيضاً ضَروريَّا لهُ في وقتِ الانخسافِ ؛ لأنَّ القمر في ذلك الوقتِ يستحيلُ وجودُهُ بِلا انخسافٍ على ما زعمُوا، فَذاتُ القمرِ مُستلزمةٌ للمجموع من ذاته ووصف الانخسافِ، وهذا المجموعُ مُستلزم للإظلام، ومُستلزمُ المستلزم مُستلزم، فذاتُ القمرِ في ذلك الوقتِ ؛ مُستلزم للإظلام، فظهر بذلكَ أَنَّ النِّسبة بين معنى المشروطة هي العمومُ مِن ،وجه، وهذا كلامٌ مُحقَّقُ قد أخطأ فيه كثيرونَ؛ زاعمينَ أنَّ النِّسبة بينَهُما العمومُ مُطلقاً؛ لأنَّ ما دامَ الوصفُ أعم مطلقاً ا.هـ. قال مَنشأ عبد الحكيم : زَعْمِهِم؛ إمَّا عدمُ الفرق بينَ الشَّرطِ والطَّرفِ، وإِمَّا بالنَّظر إلى أنَّ الثَّبوت في وقتِ الوصفِ لا بُدَّ لهُ مِن عِلَّةٍ، فَثبوت الوصف في ذلك الوقتِ ضَروري، وقد عرفتَ أنَّ النَّظر في النِّسبة إلى مُجرَّدِ مفهوم القضيَّةِ .ا.هـ.
If it is necessary at some time, then the conditional is true in both meanings, as in our saying: 'Every eclipsed [moon] is dark as long as it is eclipsed', whether the condition of its being eclipsed is intended, or without considering the condition. Al-Dasuqi: [If the attribute is] necessary in all times of this description, and they are the times of its being necessary to the essence, then the conditional is true in the second meaning; unlike if it is not necessary to it at any time, but rather possible, then the relationship of the predicate to the subject dependent on it is possible, so the conditional is not true in the second meaning, but only in the first. And if you know this, you know that the commentator's example for the exclusive second meaning with 'Every writer is a human' has something in it, because the establishment of writing to individuals of the writer (meaning Zayd and 'Amr...etc.) is not necessary at any time, so it is better to exemplify with 'Every rational is a human', unless by 'writer' is meant the potential writer. As for his saying in the last example: 'Every writer is a mover of fingers', what is meant by 'writer' here is the actual writer, so reflect. His saying: 'necessary' i.e., obligatory and necessary to the subject's essence. His saying: 'Every eclipsed...etc.' Eclipsing is the disappearance of the moon's light, i.e., that which is derived from the sun's light, and that occurs when the sun is under the earth and the moon above. Al-'Attar: What was human, unlike it from the side of the particular essence; for you say: if the subject were not the particular essence (i.e., Zayd and 'Amr up to the other individuals), it would not be human. His saying: 'The conditional is true in both meanings' – the aspect of their truth in what was mentioned is that when the subject's description has a role in the necessity of the predicate, that makes it correct for it to be a conditional of the side, for the circumstance does not necessitate the circumstanced being necessary unless it is itself necessary. By meaning: His saying: 'without considering the condition' – al-Sayyid added: after this, based on the fact that eclipsing is necessary for the moon at a specific time, which is the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. For: Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions. As for the truth of the conditional in the first meaning, it is because the establishment of darkness is necessary to the essence of the subject (i.e., the moon) under the condition of its description, which is eclipsing. Al-Dasuqi: The earth; because the moon's light is derived from the sun's light, so when the earth interposes between them, the moon's light goes away, and the moon becomes dark with no light, i.e., it returns to its original state. His saying: 'Every eclipsed' i.e., every individual among the individuals that accept eclipsing is dark; i.e., eclipsing for the moon is necessary in their view, and it must occur at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, and it is not necessary for it at other times. So considering that it is necessary for the essence of the subject at some time, the conditional is true in the second meaning; and considering that it is not necessary for it at any time, it is true in the first meaning. The conclusion: The philosophers say that the moon's body is dark, and its light is derived from the sun's light, and the moon must pass into a degree of the firmament such that the earth interposes between it and the sun, so the light that is in it goes away, which is called eclipsing; and if the earth does not interpose between it and the sun, then it is not eclipsed. So eclipsing in their view is a necessary matter at the time of interposition, and not necessary at other times. Al-'Attar: The relationship of darkness to the totality of the moon and the description of eclipsing was necessary to it, and its relationship to the essence of the moon was also necessary to it at the time of eclipsing, because the moon at that time cannot exist without eclipsing according to their claim; so the essence of the moon entails the composite of itself and the description of eclipsing, and this composite entails darkness, and the entailer of the entailer is an entailer, so the essence of the moon at that time entails darkness. Thus it became clear that the relationship between the two meanings of the conditional is 'common from some aspect', and this is a verified statement about which many have erred, claiming that the relationship between them is 'absolute commonality', because as long as the description is absolutely more general. End quote. Manasha' 'Abd al-Hakim said: Their claim is either the failure to distinguish between condition and side, or the consideration that the establishment at the time of the description must have a cause, so the establishment of the description at that time is necessary, and you have known that the consideration in the relationship is to the mere concept of the proposition. End quote.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the super-commentary (hashiya) tradition, in which the commentators Dasuqi and 'Attar clarify the distinction between the two meanings of conditional propositions (المشروطة بالمعنى الأول والثاني). The discussion centers on a specific example: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed.' The first meaning of the conditional is simply that the predicate holds whenever the subject has the attribute; the second meaning adds that the predicate is necessary for the subject during the time of that attribute. The commentators start by stating that if the attribute is necessary for the subject at some time, the conditional is true in both meanings. They contrast this with cases where the attribute is merely possible, in which case the conditional is only true in the first meaning. Dasuqi then critiques an earlier commentator's example ('Every writer is a human') for the exclusive second meaning, arguing that 'writer' is not necessary for a person. He suggests a better example: 'Every rational is a human.' This shows the distinction between a necessary attribute (nature) and a contingent one (writing). The discussion then delves into the philosophical background: the moon's light is borrowed from the sun, and eclipsing occurs only when the earth interposes. Thus, the attribute 'eclipsed' is necessary for the moon at the time of interposition but not always. The conditional in the first meaning is true because the moon is dark specifically when it is eclipsed; the second meaning is true because the moon's essence at that time necessitates darkness (since darkness is a consequence of being eclipsed, which is necessary in that moment). 'Attar further explains that the relationship between the two meanings is 'common from some aspect' (العموم من وجه), meaning they overlap under certain conditions, but they are not identical or one absolutely more general. He corrects those who mistakenly think the relationship is 'absolute commonality', because the description (eclipsing) is not always present with the subject. The passage concludes with a note from Manasha' 'Abd al-Hakim, who attributes the error to confusing the condition (شرط) with the side (طرف), or to thinking that the predicate must have a cause during the time of the description. The key takeaway is that logical analysis must consider only the concept of the proposition, not external causes.

Key Terms
المشروطة
المعنى الأول
المعنى الثاني
ضروري
الانخساف

C) Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition in the second meaning requires that the description be necessary for the subject during the time of description; otherwise, only the first meaning applies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Senses of Conditional Proposition (Mashruta)
Author explains two senses of conditional with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the first and second senses of a conditional proposition and apply the distinction to examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
First sense: predicate necessary under condition; second sense: predicate necessary at times of condition.

In this passage, the author clarifies two distinct meanings of a conditional (mashruta) proposition, a key tool in Islamic logic. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting conditional statements in logical proofs and theological arguments. We will carefully unpack the definitions and the illuminating examples of the lunar eclipse and the writer to see exactly how the two senses differ and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage closely to extract the definitions of the first and second sense. In the first sense, the predicate is necessary for the essence of the subject under the condition of the qualification. In the second sense, the predicate is necessary for the subject in the times of the qualification.
2
Understand the role of the condition in each sense
Note that both senses involve a condition, but they differ in scope. The first sense requires necessity whenever the condition holds (even if the condition itself is never necessary). The second sense requires necessity in all times when the condition actually occurs, implying the condition itself must be necessary at some time.
3
Analyze the lunar eclipse example
The author gives 'The moon is eclipsed' as a conditional. In both senses, darkness is necessary for the moon under the condition of eclipse. The second sense holds because the eclipse itself is necessary for the moon at the time of interposition.
The condition (eclipse) occurs at specific times and is unavoidable then.
4
Analyze the writing example
Consider 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Here, movement is necessary under the condition of writing (first sense true). But writing is not necessary for the subject at any time – a person can stop being a writer. Hence the second sense fails.
The condition (writing) is accidental, not necessary at any moment.
5
Synthesize the distinction
Summarise: The second sense requires that the condition itself be necessary for the subject at some time; if not, only the first sense holds. This distinction prevents confusion when evaluating conditional statements in logical and theological arguments.

فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ وَأَقْسَامِهَا. أَمَّا صِدْقُ الْمَشْرُوطَةِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَوَّلِ، فَلِأَنَّ ثُبُوتَ الْإِظْلَامِ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِذَاتِ الْمَوْضُوعِ - أَيْ: الْقَمَرِ - بِشَرْطِ وَصْفِهِ، وَهُوَ الِانْخِسَافُ. وَأَمَّا صِدْقُهَا بِالْمَعْنَى الثَّانِي، فَلِأَنَّ ثُبُوتَ الْإِظْلَامِ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِلْقَمَرِ فِي أَوْقَاتِ وَصْفِهِ، أَيِ: الِانْخِسَافِ. وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ وَصْفُ الْمَوْضُوعِ ضَرُورِيًّا لِذَاتِ الْمَوْضُوعِ فِي وَقْتٍ مَا، صَدَقَتِ الْمَشْرُوطَةُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَوَّلِ دُونَ الثَّانِي، كَقَوْلِنَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ: كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الْأَصَابِعِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِبًا، فَإِنَّ ثُبُوتَ التَّحَرُّكِ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِذَاتِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، أَيْ: أَفْرَادِ الْكَاتِبِ بِشَرْطِ وَصْفِهِ، وَهُوَ الْكِتَابَةُ، وَلَكِنْ لَيْسَ فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتِ الْوَصْفِ؛ إِذِ الْوَصْفُ - وَهُوَ الْكِتَابَةُ - لَيْسَ ضَرُورِيًّا لَهُ فِي وَقْتٍ مِنَ الْأَوْقَاتِ. وَأُجِيبَ بِالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الْمِثَالَيْنِ: فَإِنَّ الْكِتَابَةَ لَيْسَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةً فِي وَقْتٍ مِنَ الْأَوْقَاتِ، بِخِلَافِ الِانْخِسَافِ، فَإِنَّهُ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِلْقَمَرِ وَقْتَ الْحِيلُولَةِ، فَإِنَّهُ يَسْتَحِيلُ عِنْدَهُمْ أَنْ يُوجَدَ الْقَمَرُ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ غَيْرَ مُنْخَسِفٍ، فَثَبَتَ أَنَّ الْإِظْلَامَ ضَرُورِيٌّ فِي وَقْتِ الِانْخِسَافِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، أَيْ: وَقْتَ الْحِيلُولَةِ.
Chapter on the conditional and its divisions. As for the truth of the conditional in the first sense, it is because the establishment of darkness is necessary for the essence of the subject—i.e., the moon—under the condition of its description, which is the eclipse. As for its truth in the second sense, it is because the establishment of darkness is necessary for the moon in the times of its description, i.e., the eclipse. And if the description of the subject is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, then the conditional is true in the first sense without the second, like our necessary statement: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer,' for the establishment of movement is necessary for the essence of the subject, i.e., individuals of the writer, under the condition of its description, which is writing, but not in all times of the description, because the description—i.e., writing—is not necessary for it at any time. And it was answered by the difference between the two examples: indeed, writing is not necessary at any time, unlike the eclipse, for it is necessary for the moon at the time of interposition, since according to them it is impossible for the moon to be found at that time not eclipsed. So it is established that darkness is necessary at the time of eclipse necessarily, i.e., the time of interposition.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage defines two senses of a conditional (mashruta) proposition. In the first sense, the predicate is necessary for the subject under the condition of the qualification, meaning that whenever the subject has the qualification, the predicate holds. In the second sense, the predicate is necessary for the subject specifically at the times when the qualification holds, but not necessarily at all times. The author illustrates with the moon: darkness is necessary for the moon under the condition of eclipse (first sense), and also necessary for the moon at times of eclipse (second sense). Then contrasts with the example of a writer: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Here, movement is necessary for the writer under the condition of writing (first sense), but writing is not necessary for the writer at any time—a person can be a writer at one moment and not at another—so the second sense does not hold. The commentary resolves an apparent inconsistency by noting that in the moon example, the eclipse (the condition) is itself necessary for the moon at certain times (when the earth interposes), whereas writing is never necessary for a writer. Thus the two examples differ in whether the condition itself can be necessary for the subject at some time, affecting the truth of the second sense.

B) Because movement is not necessary for the writer.
C) Because the writer is not always writing.
D) Because the first sense requires the condition to be necessary.

Key Takeaway
The second sense of a conditional requires that the condition itself be necessary for the subject at some time, otherwise only the first sense holds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Propositions

Distinguishing the Two Meanings of Conditional Propositions
Author explains truth conditions for two meanings of conditional propositions with examples and glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of the conditional proposition (mashruta) and determine which meaning applies to a given example based on whether the condition is necessary for the subject at any time.

Core Idea
A conditional's second meaning requires the condition to be necessary for the subject itself at some time.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions (al-mashruta) have two distinct truth conditions. The first meaning requires the predicate to be necessary for the subject under the condition; the second meaning requires the predicate to be necessary for the subject itself during the time of the condition. The difference hinges on whether the condition (waṣf) is ever necessary for the subject. We will explore this with examples from the commentary tradition, including lunar eclipse and writing, and see how this tool is applied in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's form
Find the subject, predicate, and the condition (sharṭ) expressed by 'as long as' or 'whenever'. For example, in 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer,' the condition is 'being a writer'.
2
Check necessity of the condition for the subject at any time
Determine whether the condition (wasf) is ever necessary for the subject. Is it possible for the subject to exist without the condition at some time? For 'writer,' the condition 'writing' is never necessary for the person; for 'moon,' the condition 'eclipse' is necessary at the time of obstruction.
This step decides which meaning can be true.
3
Apply the first meaning test
Under the condition, is the predicate necessary for the subject? For 'writer during writing,' moving fingers is necessary. So the first meaning holds.
First meaning: if the condition is satisfied, the predicate must hold.
4
Apply the second meaning test
Is the predicate necessary for the subject itself at the times when the condition applies? That requires the condition to be necessary for the subject. For the moon during eclipse, darkness is necessary because the moon is necessarily eclipsed at that time. For the writer, writing is not necessary at any time, so the predicate (movement) cannot be necessary for the subject itself during writing.
Second meaning: the predicate must be necessary for the subject's essence (not just under condition) during the condition.
5
Conclude which meaning(s) apply
If the condition is necessary for the subject at some time, the second meaning can be true (and the first too). If the condition is never necessary, only the first meaning is true.
For the writer example, only the first meaning is true; for the eclipse example, both meanings are true.

وأمَّا صدقها بالمعنى الثَّاني؛ فَلِأنَّ ثبوت الإظلام ضروري للقمر في أوقات وصفه؛ أي: الانخساف. وإن لم يكن وصفُ الموضوع ضروريا لذات الموضوع في وقت ما، صدقتِ المشروطة بالمعنى الأوَّل دونَ الثَّاني، كقولنا بالضَّرورة: كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً، فإنَّ ثبوت التَّحرُكِ ضروري لذاتِ الموضوع؛ أي: أفرادِ الكاتب بشرط وصفه، وهو الكتابة، ولكن ليس أوقاتِ الوصف؛ إذ الوصف - وهو الكتابة - ليس ضروريا له في جميعِ الوقت. (العطار: قوله: ولكن ليس ضَرُوْرِيًّا لَهُ فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتِ الْوَصْفِ الأَوْلَى: في وقتٍ مِن أوقاتِ الوصف؛ لأنَّه ليس ضروريًا في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقات، وإلا؛ صدقتِ المشروطة بالمعنى الثَّاني. حاصلُهُ أَنَّهُ إِذا اعتُبِرَ الوصفُ شَرطاً في قولنا: كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً؛ كَانَتْ حركة الأصابع ضَروريَّةٌ لِلذَّاتِ المقيَّدة بهذا الوصفِ على ما تقدَّمَ في كلامِ السَّيِّدِ، فَإِن اعْتُبِرَ ظرفاً؛ انتَفَتْ ضَروريَّةُ حركة الأصابع؛ لأنَّ الوصفَ - وهو الكتابة - ليس ضروريا لذات الموضوع في وقتٍ من الأوقات، فالتَّحرُّك التَّابِعُ للكتابة لا يكون ضروريا لذات الموضوع مطلقاً، فتصدقُ المشروطة بالمعنى الأوَّل دونَ الثَّاني. (الدسوقي: قوله: مطلقاً) أي: في وقتٍ من الأوقات. واعلمْ أنَّ بينَ الضَّروريَّةِ المطلقة والمشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ عموماً وخصوصاً من وجه؛ باعتبارِ التَّحقُّقِ لا باعتبار المفهوم؛ لِتَبايُنهما، فيجتمعان في: كلُّ مُنخسفٍ مُظلم وقتَ الحيلولة، وتنفردُ الضَّروريَّةُ المطلقة في: كلُّ إنسان حيوان. (العطار: قوله: لذات الموضوع؛ أي: أفرادِهِ في وقتٍ مِنَ الأوقاتِ، فالتَّحرُّكُ التَّابِع للكتابة لا يكونُ ضروريًّا، فَلِذلكَ قال: إذ الوصفُ ... إلخ؛ فعلى تقديرِهِ جعل الوصفَ مُجُزءاً؛ فَالمراد من الكاتب خُصوص الأفراد بدونِ أنْ يجعل الوصفَ الَّذي هو مَنشأُ الضَّرورةِ قيداً فيها، ومعلوم أنَّ هذهِ الأفراد ليسَتِ الكتابةُ ضروريَّةً لَها، وبهذا تعلمُ أَنَّهُ لا وجهَ لِقَوْلِ المحنِّي: لو صح هذا الدَّليلُ؛ دلَّ على كذب إرادة المعنى الأوَّلِ لِجريانه فيه بعينه إلى آخر ما قالَ. (الدسوقي: قوله: لِذَاتِ الْمَوْضُوْعِ) أي: الأفرادُ مِن حيثُ هِيَ، فَلَا يُنافي ضرورة ثبوتِهِ لبعض الأفرادِ بسبب الارتعاش مثلاً، وقولُهُ: (مطلقاً)؛ قال العصامُ: هوَ تَعميم الضَّرورةِ؛ لا تقييدٌ لِلضَّرورة المنفيَّةِ بِالإطلاق، حتَّى يَتَّجِهَ أَنَّ نفيَ الضَّرورةِ المطلقة لا يوجبُ غيرَ الضَّرورةِ بشرط الوصفِ لجواز التَّعيين في وقتِ الوصفِ، ثمَّ قالَ: وَهَهُنا بحثٌ، وهو أنَّهُ كَمَا تتحقَّقُ الضَّرورة باعتبار الذَّاتِ مشروطةٌ بالكتابة؛ لِنَفي
As for its truth in the second meaning, it is because the subsistence of darkening is necessary for the moon at the times of its description, i.e., eclipse. And if the description of the subject is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, the conditional in the first meaning is true, not the second, as in our saying necessarily: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer', for the subsistence of movement is necessary for the essence of the subject, i.e., individuals of the writer under the condition of its description, which is writing, but not at the times of description; for the description—which is writing—is not necessary for it in all time. (al-Dusooqi: And his saying: 'Every eclipsed… etc.'), consider what is the difference between this example and the following example soon, namely his saying: 'Every writer… etc.' For it may be said also about this: that darkness is not necessary because the eclipse upon which it depends is not necessary for the moon, by analogy with the following example equally. So that this example is one where the conditional in the second and first meaning is true by ruling. And it was answered by the difference between the two examples: for writing is not necessary at any time, unlike the eclipse, for it is necessary for the moon at the time of obstruction, for it is impossible in their view that the moon exists at that time non-eclipsed. So it is established that darkness is necessary at the time of eclipse necessarily, i.e., the time of obstruction. (al-‘Attar: His saying: 'But it is not necessary for it in all times of the description' originally: at a time of the description; because it is not necessary at any time, otherwise the conditional in the second meaning would be true. The summary is that if the description is considered a condition in our saying: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer', then the movement of fingers is necessary for the essence restricted by this description as previously stated in the sayyid's speech. But if it is considered a circumstance, the necessity of the movement of fingers disappears because the description—which is writing—is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, so the movement following writing is not necessary for the essence of the subject absolutely, so the conditional in the first meaning is true, not the second. (al-Dusooqi: His saying: 'absolutely') i.e., at any time. And know that between absolute necessity and the general conditional in the first meaning there is a general-specific relationship in one respect, considering actuality not concept, due to their being mutually exclusive, so they meet in: 'Every eclipsed is dark at the time of obstruction', and absolute necessity is unique in: 'Every human is an animal'. (al-‘Attar: His saying: 'for the essence of the subject'; i.e., its individuals at any time, so the movement following writing is not necessary, therefore he said: 'for the description… etc.'; so according to his estimation he made the description a part; so what is meant by 'writer' is particular individuals without making the description which is the source of necessity a condition in it. And it is known that writing is not necessary for these individuals. And by this you know that there is no grounds for the statement of al-Muhanni: 'If this proof were valid, it would indicate the falsity of intending the first meaning because it would apply to it identically' until the end of his statement. (al-Dusooqi: His saying: 'for the essence of the subject') i.e., the individuals as such, so it does not contradict the necessity of its subsistence for some individuals due to tremor for example. And his saying: '(absolutely)'; al-‘Isam said: it is a generalization of necessity, not a restriction of the negated necessity by absoluteness, such that it would be implied that the negation of absolute necessity does not necessitate other than necessity under condition of the description, due to the possibility of specification at the time of description. Then he said: and here there is a discussion, which is that just as necessity is realized with respect to essence conditioned by writing; for negation
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage defines and clarifies the truth conditions for two meanings of the conditional proposition (mashruta) in logic. It is an expansion probe from the commentary tradition, specifically a gloss from al-Dusooqi and al-‘Attar on a matn statement. The discussion centers on when a conditional proposition (like 'every X is Y as long as X is described') is considered necessarily true in the second meaning (where the necessity holds during the time of the condition) versus the first meaning (where the necessity is for the subject itself regardless of time).

The author begins by stating that a proposition is true in the second meaning if the predicate (e.g., being dark) is necessary for the subject (the moon) at the times when the description (eclipse) applies. If the description is not essential to the subject at any time, then the proposition is true only in the first meaning. The example given is 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Here, movement is necessary for the writer under the condition of writing, but writing itself is not necessary for the individual at all times. Therefore, the conditional is true in the first meaning (the predicate is necessary for the subject under the condition) but not the second (the predicate is not necessary for the subject as such).

A question arises about another example: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark at the time of obstruction.' Why is this considered true in the second meaning while the writer example is not? The reply distinguishes: writing is never necessary for a person, but an eclipse is necessary for the moon at the time of obstruction (when the earth comes between). Thus, darkness is necessary for the moon during the eclipse, fulfilling the second meaning's requirement that the predicate be necessary for the subject during the time of the condition.

Al-‘Attar then explains that if the description is treated as a condition in the judgment, then the predicate's necessity attaches to the subject under that condition. But if the description is merely a circumstance, the necessity is lifted because the description itself is not necessary for the subject at any time. Hence, the conditional in the first meaning holds.

Al-Dusooqi adds a note on 'absolutely' (mutlaqan), specifying that it means 'at any time.' He then discusses the relationship between absolute necessity (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') and the general conditional in the first meaning (e.g., 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'). These two types have a partial overlap: they share cases like 'Every eclipsed is dark at the time of obstruction' (which is both a conditional and absolute necessity during that time), but differ in that absolute necessity also applies to statements like 'Every human is an animal', where the predicate is always necessary.

Finally, al-‘Attar clarifies that when the description is considered a part of the subject (as in 'writer' meaning 'writing individual'), the predicate necessity does not apply to the essence without the description. He refutes an objection by al-Muhanni by showing that the argument does not undermine the first meaning.

All examples are rooted in Islamic sciences: lunar eclipse (astronomy), writing (a human action), and the human-animal relationship (natural science).

ضروري
وصف
انخساف

Checkpoint
Why is the conditional 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' true in the first meaning but not the second?
A) Writing is necessary for the person at all times.
B) Movement is necessary for the writer during writing, but writing itself is not necessary for the person at any time.
C) The writer does not always move his fingers.
D) The predicate is not truly necessary for the subject even under the condition.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional proposition in its second meaning requires that the condition (wasf) be necessarily true of the subject at some time, whereas in the first meaning it only requires the predicate to be necessary under the condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the conditional statement: 'Every fasting person is abstaining from food during the day as long as he is fasting.' Determine which meaning(s) of mashruta apply, explaining each step of the guided path.

Defining the Absolute Spread Proposition
Definition of the absolute spread proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute spread proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions by its temporal specification.

Core Idea
The absolute spread proposition judges necessity at no particular time — it spreads across all times.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of the absolute spread proposition (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) as presented by the author and expanded by the commentators. Understanding this proposition is crucial for evaluating modal claims in Islamic logic, especially when the judgment is necessary but not tied to a specific moment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for 'absolute spread'
The author states that if the necessity of the relation is in an unspecified time, it is an absolute spread proposition. Note that 'unspecified' means the time is not designated in the proposition, not that it is unknown in reality.
2
Recognize the example given
The example is 'Necessarily, every human is breathing at some time' and its negative counterpart. The necessity of breathing applies to any time, not a specific one.
3
Understand the reason for the name 'spread'
It is called 'spread' because the judgment potentially applies to every time, so it spreads over times. The term 'absolute' is used similarly to the absolute temporal proposition.
4
Consider additional examples from the commentary
The commentator adds 'Necessarily, every human is dying at some time' as another instance of an absolute spread proposition. This reinforces the concept by applying it to a different natural process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّن) عطفٌ على قوله معيَّن ) أي : إن كانَ الحكم بضرورةِ النّسبةِ في وقتٍ غير معيَّن ; (فَمُنْتَشِرَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) كقولنا بالضَّرورة : كلُّ إنسان مُتَنَفِّسُ في وقت ما وبالضَّرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفّس في وقتٍ ما ; فإنَّ ثبوتَ التَّنفّس للإنسان، وسلبه عنه ضروري في وقتٍ غير معيَّن وإِنَّما سُمِّيت مُنتَشِرةً; لاحتمال الحكم فيها كلَّ وقتٍ، فيكونُ مُنتَشِراً الأوقات، ومطلقةً لِمَا ذكرنا في الوقتية المطلقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: بِالضَّرُورَةِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ ...إلخ) أي : وَكَقولنا : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ إنسانٍ مَيّت وقتاً مَا وَلا شيء مِنَ الإنسانِ بِميّتٍ في وقت ما . واعلَمْ أنَّ بينَ الوقتيّتيْن والضَّروريَّةِ العمومُ والخصوص بإطلاق، وَهِيَ أخصُّ منهما ، فَقَولُكُ : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ مُنخسف مُظلم» يصلحُ مثالاً لِلثَّلاثةِ . وتنفرد الوقتيَّةُ عَنِ الضَّروريَّةِ بـ كلُّ كاتب مُتحرّكُ الأصابع»، وتنفرد المنتشرة عنها بـ كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس، وبين المشروطة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ وَالوقتيَّتيْن العموم والخصوص الوجهيُّ ، فَقَولُكُ : بالضَّرورة كلُّ مُنخسفٍ مُظلم يصلح مثالاً لِلثَّلاثة . وتنفردُ المشروطةُ بقولِكَ : بالضّرورة كلُّ إنسان كاتب»، وتنفرد الوقتية بـ :
(قَوْلُهُ: فِي وَقْتٍ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّن لمْ يُعيَّنْ ذلكَ الوقتُ; إذْ وقت ضرورةِ النِّسبة لا يحتمل أن يكون غيرَ مُعيَّن في نفس الأمر، فالمراد بقوله : أو غيرِ مُعيَّن ; أنْ لا هو يُعيَّن ذلك الوقتُ في القضيَّةِ.
Or unspecified: conjunction to his saying 'specified'. That is: if the judgment is by necessity of the relation in an unspecified time, then it is an absolute spread proposition, like our saying: necessarily, every human is breathing at some time, and necessarily, no human is breathing at some time. For the affirmation of breathing for human, and its negation from him, is necessary in an unspecified time. It is called 'spread' because the judgment potentially applies to every time, so it spreads over times, and 'absolute' for what we mentioned in the absolute temporal proposition. His saying: 'necessarily, every human is breathing' etc. That is, and like our saying: necessarily, every human is dying at some time, and no human is dying at some time. Know that between the two temporal propositions and the necessary proposition there is absolute generality and specificity, and they are more specific than them. So your saying: necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark, is a valid example for all three. The temporal is distinguished from the necessary by: every writer is moving his fingers, and the spread is distinguished from them by: every human is breathing. And between the conditional in the first sense and the two temporals there is generality and specificity from one perspective. So your saying: necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark, is a valid example for all three. The conditional is distinguished by your saying: necessarily, every human is a writer, and the temporal is distinguished by: ... His saying: 'in an unspecified time' means that time is not specified, because the time of necessity of the relation cannot be unspecified in itself. So what is meant by his saying 'or unspecified' is that the time is not specified in the proposition.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage defines the 'absolute spread' proposition (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa). The author begins by explaining that when the judgment of necessity applies to the relation in an unspecified time, the proposition is called 'absolute spread'. The example given is: 'Necessarily, every human is breathing at some time' and its negative counterpart. The necessity here is not tied to a specific moment but to any time whatsoever. The reason for the name 'spread' is that the judgment potentially covers every time, spreading across times. The commentator al-Dasūqī then expands by providing another example: 'necessarily, every human is dying at some time'. He clarifies the relationships between the temporal (waqtiyya), necessary (daruriyya), and spread (muntashira) propositions. The temporal and necessary are in a relationship of absolute generality and specificity, with the temporal being more specific. For instance, 'necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark' fits all three types, but the temporal alone also fits 'every writer is moving his fingers' (since writing is temporal), while the spread alone fits 'every human is breathing' (since breathing occurs at some unspecified time). He also compares the conditional (mashruta) with the temporals from a one-sided perspective. Al-‘Aṭṭār then clarifies that 'unspecified time' means the time is not designated in the proposition, even though in reality the necessity occurs at a specific moment. The key takeaway is that the absolute spread proposition has the widest temporal scope among the modal propositions, applying to any time without restriction.

Which of the following is an example of an absolute spread proposition (muntashira mutlaqa)?
A) Necessarily, every human is an animal (by essence).
B) Necessarily, every human is breathing at some unspecified time.
C) Necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of eclipse.
D) Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers while writing.

Key Takeaway
The absolute spread proposition is defined by necessity in an unspecified time, spreading across all times, distinct from temporal (specified time) and necessary (no time restriction) propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Necessarily, every human is mortal at some time', identify whether it is necessary, temporal, or absolute spread, and explain why.

Propositions

Distinguishing Modal Propositions: Necessary, Temporal, Spread, and Conditional
Distinction and relationships among modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between necessary, temporal, absolute spread, and conditional propositions by their temporal and conditional restrictions.

Core Idea
The key to distinguishing modal propositions lies in whether the necessity is tied to a specific time, any time, no time restriction, or a condition.

The commentator al-Dasūqī clarifies how modal propositions relate to each other through overlapping examples and unique instances. Understanding these relationships prevents confusion when analyzing the modality of premises in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relationship between temporal and necessary propositions
The commentator states that between the temporal (waqtiyya) and necessary (daruriyya) there is absolute generality and specificity: every temporal is necessary, but not every necessary is temporal.
2
Analyze the common example
The proposition 'every eclipsed thing is dark' fits all three types (necessary, temporal, spread) because darkness is essential, occurs at eclipse time, and is tied to an unspecified eclipse event.
3
Identify unique examples for each type
Temporal: 'every writer is moving his fingers' (specified time of writing). Spread: 'every human is breathing' (unspecified time). Necessary: 'every human is an animal' (essence). Each type has a distinctive instance that only it covers.
4
Understand the relationship with the conditional proposition
Between the conditional (mashruta, first sense) and the temporals there is generality and specificity from one perspective. The conditional is distinguished by 'every human is a writer' (with a condition).
5
Clarify the meaning of 'unspecified time'
The super-commentator al-‘Aṭṭār explains that 'unspecified' refers to the proposition not designating the time, though in reality the necessity occurs at a specific moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّن) عطفٌ على قوله معيَّن ) أي : إن كانَ الحكم بضرورةِ النّسبةِ في وقتٍ غير معيَّن ; (فَمُنْتَشِرَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) كقولنا بالضَّرورة : كلُّ إنسان مُتَنَفِّسُ في وقت ما وبالضَّرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفّس في وقتٍ ما ; فإنَّ ثبوتَ التَّنفّس للإنسان، وسلبه عنه ضروري في وقتٍ غير معيَّن وإِنَّما سُمِّيت مُنتَشِرةً; لاحتمال الحكم فيها كلَّ وقتٍ، فيكونُ مُنتَشِراً الأوقات، ومطلقةً لِمَا ذكرنا في الوقتية المطلقة.
(قَوْلُهُ: بِالضَّرُورَةِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ ...إلخ) أي : وَكَقولنا : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ إنسانٍ مَيّت وقتاً مَا وَلا شيء مِنَ الإنسانِ بِميّتٍ في وقت ما . واعلَمْ أنَّ بينَ الوقتيّتيْن والضَّروريَّةِ العمومُ والخصوص بإطلاق، وَهِيَ أخصُّ منهما ، فَقَولُكُ : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ مُنخسف مُظلم» يصلحُ مثالاً لِلثَّلاثةِ . وتنفرد الوقتيَّةُ عَنِ الضَّروريَّةِ بـ كلُّ كاتب مُتحرّكُ الأصابع»، وتنفرد المنتشرة عنها بـ كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس، وبين المشروطة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ وَالوقتيَّتيْن العموم والخصوص الوجهيُّ ، فَقَولُكُ : بالضَّرورة كلُّ مُنخسفٍ مُظلم يصلح مثالاً لِلثَّلاثة . وتنفردُ المشروطةُ بقولِكَ : بالضّرورة كلُّ إنسان كاتب»، وتنفرد الوقتية بـ :
(قَوْلُهُ: فِي وَقْتٍ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّن لمْ يُعيَّنْ ذلكَ الوقتُ; إذْ وقت ضرورةِ النِّسبة لا يحتمل أن يكون غيرَ مُعيَّن في نفس الأمر، فالمراد بقوله : أو غيرِ مُعيَّن ; أنْ لا هو يُعيَّن ذلك الوقتُ في القضيَّةِ.
