The Absolute Perpetual Proposition and Its Distinctions
Definition and comparison of the absolute perpetual proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute perpetual proposition and distinguish it from the necessary, general conditional, temporal, and absolute spread propositions using the condition 'perpetuity as long as the subject's essence exists'.

Core Idea
The absolute perpetual proposition is true for the entire duration of the subject's existence, not just under a condition or at a specific time.

In this passage, the author defines the 'absolute perpetual' proposition as one where the judgment holds perpetually as long as the subject's essence exists. Through detailed commentary and comparative examples, you will learn how this modal proposition relates to and differs from other types like the necessary, conditional, temporal, and spread propositions. Understanding these distinctions is essential for accurately classifying propositions in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Find the defining condition
Locate in the Arabic where the author states that the judgment is based on 'perpetuity of the relation' (bi-dawām al-nisba) and the condition 'as long as the essence' (mā dām al-dhāt). This is the core criterion for the absolute perpetual proposition.
The phrase 'mā dām al-dhāt' is key: it means the judgment holds throughout the existence of the subject.
2
Distinguish from the necessary proposition
The commentator notes that between the perpetual and the necessary proposition there is 'absolute generality and specificity' (umūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq). The necessary proposition is more general because it can hold eternally (before and after existence), while the perpetual is limited to the subject's existence.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' is necessary (eternally true) and also perpetual (as long as humans exist). But a proposition like 'God is knowing' is necessary but not perpetual because God has no beginning.
3
Compare with the general conditional
The general conditional (mashrūṭa 'āmma) shares with the perpetual the proposition 'every human is an animal' (true as long as human or perpetual). But they differ: the conditional is exemplified by 'every writer moves his fingers necessarily while writing' (conditioned on writing), while the perpetual example is 'every writer is an animal perpetually as long as the subject exists'.
The conditional restricts the judgment to a specific condition; the perpetual does not.
4
Compare with the temporal and spread
The temporal (waqtiyya) is restricted to a specific time (e.g., 'every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth's interposition'), while the spread (muntashira) holds at some unspecified time (e.g., 'every human breathes at some time'). The perpetual holds throughout the subject's existence (e.g., 'every black person is black perpetually').
Use the examples given: temporal and spread are more specific in temporal scope; perpetual covers the entire existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
[الدَّائمةُ المطلقة) :
أَوْ بِدَوَامِهَا) عطفٌ على قوله : ( بضرورة النّسبَةِ»; أي: إن كانَ الحكم فيها بدوام النّسبةِ (مَا دَامَ الذَّاتُ); أي : ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوعِ موجودةً ; (فَدَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَدَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) بينها وبينَ الضَّروريَّةِ العموم والخصوص المطلقُ، وبينَها (١) وبين ما عداها مِمَّا تقدَّمَ ; العمومُ والخصوص الوجهيُّ.
(قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : إِنْ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ ... إِلَخ) قالَ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : وَكَمَا عَلمْتَ أَنَّ لنا ضرورةً أزليَّةٌ ; فكذلكَ لنَا دوام أزلي، هو دوامُ النِّسبة أزلاً وأبداً مطلقاً ; لا حال وجودِ فقط كَمَا مرَّ في مثالِ الضَّرورةِ الأزليَّةِ، فالأزليَّةُ هَهُنا أخصُّ مِنَ المطلقة
(قَوْلُهُ : وبينها ... إلخ) ما عدا الدائمة المطلقة هو المشروطة العامة والوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتان، ونحن نبين لك تلك النسبة على هذا الترتيب. أما المشروطة العامة فتجتمع مع الدائمة المطلقة في : كل إنسان حيوان أي بالضرورة ما دام إنساناً أو دائماً ما دام الذات، وتنفرد المشروطة في : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة بشرط الكتابة، وتنفرد الدائمة في : كل كاتب حيوان دائماً ما دام الذات، وما الوقتية المطلقة فتجتمع مع الدائمة في : كل إنسان حيوان; أي بالضرورة في وقت كونه إنساناً أو دائماً ما دام الذات، وتنفرد الوقتية في : كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الأرض، وتنفرد الدائمة المطلقة في : كل زنجي أسود دائماً ما دام الذات، وأما المنتشرة المطلقة فتجتمع مع الدائمة في : كل إنسان حيوان; أي : بالضرورة في أي وقت أو دائماً ما دام الذات وتنفرد المنتشرة المطلقة في : كل إنسان متنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما، وتنفرد الدائمة المطلقة في : كل زنجي أسود دائماً ما دام الذات.
Chapter: On Definition and its Divisions. [The Absolute Perpetual]: Or by its perpetuity: conjunction to his saying 'by necessity of the relation'. That is: if the judgment in it is by perpetuity of the relation (as long as the essence) i.e., as long as the essence of the subject exists, then it is an absolute perpetual proposition. His saying: 'absolute perpetual' — between it and the necessary there is absolute generality and specificity, and between it and what precedes other than it there is generality and specificity from one perspective. His saying: 'that is, if the judgment' etc. Al-Dawwāni said: Just as you know that we have eternal necessity, so likewise we have eternal perpetuity, which is the perpetuity of the relation absolutely from eternity to eternity, not only during existence, as passed in the example of eternal necessity. So the eternal here is more specific than the absolute. His saying: 'and between it... etc.' — what is other than the absolute perpetual is the general conditional, the temporal, and the two absolute spreads. We will clarify for you that relation in this order. As for the general conditional, it meets with the absolute perpetual in: every human is an animal, i.e., necessarily as long as he is human or perpetually as long as the essence. The conditional is distinguished by: every writer is moving his fingers necessarily by condition of writing. The perpetual is distinguished by: every writer is an animal perpetually as long as the essence. As for the absolute temporal, it meets with the perpetual in: every human is an animal, i.e., necessarily at the time of being human or perpetually as long as the essence. The temporal is distinguished by: every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition. The absolute perpetual is distinguished by: every black person is black perpetually as long as the essence. As for the absolute spread, it meets with the perpetual in: every human is an animal, i.e., necessarily at any time or perpetually as long as the essence. The absolute spread is distinguished by: every human is breathing necessarily at some time. The absolute perpetual is distinguished by: every black person is black perpetually as long as the essence.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage defines the 'absolute perpetual' proposition (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa). The author explains that when the judgment is based on the perpetuity of the relation as long as the subject's essence exists, it is called 'absolute perpetual'. The commentator al-Dasūqī then notes that between the perpetual and the necessary proposition there is absolute generality and specificity (the perpetual is more specific because it includes only propositions true throughout the existence of the subject). Between the perpetual and other types (conditional, temporal, spread) there is generality and specificity from one perspective (some overlap). Al-‘Aṭṭār quotes al-Dawwāni, who points out that just as there is an eternal necessity, there is also an eternal perpetuity that holds from eternity to eternity, not just during existence; this eternal perpetual is more specific than the absolute perpetual. Then a detailed comparison is given: The general conditional (mashruta 'amma) shares with the perpetual the proposition 'every human is an animal' (always true as long as human or perpetual), but differs in examples like 'every writer moves his fingers necessarily while writing' (conditional) vs. 'every writer is an animal perpetually' (perpetual). The absolute temporal shares 'every human is an animal' but differs in 'every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth's interposition' (temporal) vs. 'every black person is black perpetually' (perpetual). The absolute spread shares 'every human is an animal' but differs in 'every human breathes at some time' (spread) vs. 'every black person is black perpetually' (perpetual). The key distinction is that the perpetual holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence, whereas the others are restricted to a specific time, a condition, or an unspecified time.

A) 'Every human is an animal' is an example of both.

Differentiating Dawam and Darura
Distinguishing perpetuity from necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential perpetuity (al-dawam al-dhati) and necessity (al-darura) and explain why necessity entails perpetuity but not vice versa.

Core Idea
Necessity implies perpetuity, but not all perpetuity is necessary.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions are classified by whether the judgment holds perpetually, necessarily, or absolutely. This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction between perpetuity (al-dawam) and necessity (al-darura). Understanding this difference is essential for correctly assessing the modal force of propositions used in kalam and fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modal terms
In the passage, perpetuity (al-dawam) means the judgment holds as long as the subject exists. Necessity (al-darura) means the judgment cannot be otherwise, often conditional on the subject's description. Note the example: 'Every human is an animal perpetually' – is this necessary?
The commentator al-Dassuqi uses this example to show that animality is not necessary for a human (it could be otherwise?), so it is perpetual but not necessary.
2
Analyze the entailment relation
The text states: necessity entails perpetuity (al-darura tastalzim al-dawam), but not vice versa. Why? If a judgment is necessary, it must hold perpetually. But a perpetual judgment may be contingent (e.g., 'Zayd is sitting perpetually' is not necessary because he could stand).
This is a one-way entailment: darura → dawam, dawam ↛ darura.
3
Apply to real examples from Islamic sciences
Consider the proposition 'The Prophet (ﷺ) is a messenger of Allah perpetually'. Is this necessary? In kalam, prophethood is a contingent attribute; it is perpetual but not necessary (Allah could have chosen otherwise). By contrast, 'Allah exists necessarily' entails both perpetuity and necessity.
This distinction helps avoid conflating eternal truths with contingent perpetual truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الدَّوَامِ وَالضَّرُورَةِ : أَنَّ الضَّرُورَةَ تَسْتَلْزِمُ الدَّوَامَ، وَلَا عَكْسَ . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ دَائِماً) وكلُّ إنسانٍ حادث دائماً . (قَوْلُهُ : تَسْتَلْزِمُ الدَّوَامَ، وَلَا عَكْسَ أي : وحينئذ ; فكلُّ مثالٍ صح لِلضَّروريَّةِ؛ العطار أيضاً كَمَا في الضَّرورةِ، لكنَّ الدَّوامَ الذَّاتِيَّ لا يُفارقُ الإطلاق العام في قضيَّةٍ محمولها الوجود، بخلافِ الضّرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّة ا.هـ وقَولُهُ: لكنَّ الدَّوامَ الذَّاتي ... إلخ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّهُ يَتَّجِهُ على التَّعريف بأنَّهُ يستلزم أن لا يكون بين الموجبة الدائمة المطلقة والسالبة المطلقة العامَّةِ تناقض ; لاجتماعِهما على الصِّدقِ في القضيَّةِ محمولُها الوجودُ كَقولنا : زيدٌ موجود ما دامَ موجوداً، وَزيد ليس بموجود بالإطلاقِ العام، وأجابَ العصام : بأنَّ الكلام في الموجهات من القضايا الخارجية والحقيقية، والقضية المذكورة مِنَ القضيَّةِ الذهنية، وردَّهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّ الإشكال المذكور كما يرد بناءً على تلك القضيَّة؛ كذلك يرد بناءً على القضايا التي محمولاتها عوارض خارجيَّةٌ لموضوعاتِها كَقولنا : زيدٌ مُتحيّز أو أسود أو أعمى ما دامَ موجوداً، وزيد ليسَ يمتحيّز أو أسود أو أعمى بالإطلاقِ العام، وَلَا شَكٍّ أنَّها مِنَ القضايا الخارجيَّةِ أو الحقيقية، فالجواب المذكورُ غير حاسم لمادَّةِ الإشكال، والأَوْلَى في الجوابِ أنْ يُقالَ : المراد بالإطلاقِ العام هو وقت مَا مِن أوقاتِ وجودِ الموضوع، وحينئذ يظهرُ التَّناقض بينه وبينَ الدَّوامِ المطلق، وتكذبُ السَّوالب المطلقة العامة في المواد المذكورة أيضاً، ثمَّ نقل عن البعض ردَّ هذا الجواب، فالأحسنُ مَا قالَه عبد الحكيم أنَّ المتبادرَ مِنَ التَّعريف أنْ يكونَ المحمولُ غير الوجودِ، فَلَا يرد ما ذكر ا . هـ من وقد يُقالُ عليهِ إِنَّهُ تَخصيص في التّعريف ، والتَّعريفات لا تُخصَّص (قَوْلُهُ: تَسْتَلْزِمُ الدَّوَامَ ... إِلَخ) لأنَّ مَفهومَ الضَّرورة امتناع انفكاكِ الشَّيء عنِ
And the difference between perpetuity and necessity: that necessity entails perpetuity, but not vice versa. Al-Dassuqi: (His saying: 'Every human is an animal perpetually') and 'Every human is temporally originated perpetually'. (His saying: 'entails perpetuity, and not vice versa') means: then every example that is valid for the necessary... Al-'Attar: also as in the necessity, but the essential perpetuity does not depart from the general absolute in a proposition whose predicate is existence, unlike the essential necessity. End. And his saying: 'but the essential perpetuity... etc.' is an indication that it is directed at the definition that it entails that there be no contradiction between the affirmative absolute perpetual and the absolute general negative; because they both could be true in a proposition whose predicate is existence, like our saying: 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists', and 'Zayd is not existent in the general absolute'. And al-'Isam answered: that the discussion is about modal propositions from external and real propositions, and the mentioned proposition is from mental propositions. And Abu al-Fath refuted him: that the mentioned difficulty arises based on that proposition; likewise it arises based on propositions whose predicates are external accidents for their subjects, like our saying: 'Zayd is spatial or black or blind as long as he exists', and 'Zayd is not spatial or black or blind in the general absolute', and there is no doubt that they are from external or real propositions. So the mentioned answer is not decisive for the subject of the problem. And better in answering is to say: what is meant by 'general absolute' is some time from the times of the existence of the subject, and then the contradiction appears between it and the absolute perpetual, and the absolute general negatives are false in the mentioned materials as well. Then he narrated from someone the refutation of this answer. So the best is what 'Abd al-Hakim said: that the immediate understanding from the definition is that the predicate should be other than existence, so what was mentioned does not arise. End. And it may be said about it: it is a specification in the definition, and definitions are not specified. (His saying: 'entails perpetuity... etc.') because the concept of necessity is the impossibility of the thing being separate from
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage first states the logical difference between perpetuity (dawam) and necessity (darura): necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity. For example, 'Every human is an animal perpetually' is a perpetual proposition but not necessary because the animality of humans is not necessary (it could be otherwise?). However, the text then moves into a critical discussion of the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition. A problem is identified: if we take the definition literally, then for a proposition with existence as predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists'), the affirmative absolute perpetual ('Zayd is existent perpetually') and the general absolute negative ('Zayd is not existent absolutely') can both be true — a contradiction in terms (since they should be contradictories). Several solutions are offered: al-'Isam limits the discussion to external propositions, but Abu al-Fath points out that the problem persists even with external predicates like 'spatial' or 'black'. A better answer is to reinterpret 'general absolute' as meaning 'at some time of the subject's existence', which would then show the correct contradictory relation. The final suggestion from 'Abd al-Hakim is that the predicate must be other than existence itself, effectively exempting existential propositions from the definition — though this is criticized as an arbitrary specification. This passage shows the care logicians took in refining definitions to avoid paradoxes.

Key Terms
الضَّرُورَة
الدَّوام الذاتي
الإطلاق العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that necessity entails perpetuity but not vice versa?
A) Because perpetuity is a broader concept.
B) Because a necessary judgment must hold forever, but a perpetual judgment may be contingent.
C) Because necessity is defined as perpetual.
D) Because the examples given only show one direction.

Key Takeaway
Necessity is a stronger modal force than perpetuity: all necessary judgments are perpetual, but not all perpetual judgments are necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a proposition that is perpetual (dawam) but not necessary (darura) from a fiqh context, e.g., 'The obligation of prayer is perpetual as long as the mukallaf exists.' Explain why it is not necessary.
?
Identify whether the following is dawam or darura: 'Allah is knowledgeable perpetually.' (Answer: both perpetual and necessary, but careful: necessity is a stronger modal claim.)

Resolving the Contradiction in Absolute Perpetual Propositions
Addressing a definitional paradox

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem arising from the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition when the predicate is existence, and evaluate the proposed solutions.

Core Idea
A flawed definition can make contradictories both true; the logicians refined the definition to avoid this.

After distinguishing perpetuity and necessity, the text raises a critical problem: the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition (al-daima al-mutlaqa) leads to a contradiction when the predicate is existence. Several solutions are proposed and debated. This lesson walks through the problem and its resolution, showing how Islamic logicians rigorously refined definitions to maintain logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the contradiction problem
Take the proposition 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists' (affirmative absolute perpetual) and its contradictory negative 'Zayd is not existent in the general absolute' (negative general absolute). Both can be true: the first is always true, the second is true at some times (e.g., before Zayd existed). So they are not truly contradictory.
This violates the principle of contradiction for contraries? Actually they should be contradictories, but here they can both be true, so the definition fails.
2
Examine al-'Isam’s attempted solution
Al-'Isam restricts the discussion to external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions, claiming the problem only arises with mental (dhihniyya) propositions. But Abu al-Fath refutes this: the problem persists with external predicates like 'spatial' or 'black'.
This shows that the problem is not limited to existential propositions but to any predicate that can be truly denied at some time.
3
Analyze the 'better answer'
One answer redefines 'general absolute' as 'at some time of the subject’s existence'. Then the negative 'Zayd is not existent ever in his existence' is false, and a true contradictory would be 'Zayd is not existent at some time of his existence' which is also false if he always exists. This restores contradiction? Actually need to check: the original problem is that both the affirmative perpetual and the negative general absolute (interpreted as 'at no time') can be true? Wait: 'Zayd is not existent in the general absolute' means 'Zayd is not existent at any time' which is false if he exists. So the problem is that the negative is true? The text says they both could be true: the affirmative 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists' is true, and the negative 'Zayd is not existent absolutely' (i.e., at no time) is false. Hmm, let's re-read the translation: 'both could be true in a proposition whose predicate is existence, like 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists', and 'Zayd is not existent in the general absolute'.' Actually the second is false if Zayd exists. So the problem is that the definition would make them both true? Wait, the text says 'they both could be true' – perhaps the point is that the definition of the absolute perpetual (as holding at all times of subject existence) and the general absolute negative (as not holding at any time) can both be vacuously true if the subject never exists? But the example has Zayd existing. I need to be careful. Let's look at the commentary: 'it entails that there be no contradiction between the affirmative absolute perpetual and the absolute general negative; because they both could be true in a proposition whose predicate is existence, like our saying: 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists', and 'Zayd is not existent in the general absolute'.' That is confusing. Possibly the general absolute negative is 'Zayd is not existent absolutely' meaning at some time? Actually the term 'general absolute' (al-itlaq al-'amm) means 'at some unspecified time'. So the negative 'Zayd is not existent in the general absolute' means 'Zayd is not existent at some time', which is true (since he did not exist before birth and after death). So both are true: affirmative perpetual holds during his existence, negative general absolute holds at times outside. So they are not contradictories; the correct contradictory of the perpetual affirmative should be a negative perpetual (not at any time during his existence) but the general absolute negative only denies at some time, not all. So the problem is that the definition of contradictory pairs is flawed. The 'general absolute' is not the proper contradictory of the absolute perpetual. This is the issue. So one solution is to reinterpret 'general absolute' to mean 'at some time of his existence', then the negative would be false, but that changes the definition. Another solution by 'Abd al-Hakim is to exclude existential propositions from the definition (predicate must be other than existence). This is criticized as an arbitrary specification. This is the core of the critical refinement.
This step requires careful logical thinking. The problem is subtle but important for understanding how definitions are tested.
4
Evaluate the final suggestion by 'Abd al-Hakim
He says the immediate understanding from the definition is that the predicate should be other than existence. This would exclude cases where the predicate is 'existent', thus avoiding the problem. However, this is seen as a specification that narrows the definition without justification.
This shows the tension between preserving a definition and fixing a flaw; later commentators rejected arbitrary restrictions.

Commentary
This passage first states the logical difference between perpetuity (dawam) and necessity (darura): necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity. For example, 'Every human is an animal perpetually' is a perpetual proposition but not necessary because the animality of humans is not necessary (it could be otherwise?). However, the text then moves into a critical discussion of the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition. A problem is identified: if we take the definition literally, then for a proposition with existence as predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is existent as long as he exists'), the affirmative absolute perpetual ('Zayd is existent perpetually') and the general absolute negative ('Zayd is not existent absolutely') can both be true — a contradiction in terms (since they should be contradictories). Several solutions are offered: al-'Isam limits the discussion to external propositions, but Abu al-Fath points out that the problem persists even with external predicates like 'spatial' or 'black'. A better answer is to reinterpret 'general absolute' as meaning 'at some time of the subject's existence', which would then show the correct contradictory relation. The final suggestion from 'Abd al-Hakim is that the predicate must be other than existence itself, effectively exempting existential propositions from the definition — though this is criticized as an arbitrary specification. This passage shows the care logicians took in refining definitions to avoid paradoxes.

Defining the General Customary Proposition
Commentator defines 'urfiyya 'amma and contrasts it with daruriyya and da'ima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a 'urfiyya 'amma proposition and distinguish it from daruriyya and da'ima propositions.

Core Idea
The 'urfiyya 'amma ties judgment to the subject's description, not its essence.

This passage from the commentary on the Tadhheeb clarifies a key modal proposition: the 'urfiyya 'amma (general customary). The commentator defines it by linking the judgment's duration to the presence of a description in the subject, then contrasts it with necessary (daruriyya) and absolute perpetual (da'ima) propositions. Understanding this distinction allows you to classify propositions precisely in Islamic logical and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the Arabic term for the proposition being defined. It is 'urfiyya 'amma. Notice the gloss: 'if the judgment is with permanence of the relation as long as the description of the subject exists; then it is a general customary.'
2
Extract the defining condition
What makes a proposition 'urfiyya 'amma? The commentator says the judgment holds 'as long as the description of the subject exists'. This is the key condition: the truth of the proposition is tied to the continued existence of a description (like 'writer' or 'speaker').
3
Contrast with daruriyya
A necessary (daruriyya) proposition is always true, regardless of any description. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is true necessarily, whether the human is writing or not. In 'urfiyya 'amma, the truth depends on the description.
4
Contrast with da'ima
An absolute perpetual (da'ima) proposition holds at all times without any condition. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is also perpetual. But 'urfiyya 'amma holds only during the existence of the description; it is not always true. The commentator says the difference is like that between perpetual and necessary.
5
Apply the classification
Given a proposition, determine if it is 'urfiyya 'amma by checking if its truth depends on a description of the subject. For instance, 'Every writer moves their fingers as long as they write' is 'urfiyya 'amma because it requires the description 'writer'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ مَا دَامَ الْوَصْفُ) عَطْفٌ عَلَى قَوْلِهِ: (دَوَامَ الذَّاتِ)؛ أَيْ: إِنْ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ مَا دَامَ وَصْفُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُوداً؛ (فَعُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ). وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ وَالدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ وَالْمَشْرُوطَةِ هُوَ الْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الدَّائِمَةِ وَالضَّرُورِيَّةِ. مِثَالُهَا إِيجَاباً وَسَلْباً مَا مَرَّ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ. وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا كَالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الدَّائِمَةِ وَالضَّرُورِيَّةِ.
Or as long as the description) is a conjunction to his saying: (permanence of the essence); meaning: if the judgment is with permanence of the relation as long as the description of the subject exists; then it is a general customary. And the difference between it and the necessary, the absolute perpetual, and the conditional is the difference between the perpetual and the necessary. Its example affirmatively and negatively is what passed in the general conditional. And the difference between them is like the difference between the perpetual and the necessary.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage defines the 'urfiyya 'amma (general customary proposition) and distinguishes it from other modal propositions. The commentator explains that when the judgment's duration is tied to the existence of a description of the subject (not the subject's essence), the proposition is called 'urfiyya 'amma. The key distinction is between this type and the necessary (daruriyya) and perpetual (da'ima) propositions. The commentator notes that the difference between 'urfiyya 'amma and the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) is analogous to the difference between the perpetual and the necessary. In other words, the 'urfiyya 'amma is a subtype of perpetual proposition where the judgment holds only as long as the description exists, whereas the necessary proposition involves a judgment that cannot be otherwise. This classification is crucial for understanding modal logic in the Islamic tradition, as it allows precise expression of temporal and conditional ties in syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
عُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ
ضَرُورِيَّةٌ
دَائِمَةٌ
مَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a 'urfiyya 'amma proposition?
A) Every human is an animal necessarily.
B) Every writer moves their fingers as long as they are writing.
C) Every human is an animal perpetually.
D) Every eclipse is dark necessarily when it occurs.

Key Takeaway
The 'urfiyya 'amma is a perpetual proposition whose truth depends on the continued existence of a description of the subject.

Distinguishing General Conditional from General Customary Propositions
Commentator distinguishes general conditional from general customary by the mode of necessity vs. permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general conditional and general customary propositions by identifying their respective modes (necessity vs. permanence).

Core Idea
The mode of necessity vs. permanence separates two superficially similar proposition types.

In modal logic, propositions can be restricted by time or attribute. The general conditional and general customary both restrict the judgment to the duration of an attribute, but they differ in the mode: necessity for the conditional, permanence for the customary. This distinction mirrors the more fundamental difference between necessary and permanent propositions, which is central to evaluating the logical strength of claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate the terms 'general conditional' (المشروطة العامة) and 'general customary' (العرفية العامة) in the passage. Note that both are types of modal propositions that restrict the judgment to the duration of a subject's attribute.
2
Note their shared feature
Both propositions tie the judgment to the continuation of an attribute, expressed by 'as long as' (ما دام). For example, 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' This shared feature is why the distinction is subtle.
3
Extract the mode
The passage states that the general conditional belongs to necessary propositions (الضروريات) and the general customary belongs to permanent propositions (الدوائم). The mode of the general conditional is necessity (الضرورة), while the mode of the general customary is permanence (الدوام).
4
Apply the analogy with permanent and necessary propositions
The difference is likened to that between permanent and necessary propositions. Necessity means the judgment cannot be otherwise; permanence means it holds continuously but not necessarily. For instance, 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' is necessary if writing necessitates finger movement, but if it is merely a customary regularity, it would be permanent but not necessary.
In Islamic theology, this distinction helps differentiate between essential attributes of Allah (necessary) and contingent but constant patterns in creation (permanent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. ومثالها إيجاباً وسلباً ما مرَّ في المشروطة العامة. والفرق بينهما كَالفرق بينَ الدَّائمةِ والضَّروريّة. الدسوقي بالممعنيين ; عموم وخصوص مُطلق هي أعمُّ الجميع، وبينها وبين الوقتية العموم والخصوص الوجهيُّ. قَوْلُهُ: مَا مَرَّ فِي الْمَشْرُوْطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ أي : بإبدال الضَّرورةِ بالدَّوامِ؛ لأنَّ الجهة هنا الدوام. وفيما مرَّ؛ الضَّرورةُ كَقولنا : دائماً كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً ، وَكَقولنا : دائماً لا شيء مِنَ الكاتبِ بِساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتباً. قَوْلُهُ: وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا أي : بين المشروطةِ العامَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِنَ الضَّروريَّاتِ، والعُرفيَّةِ العامَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِنَ الدَّوائم بعدَ اشتراكِهِمَا في أنَّ الحكم في كُلِّ مُقيَّدٌ بِدوامِ الوَصْفِ. قَوْلُهُ : كَالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الدَّائِمَةِ وَالضَّرُوْرِيَّةِ فيه : أَنَّ مَا تقدَّمَ الفرقُ بينَ الضَّرورةِ والدَّوام. العطار. زمان الوصفِ؛ لأنَّ معنى الدَّوامِ استمراره وعدم انفكاكه، وهو حاصل بالقياس إلى المجموع وبالقياس إلى الذَّاتِ وحدَه في زمان الوصف؛ سواء كانَ لِلوصفِ مدخل في دوامِ المحمولِ كَمَا في المثالِ المذكور، أو لم يكنْ كَمَا في قولِكَ : كُلُّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً
A chapter on definition and its divisions. Their examples in affirmation and negation are what passed in the general conditional. The difference between them is like the difference between the permanent and the necessary. Al-Dasuqi says: by the two meanings, absolute generality and particularity; it is the most general of all, and between it and the temporal there is generality and particularity from one aspect. His statement: 'what passed in the general conditional' means by replacing necessity with permanence, because the mode here is permanence. In what passed, necessity is like our statement: always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, and our statement: always nothing of the writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer. His statement: 'the difference between them' means between the general conditional, which is among the necessary propositions, and the general customary, which is among the permanent propositions, after their sharing in that the judgment in each is restricted to the permanence of the attribute. His statement: 'like the difference between the permanent and the necessary' in it: that what preceded the difference between necessity and permanence. Al-Attar: the time of the attribute; because the meaning of permanence is its continuation and non-separation, and it is obtained in respect of the whole and in respect of the essence alone in the time of the attribute, whether the attribute has an entry in the permanence of the predicate as in the mentioned example, or not, as in your statement: every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a base text (matn) about the distinction between two types of modal propositions: the 'general conditional' (المشروطة العامة) and the 'general customary' (العرفية العامة). The commentator begins by restating the matn: the difference between them is like the difference between the permanent (الدائمة) and the necessary (الضرورية). Then al-Dasuqi explains that the general conditional is actually two meanings: a general absolute and a particular absolute, and it is the most general of all, having a face-to-face generality with the temporal. Al-Attar clarifies that the example for the general conditional is replacing necessity with permanence because the mode here is permanence. He gives examples for the necessary mode: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Always nothing of the writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer.' Then he explains the difference between the two proposition types: the general conditional is from necessary propositions, while the general customary is from permanent propositions, but they share the restriction of the judgment to the permanence of the attribute. Finally, al-Attar explains the difference between necessity and permanence: permanence is continuation and non-separation, obtaining either in respect of the whole or the essence alone in the time of the attribute, regardless of whether the attribute affects the permanence of the predicate or not, as in the last example.

العرفية العامة
الدائمة
الضرورية

Distinguishing Proposition Types through Unique Examples
Enumeration of examples distinguishing proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which proposition type (general conditional, absolute temporal, absolute distributed, or general customary) is uniquely applicable to a given example by applying the distinguishing conditions.

Core Idea
Each proposition type has exclusive examples that only it can express correctly.

In this passage, the commentator illustrates how different proposition types are distinguished by providing specific examples where only one type can apply. By examining these examples—such as 'every eclipse is dark' for the conditional, 'every moon is eclipsed at opposition' for the temporal, 'every human is breathing at some time' for the distributed, and 'every black person is black as long as he is black' for the customary—we learn the precise conditions of time, permanence, and necessity that define each type. This lesson will guide you through the logic behind these classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
For each example, note the subject (e.g., 'eclipse', 'black person', 'moon', 'writer', 'Roman') and predicate (e.g., 'dark', 'black', 'eclipsed', 'moving fingers', 'white').
2
Determine the time or condition qualifier
Look for phrases like 'as long as' (دائماً ما دام), 'at the time of' (وقت), or 'at some time' (في وقت ما). This qualifier indicates the type of generality: conditional, temporal, or distributed.
3
Check if necessity (بالضرورة) is explicitly stated or implied
The text indicates whether the judgment holds by necessity (e.g., 'by necessity at the time of opposition') or not (e.g., 'permanently as long as he is a writer' without necessity). Necessity is key for conditional and temporal types.
4
Match the qualifier and necessity to the definition of each type
Use the definitions: General conditional: necessity/permanence as long as a condition holds. Absolute temporal: necessity at a specific time. Absolute distributed: necessity at some unspecified time. General customary: permanence without necessity, as long as the subject exists.
5
Confirm the unique type based on the text's verdict
The text states that certain examples are unique (تنفرد) to one type. For instance, 'every black person is black as long as he is black' is unique to the customary, because necessity would be absurd (a black person is not necessarily black by external cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثاً : مع المشروطة بمعنييها في: كل منخسف مظلم: أي بالضرورة ما دام منخسفاً ، أو بشرط الانخساف أو دائماً ما دام منخسفاً، وتنفرد العرفية كل زنجي أسود دائماً ما دام زنجياً ورابعاً مع الوقتية المطلقة في : كل إنسان حيوان؛ أي: بالضرورة في وقت كونه إنساناً أو دائماً ما دام إنساناً، وتنفرد الوقتية في: كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت الحيلولة، وتنفرد العرفية في كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً وخامساً : مع المنتشرة المطلقة في : كل إنسان حيوان؛ أي: بالضرورة في وقت ما ، أو دائماً ما دام إنساناً، وتنفرد المنتشرة في : كل إنسان منتف بالضرورة في وقت ما، وتنفرد العرفية في: كل رومي أبيض دائماً ما دام رومياً إذ لا تصح الضرورة فيها .
And third: with the conditional in its two meanings in: every eclipse is dark: i.e., by necessity as long as it is eclipsed, or by condition of eclipse, or permanently as long as it is eclipsed. And the customary is unique: every black person is black permanently as long as he is black. And fourth: with the absolute temporal in: every human is an animal; i.e., by necessity at the time of being human or permanently as long as he is human. And the temporal is unique in: every moon is eclipsed by necessity at the time of opposition. And the customary is unique in: every writer is moving his fingers permanently as long as he is a writer. And fifth: with the absolute distributed in: every human is an animal; i.e., by necessity at some time, or permanently as long as he is human. And the distributed is unique in: every human is breathing by necessity at some time. And the customary is unique in: every Roman is white permanently as long as he is Roman, since necessity is not valid in it.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed enumeration of five comparative cases (third, fourth, fifth) showing how the general customary proposition (العرفية) is distinct from other types. The commentator first compares the general conditional (المشروطة) with its two meanings (necessity or permanence) and gives the example 'every eclipse is dark' which can be interpreted with necessity or permanence. The customary proposition is unique (تنفرد) in 'every black person is black permanently as long as he is black'. Then comparing with the absolute temporal (الوقتية المطلقة), the example 'every human is an animal' can be with necessity at the time of being human or permanently. The temporal is unique in 'every moon is eclipsed by necessity at the time of opposition', while the customary is unique in 'every writer is moving his fingers permanently as long as he is a writer'. Finally, comparing with the absolute distributed (المنتشرة المطلقة), the example 'every human is an animal' can be with necessity at some time or permanently. The distributed is unique in 'every human is breathing by necessity at some time', while the customary is unique in 'every Roman is white permanently as long as he is Roman', because necessity is not valid for that predicate. The enumeration illustrates the specific conditions under which each proposition type applies.

Why 'Customary'? The Role of Common Usage in Temporal Propositions
Explanation and refinement of the term 'customary' in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general conditional and general customary propositions, and identify when custom understands a temporal restriction in negative versus affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
Custom fills the gap when the temporal mode is unstated—but only where attributes are connected.

The commentator explains that certain propositions are called 'customary' because common usage conveys a temporal restriction even when the phrase 'as long as' is omitted. However, this understanding is not universal; it depends on the type of proposition and the connection between subject and predicate. In this lesson, you will trace the logic behind the naming, the relationship with conditional propositions, and the nuanced conditions under which custom infers the restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rationale for the term 'customary'
The commentator gives an example: 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' without 'as long as he is sleeping'. Custom understands the negation of wakefulness from the essence of the sleeper. Why? Because there is a natural connection between sleeping and not being awake.
This connection is key—custom only works when the attributes are related.
2
Distinguish between general conditional and general customary
The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) entails the general customary (عرفية عامة), but not vice versa. An example of both: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'. An example of only customary: 'Nothing of the horse is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a horse' given Zayd's oath not to ride a horse.
The conditional has a logical condition; the customary relies on common usage.
3
Examine the conditions for customary understanding in negatives vs affirmatives
Al-Harawi says: in negatives, the understanding is permanent and obvious; in affirmatives, it is for the most part because attribution to a derived term indicates causality. But Abd al-Hakim refutes this claim for negatives.
Abd al-Hakim argues that only negatives with a connection between attributes work.
4
Refine with Abd al-Hakim's objection
Abd al-Hakim says custom understands restriction only in negatives where there is a connection (تعلق) between the attributes of subject and predicate, e.g., 'Nothing standing is sitting'. In negatives like 'There is no man in the house', custom does not infer a temporal restriction.
The connection is necessary; not all negatives are alike.
5
Note the role of explicit mode mention
If the mode (جهة) is mentioned, the meaning is explicit and custom is not needed. Custom only operates when the mode is omitted.
Implicit understanding only occurs when the mode is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقية. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيت عُرفيَّةٌ ؛ لأنَّكَ إذا قُلْتَ: لا شيء من النائم بمستيقظ، ولم تذكرُ : ما دامَ نائماً، يفهمُ العُرفُ أنَّ سلب الاستيقاظ عن ذاتِ الدسوقي. وأجيب : بأنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنَ الفرق بينَ الضَّرورةِ والدَّوامِ؛ الفرقُ بين الدائمةِ والضَّروريَّةِ ؛ لأنَّهما مَأخوذانِ مِنْهُمَا ، فَيُقالُ : إِنَّ المشروطةً العامَّةَ تستلزمُ العُرفيَّةَ العامَّةَ ، وَلَا عَكْسَ، فكلُّ مثال صلح للمشروطة العامة ؛ صلح للعرفية العامة، نحو: كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً، وَليسَ كُلُّ مَا صلح أنْ يكونَ عُرِفيَّةٌ عامَّةٌ ؛ يصلحُ أنْ يكونَ مَشروطةٌ . ومثال انفرادِ العُرفيَّةِ : لا شيء مِنَ الفرس بِمَركوب زيدٍ ما دام فرساً، والحال أنَّ زيداً حلف لا يركب فرساً ، فَهذهِ عُرفيَّةٌ؛ لِصحة توجيهها بالدَّوامِ. العطار. حيوان؛ قالَهُ السَّيِّدُ ، ولم يعتبرُوا في الدوام نظير الوقتيَّةِ المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة كَمَا في الضَّرورةِ؛ لأنَّ الدَّوامَ يُنافي معناهما . قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ عُرْفِيَّةٌ ... إِلَخ) قالَ الهروي : وكون هذا المعنى مفهوماً مِنَ العرفِ، أمَّا في السَّالبةِ : فَعَلى الدَّوامِ معَ الظَّهورِ ، وأمَّا في الموجبة : فَعَلى الأغلب؛ إذ الإسناد إلى المشتق يُشعِرُ بِعلِّيَّةِ المأخذ ا.هـ. ويردُّ دعوى الدَّوامِ قولُ عبد الحكيم : إِنَّ العَرَّفَ العام يفهم هذا المعنى مِن بعضِ السَّوالب الغير المقيد بقيدِ ما دامَ، وَهِيَ الَّتي يكون بينَ وَصفَي موضوعِهَا ومحمولها تَعلُّق؛ نحو: لا شيء مِنَ القائم بقاعد، وهذا القدر كافٍ لنسبة هذا المعنى إلى العُرفِ، ولا يجبُ اطردادُ هذا الفهم في جميع الشوالبِ، فَمَا قِيْلَ بَقِيَ أنَّهُ لا يفهم العرفُ التقييد بالوصف في : ليس رجل في الدَّارِ، وَلَا في ليسَ الإنسانُ حجراً، وأمثال ذلكَ وَهُم . قَوْلُهُ: يُفْهَمُ الْعُرْفُ أي : عندَ عدمِ ذِكْرِ الجهة، وأما إذا ذُكِرَتِ الجهة؛ فالمعنى المذكورُ مفهوم منها .
Logic. And it was only called 'customary' because if you say: 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' and you do not mention 'as long as he is sleeping', common custom understands the negation of wakefulness from the essence... Al-Dasuqi: And it was answered: That the difference between necessity and permanence necessarily implies the difference between the permanent and the necessary; because they are taken from them. So it is said: The general conditional entails the general customary, and not vice versa. So every example that is valid for the general conditional is valid for the general customary, like: every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer. And not every example that is valid to be a general customary is valid to be a general conditional. And an example of the uniqueness of the customary: Nothing of the horse is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a horse, while Zayd has sworn not to ride a horse. So this is customary because it is valid to be directed with permanence. Al-Attar: Animal; said by al-Sayyid, and they did not consider in permanence the counterpart of the absolute temporal and absolute distributed as in necessity, because permanence contradicts their meanings. His statement: 'And it was only called customary' etc. Al-Harawi said: That this meaning is understood from custom; as for the negative, it is with permanence and obviousness, and as for the affirmative, it is for the most part; because attribution to a derived term indicates the causality of the derivation. End. And the claim of permanence is refuted by the statement of Abd al-Hakim: Common custom understands this meaning from some negative propositions not restricted by the condition 'as long as', which are those where there is a connection between the two attributes of subject and predicate; like: Nothing of the standing is sitting. And this much is sufficient to attribute this meaning to custom, and it is not necessary for this understanding to be consistent in all negatives. So what was said remains: that custom does not understand the restriction by the attribute in: 'There is no man in the house', nor in 'Man is not stone', and similar examples, and they are mistaken. His statement: 'Custom understands' means when the mode is not mentioned; but if the mode is mentioned, the mentioned meaning is understood from it.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of why this proposition type is called 'customary' (عُرفية). The commentator begins by stating the reason: if you say 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' without explicitly saying 'as long as he is sleeping', common custom (العرف) understands the negation of wakefulness from the sleeper. Then al-Dasuqi adds an answer: the difference between necessity and permanence implies the difference between the permanent and the necessary. The general conditional entails the general customary, but not vice versa; thus any example valid for the conditional is valid for the customary, but not all customary examples are conditional. An example of a uniquely customary proposition is 'Nothing of the horse is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a horse' given Zayd's oath. Al-Attar then notes that permanence does not have counterparts for absolute temporal and distributed as necessity does, because permanence contradicts their meanings. Al-Harawi further explains that in negative propositions the customary understanding is permanent and obvious, while in affirmatives it is for the most part, because attribution to a derived term indicates causality. However, Abd al-Hakim refutes the claim of permanence, arguing that custom understands the restriction only in negatives where there is a connection between the attributes of subject and predicate, like 'Nothing standing is sitting'. It is not necessary that custom understands restriction in all negatives, and examples like 'There is no man in the house' or 'Man is not stone' are mistaken. The final note clarifies that custom understands the restriction only when the mode is not mentioned; if the mode is mentioned, the meaning is explicit.

Key Terms
العرف
السالبة
المشتق

Key Takeaway
Custom understands a temporal restriction only in propositions where there is a natural connection between subject and predicate attributes, and this understanding is more consistent in negatives than affirmatives, but not universal.

The Customary Proposition: Definition, Naming, and Logical Relation
Author defines the customary proposition and explains its naming and relation to the conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the customary proposition from the conditional proposition based on the entailment relation and identify examples exclusive to the customary.

Core Idea
The customary proposition restricts the predicate to the subject's description duration without implying necessity.

Why is a proposition called 'customary'? Because common custom understands an implicit time restriction: when you say 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake', you mean 'as long as he is sleeping'. This lesson unpacks the definition of the customary proposition, the reason for its name, and its logical relationship to the conditional proposition, which carries necessity. By analyzing the examples and the entailment rule, you will grasp how these modal propositions function in logical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and eponymy
Locate why the author calls it 'customary'. Notice that the name comes from common custom (ʿurf) understanding the temporal restriction even when not explicitly stated.
The term 'urfiya (customary) is derived from ʿurf (custom).
2
Unpack the implicit temporal restriction
The example 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' is understood to mean 'as long as he is sleeping', not permanently. This is the core feature: the predicate holds only during the duration of the subject's description.
3
Analyze the entailment from mashruta to 'urfiya
The author explains that every conditional proposition (mashruta) entails a customary proposition, but not vice versa. This means the conditional is a stricter type that includes necessity.
Mashruta often implies a necessary connection, while 'urfiya only implies a habitual or customary connection.
4
Examine the examples provided
The first example 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' is both conditional and customary. The second example 'Nothing of the horse is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a horse' is only customary because it depends on a specific vow, not a necessary truth.
The second example is valid for 'urfiya because it can be 'directed to permanence' but lacks the necessity of mashruta.
5
Apply the distinction to real arguments
In logical analysis, misidentifying a customary proposition as conditional can lead to false conclusions. For instance, in legal reasoning about oaths, the modal force matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما سُمِّيت عُرفيَّةٌ؛ لأنَّكَ إذا قُلْتَ: لا شيء من النائم بمستيقظ، ولم تذكرُ: ما دامَ نائماً، يفهمُ العُرفُ أنَّ سلب الاستيقاظ عن ذاتِ النائم ليس دائماً، بل ما دام نائماً. وأجيب: بأنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنَ الفرق بينَ الضَّرورةِ والدَّوامِ الفرقُ بين دائمة والضَّروريَّةِ؛ لأنَّهما مأخوذانِ منهما، فيقال: إنَّ المشروطة العامَّةَ تستلزمُ العُرفيَّةَ العامَّةَ، ولا عكسَ، فكلُّ مثال صلح للمشروطة العامة صلح للعرفية العامة، نحو: كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرّكُ الأصابع ما دام كاتباً. وليس كلُّ ما صلح أن يكون عُرفيَّة عامَّة يصلح أن يكون مشروطة. ومثال انفراد العُرفيَّة: لا شيء مِنَ الفرس بِمَركوب زيدٍ ما دام فرساً، والحال أنَّ زيداً حلف لا يركب فرساً، فهذه عُرفيَّةٌ؛ لصحة توجيهها بالدَّوامِ.
It is called 'customary' because if you say: 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' and you do not mention 'as long as he is sleeping', custom understands that the negation of wakefulness from the essence of the sleeper is not permanent, but rather as long as he is sleeping. And it is answered: from the difference between necessity and permanence, the difference between the permanent and the necessary necessarily follows; because they are taken from them. So it is said: The general conditional proposition entails the general customary proposition, but not vice versa. So every example valid for the general conditional is valid for the general customary, such as: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer.' But not everything that is valid to be a general customary is valid to be a conditional. And an example exclusive to the customary: 'Nothing of the horse is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a horse', while Zayd swore not to ride a horse, so this is customary because it is valid to be directed to permanence.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage defines the customary proposition ('urfiya), one of the types of modal propositions. The author begins by explaining why it is called 'customary': because common custom (urf) understands the temporal restriction implicitly. For example, saying 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' is understood to mean 'as long as he is sleeping', not that he is never awake. This is the essence of the customary proposition: the predicate holds only during the duration of the subject's description. Then the author addresses a potential objection about the distinction between necessity (darura) and permanence (dawam). It is explained that the general conditional proposition (mashruta 'amma) entails the general customary proposition, but not the reverse. A conditional proposition, like 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer', is true at all times during the writing state; it is also customary. However, a proposition like 'Nothing of the horse is ridden by Zayd as long as it is a horse' is customary but not conditional, because it is based on a specific vow and not a necessary truth. The phrase 'valid to be directed to permanence' means that it can be interpreted as a temporal restriction but lacks the necessity of the conditional. This distinction is crucial in logical analysis, as it affects the validity of inferences in arguments.

Key Terms

المشروطة
الدَّوام

The customary proposition implicitly restricts the predicate to the duration of the subject's description without requiring necessity; every conditional is customary but not every customary is conditional.

The Absolute Proposition as a Modal Type
Author defines the absolute proposition and explains that actuality is a modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute proposition and explain why it is considered modal.

Core Idea
Actuality itself is a modality in Islamic logic.

In this passage, the author defines a fundamental type of proposition: the absolute (al-mutlaqa). Unlike propositions qualified by necessity or permanence, the absolute proposition simply affirms or negates the predicate as a matter of fact. Yet the author insists it is still modal—because the quality of actuality (al-fiʿliyya) is itself a modality. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating syllogisms that involve temporal or non-temporal judgments, as in theological discussions about the divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modal categories
Note that the passage contrasts 'absolute' with 'necessity' (darura) and 'permanence' (dawam). The absolute is defined by the absence of both.
The base text likely listed these three as the primary modalities.
2
Understand the definition of actuality
Actuality means the predicate is affirmed or negated of the subject 'in actuality'—i.e., as a matter of fact, without any temporal or modal restriction. The phrase 'in general' (fi al-jumla) indicates it applies regardless of time or necessity.
3
Recognize why actuality is a modality
The author states that the absolute proposition is considered modal (mujjaha) because actuality is an 'additional quality' (kayfiyya za'ida). Thus, even a simple factual claim has a modal dimension.
This is a key point to avoid confusion about what counts as modal.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
The author gives the example of divine attributes, which are absolute because they are realized before time and are not contingent on temporal conditions. This shows how the absolute proposition applies in kalam.
Contrast with human actions, which are temporally conditioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ بِفِعْلِيَّتِهَا) عطفٌ على قولِهِ : بضرورة النِّسبَةِ؛ أي: إن لم يكن الحكم بضرورةِ النّسبة ولا بدوامها بل يكون الحكم بفعليتها؛ أي: بثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع أو سليه عنه بالفعل؛ أي: في الجملة من غيرِ التفات إلى كونه ضروريًا أو دائماً أو لا، وسواء كانَ في أحدِ الأزمنة أو لَا، كَمَا فِى صفاتِ اللهِ؛ لتحققها قبلَ الزَّمانِ. وإِنَّما كانَتِ المطلقة بهذا المعنى مُوجَّهةً؛ لأنَّ الفعليَّةَ بهذا المعنى كيفية زائدة.
('Or by its actuality') conjoined to his saying 'by necessity of the relation'; i.e., if the judgment is not by necessity of the relation nor by its permanence, but rather the judgment is by its actuality. That is, by the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation from it in actuality; i.e., in general, without regard to whether it is necessary or permanent or not, and whether it is in one of the times or not, as in the attributes of God because they are realized before time. And the absolute proposition in this sense is considered modal only because actuality in this sense is an additional quality.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa) as one of the types of modal propositions. The absolute proposition is contrasted with those qualified by necessity (darura) or permanence (dawam). Instead, the absolute proposition is based on actuality (fi'liyya): the predicate is affirmed or negated of the subject as a matter of fact, without any temporal or modal restriction. It is called 'absolute' because it is free from such qualifications. The author notes that actuality is considered a modality (mujjaha) because it adds a quality to the proposition, similar to necessity and permanence. An example from the text: the attributes of God are absolute because they are realized before time and are not subject to necessity or permanence in the same way. This distinction is important in logical analysis because different modal propositions behave differently in syllogisms. The absolute proposition is the simplest form, but it is still considered modal due to the added quality of actuality.

الجهة

Key Takeaway
The absolute proposition is modal because actuality itself is an additional quality attached to the judgment, not because it lacks modality.

The General Absolute Proposition: Definition and Actuality Modality
Shaykh defines al-mutlaqa al-‘amma with its actuality modality and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma) and distinguish it from necessary and perpetual propositions.

Core Idea
The general absolute judges by actuality alone, ignoring necessity and perpetuity.

This passage defines al-mutlaqa al-‘amma by its modality of actuality (fi'liyya). This modal category is crucial for evaluating statements in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where the truth of a proposition may depend on actual occurrence rather than necessity. The shaykh clarifies that actuality is distinct from necessity (darura) and perpetuity (dawam), and provides examples from common human attributes and divine attributes. Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing arguments that rely on modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term 'al-mutlaqa al-‘amma' in the passage. It is the proposition being defined.
2
Extract the defining modality
The definition centers on 'bi-fi'liyyatiha' (by its actuality). This means the predicate holds or is negated in actual existence, without extra conditions.
Actuality is the key modal qualifier; contrast it with necessity and perpetuity.
3
Contrast with excluded modalities
The passage explicitly excludes necessity (darura) and perpetuity (dawam). Note that a general absolute proposition does not require the predicate to hold in all cases or at all times.
This contrast sharpens the definition by showing what it is not.
4
Analyze the example
The example 'Every human is breathing with general absolute' shows a positive general absolute. The negation 'No human is breathing' shows a negative one. Why are these not necessary or perpetual? Because breathing is not always present (e.g., sleep) nor forever.
Apply the definition to confirm the example fits.
5
Consider the special case of divine attributes
The passage mentions attributes of God as realized before time, showing that actuality can apply timelessly. This extends the concept beyond temporal actuality.
This nuance is important for theological applications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ بِفِعْلِيَّتِهَا ) أي : بثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع أو سليه عنه بالفعل ; أي: في الجملة من غيرِ التفات إلى كونه ضروريًا أو دائماً أو لا، وسواء كانَ في أحدِ الأزمنة أوْ لَا ، كَمَا فِى صفاتِ اللهِ ; لتحققها قبلَ الزَّمانِ. فَالمُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ كقولنا : كلُّ إنسان متنفّس بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفّس بالإطلاق العام، فإِنَّ ثبوت التَنفُسِ للإنسانِ وسلبَهُ عنه ليس ضروريا ولا دائماً، بل بالفعل؛
Or by its actuality: that is, by the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation from it in actuality; meaning, in general without regard to whether it is necessary or perpetual or not, and whether it is in one of the times or not, as in the attributes of God; because they are realized before time. So the general absolute is like our saying: 'Every human is breathing' with general absolute, and 'No human is breathing' with general absolute, for the affirmation of breathing to the human and its negation from him are neither necessary nor perpetual, but actual.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the modal proposition known as 'al-mutlaqa al-‘amma' (general absolute). It begins by clarifying the modality 'bi-fi'liyyatiha' (by its actuality), which means the judgment is made based on actual occurrence or non-occurrence of the predicate for the subject, without considering whether that relationship is necessary (daruri) or perpetual (da'im). The modality is unrestricted by time; it applies even in cases like the attributes of God, which are realized before time. The definition proper then gives examples: 'Every human is breathing' as a positive general absolute, and 'No human is breathing' as a negative general absolute. The key point is that the affirmation or negation in these propositions is not necessary (e.g., humans do not necessarily breathe at all times) nor perpetual (they do not breathe forever), but rather actual: the judgment holds in actuality, at least in some instances. This distinguishes the general absolute from necessary and perpetual propositions. The commentary thus serves to expand the matn's concise treatment by detailing the modality and providing examples.

الفعلية
الضرورة
الدوام

The Absolute General Proposition
Commentators define the absolute general proposition and distinguish it from necessity and perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the absolute general proposition from the spread absolute and identify its key modal property.

Core Idea
The absolute general proposition asserts actual predication without necessity or perpetuity.

In the study of modal propositions, logicians distinguish between propositions that express necessity, perpetuity, and actuality. The absolute general proposition (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) is a key type that simply states that a predicate holds for a subject in actuality, without any temporal or modal restriction. This lesson unpacks its definition and clarifies how it differs from the spread absolute proposition (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the condition that the predication is 'bil-fi'l' (actual) and not 'daruri' (necessary) nor 'da'im' (perpetual).
The definition is given by example and explicit negation.
2
Examine the example
Note the example 'Every human is breathing' and its negation. This same example is used for the spread absolute, leading to potential confusion.
The commentator points out a contradiction in using the same example.
3
Understand the objection
The spread absolute involves necessity at some time, while the absolute general does not. How does the commentator resolve this?
The resolution is that the absolute general does not exclude necessity or possibility; it merely asserts actuality.
4
Grasp the resolution
The absolute general is broader: it covers both necessary and possible predications as long as they are actual. Thus, the example can be interpreted differently for each proposition.
This shows the modal neutrality of the absolute general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمطلقة العامة كقولنا: كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام، ولا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام، فإن ثبوت التنفس للإنسان وسلبه عنه ليس ضروريا ولا دائما، بل بالفعل. (قوله: ليس ضروريا) أي: في كل الأوقات، فلا ينافي أنه ضروري في وقت غير معين كما تقدم التمثيل به في المنتشرة المطلقة بقوله: بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ما. وفيه: أن التمثيل بهذا المثال في المطلقة العامة ينافي التمثيل به في المنتشرة المطلقة; لأنها ضرورية، والمطلقة العامة ليست ضرورية، وأجيب: بأن المطلقة العامة لا تخرج عن الضرورة والإمكان.
The absolute general (proposition) is like our saying: 'Every human is breathing' with absolute generality, and 'No human is breathing' with absolute generality. For the affirmation of breathing for humans and its negation from them is neither necessary nor perpetual, but actual. (His saying: 'not necessary') means in every time, so it does not negate that it is necessary at an unspecified time, as was previously exemplified in the spread absolute by his saying: 'Necessarily, every human is breathing at some time.' And it is said: that exemplifying with this example in the absolute general contradicts exemplifying with it in the spread absolute, because the latter is necessary while the absolute general is not necessary. And it is answered: that the absolute general does not exit from necessity and possibility.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage defines the 'absolute general' proposition (al-mutlaqa al-'amma), a type of modal proposition. The author first gives an example: 'Every human is breathing' and 'No human is breathing' with absolute generality (i.e., actual existence, not necessary or perpetual). He explains that the affirmation or negation is neither necessary (like 'Every human is rational', which is essential) nor perpetual (like 'The sky is rotating'), but actual (bil-fi'l), meaning it occurs at some time but not always. The commentary then clarifies that 'not necessary' means not necessary at every time, so it does not contradict the 'spread absolute' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), which is necessary at some time. An objection is raised: using the same example ('human breathing') for both types is problematic because the spread absolute involves necessity while the absolute general does not. The answer is that the absolute general does not exclude necessity and possibility altogether; it merely states the actual occurrence without specifying mode. Thus, the absolute general is broader, covering both necessary and possible predications as long as they are actual.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
بالفعل
المنتشرة المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The spread absolute is always perpetual, while the absolute general is not.

Key Takeaway
The absolute general proposition asserts actual predication without implying necessity or perpetuity, and it is distinct from the spread absolute which involves necessity at an unspecified time.

Why the Absolute Proposition is Called Mutlaqa and the Inversion Issue
Naming of the absolute proposition and commentary on inversion and objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the absolute proposition is named mutlaqa, identify the inversion in the commentator's phrasing, and summarize al-Razi's critique of actuality as a quality.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition's name indicates its unrestricted relation, not its modality.

This passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies the terminology of modal propositions. You will learn how the name 'mutlaqa' is derived from the relation (nisba) being unrestricted, why the commentator's phrasing contains an inversion, and how al-Razi reinterprets actuality. Understanding these nuances is key to handling modal propositions in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Trace the name 'mutlaqa' to the relation
The author says the proposition is called 'mutlaqa' because its relation (nisba) is released from any modal restriction. The release applies to the relation, not the proposition itself. So calling the proposition 'mutlaqa' is a transfer of the name from the signified (relation) to the signifier (proposition) — tasmiya al-dal bi-ism al-madlul.
2
Detect the inversion in the commentator's phrase
The commentator originally said 'tasmiiya lil-madlul...' but the correct wording should be 'tasmiiya al-dal bi-ism al-madlul' (naming the signifier with the name of the signified). This is an inversion (qalb). Two scholars are cited: al-Manufi and al-Nafrawi, who agree on the correction.
3
Distinguish original vs. conventional meaning of mutlaqa
Originally, the absolute proposition encompassed both actual (fi'liyya) and possible (mumkina) propositions. But in common usage, it is restricted to those where the relation is actual. This conventional restriction excludes possibles. The author notes this in his commentary on the Risala.
4
Analyze al-Razi's critique of actuality as a quality
Al-Razi (in Sharh al-Mutali') argues that actuality (fi'l) is not a quality (kayfiyya) of the relation, because its meaning is simply the occurrence of the relation. A quality must be something else. Therefore the absolute is only metaphorically a modal proposition. He also says the possible proposition lacks actual judgment and is merely potential. The author begins to reply but the text cuts off.

وَمِنْ هُنَا؛ يُعْلَمُ مَا في عبارةِ الشَّارحِ مِنَ القلبِ، كَذَا قرَّر بعضُهُم، وقرَّر بعضُهُم أنَّ قولَه : (تسمية للمدلول ... إلخ)؛ العبارة فيها قلبٌ، فَكانَ الأَوْلَى أنْ يقول : تسمية للدَّالّ باسم المدلول، فالدَّالُّ هو القضيَّةُ، والمدلول هو قطعاً النِّسبةُ، والتَّقريرُ الأَوَّلُ رأيتُهُ مَعرُوا لِلشَّيخ منصور المنوفي، والثَّاني رأيتُهُ مَعزواً للشيخ سالم النفراوي . و لِتَصادقِهما في الصُّورةِ الثَّانيةِ، وصدق القوَّة بدونِ الإمكان في الصُّورةِ الأ لصدق قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ بهواء بالضَّرورةِ، فَلَا يصدقُ : الماء هواء بالإمكان، و صدق الإمكان بدونِ القوَّةِ: حيثُ تكونُ النّسبةُ فعليّة . And it is only called 'mutlaqa' (absolute) because when the proposition is released (i.e., not restricted by a modality) ... Dassuqi says: (His statement: 'when it is released') meaning: its relation (nisba) understood from it is released from this restriction. So the release in reality is for the relation signified, so calling it 'mutlaqa' is naming the signifier (the proposition) with the name of the signified (the relation). And from this it is known that there is an inversion in the commentator's expression. Thus some have decided, and some decided that his statement: 'naming for the signified... etc.' contains an inversion; it would be better to say: naming the signifier with the name of the signified. For the signifier is the proposition, and the signified is definitely the relation. The first determination I saw attributed to Shaykh Mansur al-Manufi, and the second I saw attributed to Shaykh Salim al-Nafrawi. (His statement: 'because when the proposition is released... etc.') If you say: then there is no need for this modality, since its meaning is understood without it, I say: his statement: 'the actuality of the relation is understood from it' means: it may be understood from that. And for the coincidence of the two in the second figure, and the truth of potentiality without possibility in the first figure? Because we say: 'Nothing from... air by necessity', so it is not true that 'water is air by possibility', and the truth of possibility without potentiality occurs where the relation is actual. (His statement: 'because when the proposition is released') means: the absolute proposition in origin is what is not restricted by any modality, and it encompasses actual and possible propositions. But since the common and linguistic understanding of a proposition is that the relation is actual, they specifically restricted the absolute to this, and the possible propositions are excluded. This is what the author said in his commentary on the Risala. And al-Razi said in Sharh al-Mutali': 'The truth is that actuality is not a quality of the relation, because its meaning is nothing but the occurrence of the relation, and a quality must be something other than the occurrence of the relation, which is the judgment. And they only counted the absolute among the modal propositions metaphorically, just as they counted the negative among categorical and conditional propositions. And that the possible proposition is not an actual proposition because it does not contain a judgment; rather it is a proposition by potentiality close to actuality, because it contains subject, predicate, and relation, and they count it among propositions just as they count imaginary propositions among them, although there is no actual judgment in them.' End quote. And the author answered the first point: that the actuality of the relation...
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage discusses why the absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa) is so named and clarifies the terminology. The author first states that it is called 'mutlaqa' because the proposition is released from any modal restriction. Dassuqi explains that the release applies to the relation (nisba), and calling the proposition 'mutlaqa' is a transfer of the name from the signified to the signifier. He then points out an inversion in the commentator's wording: instead of saying 'naming the signifier with the name of the signified,' the commentator said 'naming for the signified.' This inversion is noted and attributed to two scholars. Then an objection is raised: if the meaning is understood without the modality, why is it mentioned? The answer is that it may be understood. There follows a brief discussion about the scope of the absolute proposition: originally it includes both actual and possible, but common usage restricts it to actual propositions where the relation is actual. Al-Razi's view is quoted, arguing that actuality is not a quality of the relation but its occurrence, and that the possible proposition is not actual but potential. The author begins to answer this, but the text is cut off at the page break.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the absolute proposition called 'mutlaqa'?
A) Because its modality is always necessity.

Key Takeaway
The absolute proposition is named for its unrestricted relation, not its modality, and the terminology involves a transfer of name from signified to signifier, with al-Razi later arguing that actuality is not a quality.
