Propositions
intermediate
8 min
Defining the Absolute Proposition and Its Modal Status
Author defines the absolute proposition and debates its modal classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the absolute proposition, explain its conventional actuality, and analyse the debate over whether actuality is a quality of the relation.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition is not truly modal; it is classed among modals only metaphorically.

The term 'absolute proposition' may seem straightforward, but classical logicians engaged in deep debates over its definition and modal classification. In this passage, the author explains why it is called 'absolute', clarifies that its relation is unrestricted, and then examines the controversy: is actuality (fi'liyyah) a quality of the relation or just its occurrence? You will learn to parse these subtle distinctions and understand why the absolute is counted among the modals at all.

Guided Path
1
Understand the naming inversion
The author states that the proposition is called 'absolute' because its relation is unrestricted. But the commentator al-Dasūqī points out a logical inversion: the proposition (signifier) is named after the relation (signified). Identify this inversion and grasp why precision in naming matters.
The inversion correction is attributed to two scholars, showing the collaborative nature of the commentary tradition.
2
Identify the conventional meaning
Although 'absolute' originally meant unrestricted by any modality, conventional usage restricts it to propositions where the relation is actual (fi'liyyah). Recognise how linguistic custom shapes logical terminology.
This conventional restriction excludes possibles from the absolute category.
3
Distinguish actuality from possibility
The passage contrasts actuality (occurrence of the relation) with possibility (non-necessary, non-absurd). Al-Rāzī argues that actuality is not a quality but sheer occurrence. Follow the exchange between al-‘Aṭṭār and ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm on whether a possible proposition contains a judgment.
The key question: does the possible proposition involve an actual judgment about the relation?
4
Analyse the modal classification
The absolute proposition is counted among modals by metaphor, because it has the form of a modal (it carries a restriction of actuality). The example 'Every human is breathing' shows that language implies actuality unless specified otherwise.
The author concludes that if establishment-by-possibility is distinct from possibility-of-establishment, then the possible is a real modal; otherwise, it lacks judgment.

الأصل؛ ما لا تكونُ مُقيَّدةً بجهةٍ مِنَ الجهات، وهي تعم الفعليَّاتِ والممكنات، لكنْ لمَّا كَانَ المفهومُ مِنَ القضيَّةِ عُرفاً ولغةً ما تكونُ النّسبةُ فعليَّةٌ ؛ خَصُّوا المطلقة بهذا، وخرجَتِ الممكناتُ ؛ قالَهُ المصنِّفُ في شرحِ الرِّسالة، وقالَ الرَّازِيُّ في شرح المطالعِ : الحقُّ أنَّ الفعل ليسَ كيفيَّةٌ لِلنّسبةِ ؛ لأنَّ مَعناه ليس إلا وقوع النّسبة، والكيفية لا بدَّ أن تكون أمراً مغايراً لوقوع النّسبة الذي هو الحكم، وإنَّما عَدُّوا المطلقة في الموجّهات بالمجاز ؛ كما عَدُّوا السَّالبة في الحمليَّاتِ والشَّرطيَّاتِ وأنَّ الممكنة ليست قضيَّةٌ بالفعل ؛ لعدم اشتمالها على الحُكْمِ، وإنَّما هِيَ قضيَّةٌ بالقوَّةِ القريبةِ مِنَ الفعل باعتبارِ اشتمالها على الموضوع والمحمول والنِّسبةِ، وعَدُّها من القضايا كَعَدِّهِم المَخيَّلاتِ منها ، مع أنَّهُ لا حُكْمَ فيها بالفعل .ا.هـ. وأجاب المصنِّفُ عن الأوَّلِ : بأنَّ فعليَّةَ النسبة

وقد يُفهَمُ منها : أنَّ نسبة المحمول للموضوع على جهة الإمكان، فإذا صرَّحَ بهذه الجهةِ؛ اندفَعَ هذا الاحتمالُ، فَاستُفيد من ذكرِ الجهة التصريح بهذا المعنى.
انتهى تقرير شيخنا السَّيّد البليدي .
العطار
كيفيَّةٌ زائدةٌ على نفسِ النِّسبةِ ؛ لأنَّ النِّسبة أعمُ مِن أنْ تكونَ بالفعل أو بالإمكان، وعن الثَّاني : بأنَّ قولَنا : كُلُّ ج ب بالإمكانِ مُشتمل على حكم ورابطة لا محالة، ومفهومه أنَّ (ب) ثابت لِ(ج)؛ مع انتفاء الضَّرورةِ عن الثَّبوتِ واللاثبوتِ، وَلَا مَعنى للقضيَّةِ إِلَّا أنْ يحكم فيها بأنَّ وصفَ المحمول صادق على ذاتِ الموضوع؛ سواء كانَ بِالإمكانِ أو بالفعل، وكُلُّ منهما كيفيَّةٌ زائدة على نفسِ النِّسبةِ، وردَّه عبد الحكيم بأنَّهُ : لا يدفعُ مَا ذكرَهُ مِنْ أنَّ القضيَّةَ لا بُدَّ فيها من وقوع النِّسبةِ وَلَا وقوع في مادَّةِ الإمكان، فإنْ أرادَ بقولِهِ : إِنَّ قولَنا : كُلُّ جب بالإمكانِ مُشتمل على الحكم ؛ أَنَّهُ مُشتمل على وقوع النسبة؛ فَممنوع، وإن أرادَ أَنَّهُ مُشتمل على صورة الحكم كَمَا يشعرُ بِهِ عطفُ الرَّابِطَةِ عليهِ؛ فَمُسلَّم، لكن لا يصيرُ بِهِ قضيَّةٌ مِن حيثُ الصُّورَةُ كالمخيَّلاتِ ؛ لا بحسب الحقيقة، والذي يقتضيهِ النَّظَرُ الصَّائبُ : أَنَّ الثَّبوت بطريق الإمكانِ إِنْ كَانَ مُغايراً لإمكانِ الثَّبوتِ؛ فالممكنة مشتملة على الحكم والجهةِ، فيكونُ قضيَّةٌ موجّهةً، وَكَذا المطلقة العامَّةُ ؛ لِكَونِ الفعل جهةً مُقابلةٌ لِلإمكان حينئذٍ ، وإِنْ لم يكنْ مُغايراً ؛ فَلَا محكم فيها، والمطلقة العامَّةُ : هي القضيَّةُ المطلقةُ، وَعدُّهَا مِنَ الموجهاتِ باعتبارِ كونها في صورة الموجهة ؛ لاشتمالها على قيد بالفعل. (قَوْلُهُ: مِنْ غَيْرِ تَقْبِيْدِ بِاللَّا دَوَامِ وَاللَّا ضَرُورَةِ كَمَا إِذَا قِيلَ: كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس؛ يفهم منه عرفاً ولغةٌ : ثبوتُ التَّنفّس للإنسانِ بالفعل ؛ أي: بكونه حاصلاً لهُ، أَمَّا أَنَّ التَنفُسَ دائم أو غير دائم، ممكن أو غير ممكن؛ فإنَّما يُفهم بقيدٍ آخَرَ إِمَّا بِزيادةٍ على الفعل كالضَّرورةِ والدَّوامِ ، أو بنقصانٍ عن الفعل كالإمكان؛ فَإِنَّهُ أَقلُّ مِنَ الفعلِ إِذْ جاز أن يكون بالقوَّةِ، وقد كان الأولى لِلشَّارحِ أَنْ لا يُقيَّدَ القيدَ بِاللادوامِ واللاضرورةِ كَمَا قالَ غيرُه : ولمْ تُقيَّدْ بِجهةٍ .
حُكْمَ
And it is called 'absolute' only because when the proposition is unrestricted... al-Dasūqī: (His saying: 'when it is unrestricted') meaning: its relation understood from it is unrestricted from this restriction, so the restriction in reality is only for the relation signified by it, so naming it 'absolute' is naming the signifier by the name of the signified. From this, one knows what is in the commentator's expression of inversion, as some of them decided, and some of them decided that his saying: (naming the signified...) the expression has an inversion, so it would have been better for him to say: naming the signifier by the name of the signified, for the signifier is the proposition and the signified is undoubtedly the relation. The first decision I saw attributed to Shaykh Mansur al-Manufi, and the second I saw attributed to Shaykh Salim al-Nafrawi. (His saying: because the proposition when unrestricted...) If you say: then there is no need for this modality, since its meaning is understood without it, I say: His saying: (actuality of the relation is understood from it) means: it may be understood from it that. al-Matar: And because they coincide in the second figure, and the truth of potentiality without possibility in the first figure... (His saying: because the proposition when unrestricted) means: the absolute proposition in origin is not restricted by a modality from among the modalities, and it encompasses actualities and possibles, but since what is understood from the proposition by convention and language is that whose relation is actual, they restricted the absolute to this, and the possibles are excluded. The author said this in his commentary on the Risala, and al-Razi said in Sharh al-Matali': The truth is that actuality is not a quality of the relation, because its meaning is nothing but the occurrence of the relation, and quality must be something other than the occurrence of the relation which is the judgment. They only counted the absolute among the modals by way of metaphor, just as they counted the negative among the categoricals and conditionals, and that the possible is not an actual proposition because it does not contain judgment, but it is a proposition in proximate potentiality from actuality by virtue of containing subject, predicate, and relation, and its being counted among propositions is like their counting imaginative propositions among them, even though there is no actual judgment in them. End quote. And the author answered the first: that the actuality of the relation... [PAGE_567] without restriction by non-permanence or non-necessity, al-Dasūqī: And it may be understood from it that the relation of the predicate to the subject is in the mode of possibility, so if this modality is explicitly stated, this probability is repelled, so the mention of the modality benefits making this meaning explicit. End of the report by our master al-Sayyid al-Balidi. al-‘Attar: a quality additional to the relation itself, because the relation is more general than being actual or possible. And regarding the second: that our saying: 'Every J is B possibly' contains a judgment and a connective undoubtedly, and its concept is that B is established for J, while necessity of establishment and non-establishment is negated. There is no meaning for proposition except that one judges in it that the description of the predicate is true of the subject's essence, whether it is by possibility or actuality, and each is a quality additional to the relation itself. And Abd al-Hakim refuted him: that it does not repel what he mentioned, that a proposition must have occurrence of the relation, and there is no occurrence in the matter of possibility. If he meant by his saying: 'Our saying: Every J is B possibly' contains judgment, that it contains occurrence of the relation, it is denied. If he meant that it contains the form of judgment, as is suggested by his conjoining the connective with it, it is granted, but it does not thereby become a proposition in terms of form, like imaginatives, not in reality. What sound consideration requires is that establishment by way of possibility, if it is different from the possibility of establishment, then the possible proposition contains judgment and modality, so it is a modal proposition, and likewise the general absolute, because actuality becomes a modality opposite to possibility then. And if it is not different, then there is no judgment in it. The general absolute is the absolute proposition, and its counting among the modals is by virtue of its being in the form of a modal, because it contains a restriction of actuality. (His saying: 'without restriction by non-permanence or non-necessity' as when it is said: Every human is breathing; it is understood by convention and language: the establishment of breathing for the human actually, i.e., it being present for him. As for whether breathing is permanent or non-permanent, possible or impossible, it is only understood by another restriction, either by increase over actuality like necessity and permanence, or by deficiency from actuality like possibility, for it is less than actuality since it may be potential. And it would have been better for the commentator not to restrict the restriction with non-permanence and non-necessity, as another said: it was not restricted by a modality. Judgment.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute proposition (al-qadyyah al-muṭlaqah) and discusses its classification among modal propositions. It begins by explaining the naming: the proposition is called 'absolute' because its relation (nisbah) is unrestricted—that is, not tied to any modality like necessity or possibility. The commentator al-Dasūqī points out a logical inversion in the sharh's phrasing: technically, the proposition (the signifier) is named after the relation (the signified). Two scholars, al-Manufi and al-Nafrawi, are cited for this correction. The passage then addresses an objection: if the mere mention of the proposition already implies actuality, why add the modality of actuality? The answer is that the phrase 'actuality of the relation is understood from it' means only that it may be understood, not that it always is—so the explicit modality removes ambiguity. The text then cites al-Rāzī's view that actuality is not a quality (kayfiyyah) of the relation but simply its occurrence; nonetheless, the absolute is classed among modals metaphorically, like how negative propositions are treated. The author responds that actuality is indeed a quality added to the relation. A further exchange by al-‘Aṭṭār and ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm debates whether a possible proposition truly contains a judgment, concluding that if the establishment-by-possibility is distinct from the possibility-of-establishment, then it does. Finally, an example ('Every human is breathing') illustrates that conventional language implies actuality, and additional restrictions (permanence, necessity, possibility) modify it. The lesson shows the precision required in modal logic and the subtle distinctions between actuality and possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the absolute proposition counted among the modals (al-muwajjahāt)?
A) Because it contains an explicit modality of necessity
B) Because it has the form of a modal, including a restriction of actuality
C) Because it is always true
D) Because it is the opposite of the possible proposition

Key Takeaway
The absolute proposition is defined by an unrestricted relation but conventionally understood as actual; its inclusion among the modals is metaphorical, due to its form rather than a genuine modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Every prayer is an act of worship', explain why it is considered an absolute proposition. Identify the modality implied by convention and rewrite it as a possible proposition ('Every prayer may be an act of worship'). Then explain how the meaning changes and whether the possible version contains a genuine judgment.
